<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Glossary of nautical terms</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Abeam&amp;redirect=no" title="Abeam">Abeam</a>)</span></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable plainlinks" role="note">This list is <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Lists#Incomplete_lists" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Lists">incomplete</a>; you can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit">expanding it</a>.</div>
<p>This is a partial <b>glossary of nautical terms</b>; some remain current, while many date from the 17th to 19th centuries. See also <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Category:en:Nautical" title="wikt:Category:en:Nautical">Wiktionary's nautical terms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Category:Nautical_terms" title="Category:Nautical terms">Category:Nautical terms</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nautical_metaphors_in_English" title="Nautical metaphors in English">Nautical metaphors in English</a>. See the <a href="#Further_reading">Further reading</a> section for additional words and references.</p>
<div style="text-align:center;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="A">A</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: A">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="a_.26_a.27s" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">A &amp; A's</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Alterations and additions to the structure, rigging and equipment of a warship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-covey_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-covey-1">[1]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="a-back" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">A-back</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A foresail when against the wind, used when tacking to help the vessel turn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="abaft" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Abaft</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Toward the stern, relative to some object ("abaft the fore hatch").</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="abaft_the_beam" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Abaft the beam</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Further aft than the beam: a relative bearing of greater than 90 degrees from the bow: "two points abaft the beam, starboard side". That would describe "an object lying 22.5 degrees toward the rear of the ship, as measured clockwise from a perpendicular line from the right side, center, of the ship, toward the horizon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navalslang_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-navalslang-3">[3]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="abandon_ship.21" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Abandon ship!</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An imperative to leave the vessel immediately, usually in the face of some imminent overwhelming danger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hope_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-hope-4">[4]</a></sup> It is an order issued by the Master or a delegated person in command. (It must be a verbal order). It is usually the last resort after all other mitigating actions have failed or become impossible, and destruction or loss of the ship is imminent; and customarily followed by a command to "man the lifeboats" or life rafts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hope_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-hope-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-layton_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-layton-5">[5]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="abeam" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Abeam</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"><i>On the beam</i>, a relative bearing at right angles to the ship's <a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">keel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="able_seaman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Able_seaman" title="Able seaman">Able seaman</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Also able-bodied seaman. A merchant seaman qualified to perform all routine duties, or a junior rank in some navies.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="aboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Aboard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">On or in a vessel. Synonymous with "on board." (See also <i>close aboard</i>.)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="about" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">About</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">"To go about is to change the course of a ship by tacking. Ready about, or boutship, is the order to prepare for tacking."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naval_encyclopedia_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naval_encyclopedia-7">[7]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="above_board" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Above board</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">On or above the deck, in plain view, not hiding anything. Pirates would hide their crews below decks, thereby creating the false impression that an encounter with another ship was a casual matter of chance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacKenzie_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacKenzie-8">[8]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="above-water_hull" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Above-water hull</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The hull section of a vessel above the waterline, the visible part of a ship. Also, <a href="/wiki/Topsides" title="Topsides">topsides</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="absentee_pennant" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Absentee pennant</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Special pennant flown to indicate absence of commanding officer, admiral, his chief of staff, or officer whose flag is flying (division, squadron, or <a href="/wiki/Flotilla" title="Flotilla">flotilla</a> commander).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="absolute_bearing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Absolute_bearing" title="Absolute bearing">Absolute bearing</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The bearing of an object in relation to north. Either <i>true bearing</i>, using the geographical or <a href="/wiki/True_north" title="True north">true north</a>, or <i>magnetic bearing</i>, using <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_declination" title="Magnetic declination">magnetic north</a>. See also <i>bearing</i> and <i>relative bearing</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="accommodation_ladder" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Accommodation_ladder" title="Accommodation ladder">Accommodation ladder</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A portable flight of steps down a ship's side.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="accommodation_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Accommodation ship (or accommodation hulk)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hulk_(ship)" title="Hulk (ship)">hulk</a> used as housing, generally when there is a lack of quarters available ashore. An operational ship can be used, but more commonly a hulk modified for accommodation is used.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="act_of_pardon" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Act of Pardon or Act of Grace <span id="Act_of_Grace"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A letter from a state or power authorising action by a <a href="/wiki/Privateer" title="Privateer">privateer</a>. See also <a href="/wiki/Letter_of_marque" title="Letter of marque">Letter of marque</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="action_stations" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Action Stations</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>Battle stations</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="admiral" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Admiral" title="Admiral">Admiral</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Senior naval officer of Flag rank. In ascending order of seniority, Rear Admiral, Vice Admiral, Admiral and (until about 2001 when all UK five-star ranks were discontinued) Admiral of the Fleet (Royal Navy). Derivation Arabic, from <i>Amir al-Bahr</i> ("Ruler of the sea").</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="admiralty" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">Admiralty</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A high naval authority in charge of a state's Navy or a major territorial component. In the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> (UK) the <a href="/wiki/Admiralty_Board_(United_Kingdom)" title="Admiralty Board (United Kingdom)">Board of Admiralty</a>, executing the office of the Lord High Admiral, promulgates Naval law in the form of Queen's (or King's) Regulations and Admiralty Instructions.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Admiralty law</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="admiralty_law" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Admiralty_law" title="Admiralty law">Admiralty law</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Body of law that deals with maritime cases. In the UK administered by the Probate, Divorce and Admiralty Division of the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice" title="High Court of Justice">High Court of Justice</a> or supreme court.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="adrift" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Adrift</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Afloat and unattached in any way to the shore or seabed, but not under way. When referring to a vessel, it implies that the vessel is not under control and therefore goes where the wind and current take her (<i>loose from moorings</i> or <i>out of place</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Any gear not fastened down or put away properly.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  Any person or thing that is misplaced or missing. When applied to a member of the navy or marine corps, such a person is "absent without leave" (AWOL) or, in US Navy and US Marine Corps terminology, is guilty of an "unauthorized absence" (UA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-covey_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-covey-1">[1]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="advance_note" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Advance note</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A note for one month's wages issued to sailors on their signing a ship's articles.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="adviso" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Adviso</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>aviso</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="afloat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Afloat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Of a vessel which is floating freely (not aground or sunk). More generally of vessels in service ("the company has 10 ships afloat").</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="afore" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Afore</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  In, on, or toward the front of a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In front of a vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="aft" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Aft" title="Aft">Aft</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The portion of the vessel behind the middle area of the vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Towards the stern (of the vessel).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="afterbrow" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Afterbrow</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  On larger ships, a secondary gangway rigged in the area aft of midship. On some military vessels, such as US Naval vessels, enlisted personnel below E-7 board the ship at the afterbrow; officers and <a href="/wiki/Chief_petty_officer_(United_States)" title="Chief petty officer (United States)">CPO</a>/<a href="/wiki/Senior_chief_petty_officer" title="Senior chief petty officer">SCPO</a>/<a href="/wiki/Master_chief_petty_officer" title="Master chief petty officer">MCPO</a> board the ship at the brow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="afternoon_watch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Afternoon <a href="/wiki/Watch_system" title="Watch system">watch</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The 1200–1600 watch.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="aground" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Aground</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Resting on or touching the ground or bottom (usually involuntarily).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ahead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ahead</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Forward of the bow.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ahoy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ahoy</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A cry to draw attention. Term used to hail a boat or a ship, as "<i>Boat ahoy!</i>"</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ahull" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ahull</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Lying broadside to the sea.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To ride out a storm with no sails and helm held to leeward.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="aid_to_navigation" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Navigational_aid" title="Navigational aid">Aid to Navigation</a> <span id="Navigational_aid"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  (ATON) Any device external to a vessel or aircraft specifically intended to assist navigators in determining their position or safe course, or to warn them of dangers or obstructions to navigation.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  (ATON) Any sort of marker which aids the traveler in navigation; the term is most commonly used to refer to nautical or aviation travel. Common types of such aids include lighthouses, buoys, fog signals, and day beacons.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="aircraft_carrier" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">Aircraft carrier</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A warship designed with a primary mission of deploying and recovering aircraft, acting as a seagoing airbase. Frequently shortened to <i>carrier</i>. Since 1918, the term generally has been limited to a warship with an extensive flight deck designed to operate conventional fixed-wing aircraft. In United States Navy slang, also called a <i>flat top</i> or a <i>bird farm</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="alee" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Alee</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  On the lee side of a ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To leeward.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="all_hands" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">All hands</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Entire ship's company, both officers and enlisted personnel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="all_night_in" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">All night in</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Having no night watches.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="all_standing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">All standing</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Bringing a person or thing up short, that is an unforeseen and sudden stop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-covey_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-covey-1">[1]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="allision" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Allision</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A term used in maritime law – To impact a stationary object (not submerged), such as a bridge abutment or dolphin, pier or wharf, or another vessel made fast to a pier or wharf. More than incidental contact is required. The vessel is said to "allide" with the fixed object and is considered at fault. As opposed to collision.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="aloft" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Aloft</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In the rigging of a sailing ship. Above the ship's uppermost solid structure; overhead or high above.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">1.  In the rigging of a sailing ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Above the ship's uppermost solid structure.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  Overhead or high above.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="alongside" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Alongside</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">By the side of a ship or pier.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="amidships" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Amidships</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The middle section of a vessel with reference to the athwartships plane, as distinguished from port or starboard ("Put your rudder amidships." (Compare <i>Midships</i>.))</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anchor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Anchor" title="Anchor">Anchor</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  an object designed to prevent or slow the drift of a ship, attached to the ship by a line or chain; typically a <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a>, hook-like or plough-like object designed to grip the bottom under the body of water (but see also <i>sea anchor</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  to deploy an anchor ("She anchored offshore.")</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anchor_ball" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Anchor ball</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Round black shape hoisted in the forepart of a vessel to show that it is anchored.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anchor_buoy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Anchor buoy</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small buoy secured by a light line to an anchor to indicate position of anchor on bottom.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anchor_chain" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Anchor chain (or anchor cable) <span id="Anchor_cable"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Chain connecting the ship to the anchor.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anchor_detail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Anchor detail</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Group of men who handle ground tackle when the ship is anchoring or getting under way.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anchor_home" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Anchor home</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When the anchor is secured for sea. Typically rests just outside the hawsepipe on the outer side of the hull, at the bow of a vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anchor_light" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Anchor light</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">White light displayed by a ship at anchor. Two such lights are displayed by a ship over 150 feet (46 m) in length.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anchor_rode" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Anchor rode</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The anchor line, rope or cable connecting the anchor chain to the vessel. Also Rode.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anchor_sentinel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Anchor sentinel</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A separate weight on a separate line which is loosely attached to the anchor rode so that it can slide down it easily. It is made fast at a distance slightly longer than the <a href="/wiki/Draft_(hull)" title="Draft (hull)">draft</a> of the boat. It is used to prevent the anchor rode from becoming fouled on the <a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">keel</a> or other underwater structures when the boat is resting at anchor and moving randomly during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slack_tide" title="Slack tide">slack tide</a>. Also called a kellet.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anchor_watch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Anchor watch</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The crewmen assigned to take care of the ship while anchored or moored, charged with such duties as making sure that the anchor is holding and the vessel is not drifting. Most marine <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">GPS</a> units have an Anchor Watch alarm capability.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anchor_winch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Anchor winch</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Horizontal capstan in the bow used for weighing anchor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anchorage" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Anchorage</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A suitable place for a ship to anchor. Area of a port or harbor.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anchor.27s_aweigh" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Anchor's aweigh</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Said of an anchor when just clear of the bottom.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="andrew" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Andrew</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Traditional lower-deck slang term for the Royal Navy.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="answer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Answer</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The expected response of a vessel to control mechanisms, such as a turn <i>answering</i> to the wheel and rudder. "She won't answer" might be the report from a helmsman when turning the wheel under a pilot's order fails to produce the expected change of direction.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="anti-rolling_tanks" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Anti-rolling tanks</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A pair of fluid-filled, usually water, tanks mounted on opposite sides of a ship below the <a href="/wiki/Waterline" title="Waterline">waterline</a>. Fluid would be pumped between them in an attempt to dampen the amount of roll.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="aport" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Aport</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Over to the port side.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="apparent_wind" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Apparent_wind" title="Apparent wind">Apparent wind</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The combination of the true wind and the headwind caused by the boat's forward motion. For example, it causes a light side wind to appear to come from well ahead of the beam.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="arc_of_visibility" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Arc of visibility</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The portion of the horizon over which a lighted aid to navigation is visible from seaward.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="archboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Archboard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The plank along the stern where the name of the ship is commonly painted.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="armament" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Armament</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship's weapons.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="articles_of_war" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Articles_of_War" title="Articles of War">Articles of War</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Regulations governing the <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> and <a href="/wiki/Navy" title="Navy">naval</a> forces of <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">UK</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">US</a>; read to every <a href="/wiki/Ship%27s_company" title="Ship's company">ship's company</a> on <a href="/wiki/Ship_commissioning" title="Ship commissioning">commissioning</a> and at specified intervals during the commission.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="as_the_crow_flies" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">As the crow flies</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A direct line between two points (which might cross land) which is the way crows travel rather than ships which must go around land. See also <a href="/wiki/Great_circle" title="Great circle">Great circle</a></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="asdic" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ASDIC" title="ASDIC">ASDIC</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Purportedly an acronym. A type of <a href="/wiki/Sonar" title="Sonar">sonar</a> used by the Allies for detecting submarines during the First and Second World War. abbreviation: Allied Submarine Devices Investigation Committee (World War I). The term has been generically applied to equipment for "under-water supersonic echo-ranging equipment" of submarines and other vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbot_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abbot-10">[10]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ashore" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ashore</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  On the beach, shore, or land (as opposed to <i>aboard</i> or <i>on board</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Towards the shore.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  "To <i>run ashore</i>": To collide with the shore (as opposed to "to <i>run aground</i>," which is to strike a submerged feature such as a reef or sandbar)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="astarboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Astarboard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Over to the starboard side.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="astern" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Astern</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Toward the stern (rear) of a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Behind a vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="astern_gear" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Astern gear</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The gear or gears which, when engaged with an engine or motor, result in backwards movement or force. Equivalent to reverse in a manual-transmission automobile.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="asylum_harbour" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Asylum harbour</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A harbour used to provide shelter from a storm. See "Harbor of refuge."</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="asw" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">ASW</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Anti-submarine warfare.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="athwart.2C_athwartships" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Athwart, athwartships</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">At right angles to the fore and aft or centerline of a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="auxiliary_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_ship" title="Auxiliary ship">Auxiliary ship</a> (or auxiliary) <span id="auxiliary"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A naval ship designed to operate in any number of roles supporting combatant ships and other naval operations, including a wide range of activities related to replenishment, transport, repair, harbor services, and research.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="avast" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Avast</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Stop, cease or desist from whatever is being done. From the Dutch hou' vast ("hold on"), the imperative form of vasthouden ("to hold on to") or the Italian word "Basta"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-covey_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-covey-1">[1]</a></sup> Compare <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ya_Basta" title="Ya Basta">¡Ya basta!</a></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="aviso" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Aviso (formerly also an <i>adviso</i>) <span id="Adviso"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A kind of <a href="/wiki/Dispatch_boat" title="Dispatch boat">dispatch boat</a> or advice boat, survives particularly in the French navy, they are considered equivalent to the modern sloop.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="awash" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Awash</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">So low in the water that the water is constantly washing across the surface.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="aweigh" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Aweigh</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Position of an anchor just clear of the bottom.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="axial_fire" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Axial fire</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Fire oriented towards the ends of the ship; the opposite of broadside fire. In the age of sail this was known as 'raking' fire.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="aye.2C_aye" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Aye, aye <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span></span> <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span></span>/</a></span></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Reply to an order or command to indicate that it, firstly, is heard; and, secondly, is understood and will be carried out. ("Aye, aye, sir" to officers). Also the proper reply from a hailed boat, to indicate that an officer is on board.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="azimuth_circle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Azimuth circle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Instrument used to take bearings of celestial objects.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="azimuth_compass" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Azimuth_compass" title="Azimuth compass">Azimuth compass</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An instrument employed for ascertaining position of the sun with respect to magnetic north. The azimuth of an object is its bearing from the observer measured as an angle clockwise from true north.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="B">B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="b_.26_r_rig" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">B &amp; R rig</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A style of standing rigging used on sailboats that lacks a backstay. The mast is said to be supported like a "tripod," with swept-back spreaders and a forestay. Used widely on Hunter brand sailboats, among others. Designed and named by Lars Bergstrom and Sven Ridder and protected by US Patent number 3866558, dated February 18, 1975.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="back_and_fill" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Back and fill</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To use the advantage of the tide being with you when the wind is not.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="backstays" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Backstay" title="Backstay">Backstays</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Long lines or cables, reaching from the stern of the vessel to the mast heads, used to support the mast.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="baggywrinkle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Baggywrinkle" title="Baggywrinkle">Baggywrinkle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A soft covering for cables (or any other obstructions) that prevents sail chafing.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bailer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Hand_bailer" title="Hand bailer">Bailer</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A device for removing water that has entered the boat.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ballast" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ballast" title="Ballast">Ballast</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Heavy material that is placed in the hold of a vessel to provide stability. (<i>See also <b>in ballast</b>.</i>)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ballast_tank" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ballast_tank" title="Ballast tank">Ballast tank</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A device used on ships and submarines and other submersibles to control buoyancy and stability.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="balls_to_four_watch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Balls to four <a href="/wiki/Watch_system" title="Watch system">watch</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The 0000–0400 watch. (US Navy)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bank" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bank_(topography)" title="Bank (topography)">Bank</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large area of elevated sea floor.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="banyan" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Banyan" title="Banyan">Banyan</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Traditional Royal Navy term for a day or shorter period of rest and relaxation.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bar" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Shoal" title="Shoal">Bar</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Large mass of sand or earth, formed by the surge of the sea. They are mostly found at the entrances of great rivers or havens, and often render navigation extremely dangerous, but confer tranquility once inside. See also: <i>touch and go</i>, <i>grounding</i>. Alfred Lord Tennyson's poem "Crossing the bar" is an allegory for death.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bar_pilot" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Maritime_pilot" title="Maritime pilot">Bar pilot</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A bar pilot guides ships over the dangerous sandbars at the mouth of rivers and bays.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="barber_hauler" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Running_rigging#Shaping" title="Running rigging">Barber hauler</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A technique of temporarily rigging sailboat lazy sheet allowing the boat to sail closer to the wind. i.e. Using the lazy jib sheet to pull the jib closer to the mid line, allowing a point of sail that would otherwise not be achievable.</dd>
<dd><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="barbette" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Barbette" title="Barbette">Barbette</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  During the second half of the 19th century, a fixed armored enclosure protecting a ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s guns aboard warships without gun turrets, generally taking the form of a ring of armor over which guns mounted on an open-topped rotating turntable could fire.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Since the late 19th century, the inside fixed trunk of a warship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s turreted gun-mounting, on which the turret revolves, containing the hoists for shells and cordite from the shell-room and magazine.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="barca-longa" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Barca-longa" title="Barca-longa">Barca-longa</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A two- or three-masted <a href="/wiki/Lugger" title="Lugger">lugger</a> used for fishing on the coasts of Spain and Portugal and more widely in the Mediterranean Sea in the late 17th century and 18th century. The British <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> also used them for shore raids and as <a href="/wiki/Dispatch_boat" title="Dispatch boat">dispatch boats</a> in the Mediterranean.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bareboat_charter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bareboat_charter" title="Bareboat charter">Bareboat charter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An arrangement for the chartering or hiring of a vessel, whereby the vessel<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s owner provides no crew or provisions as part of the agreement; instead, the people who rent the vessel are responsible for crewing and provisioning her.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="barge" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Barge" title="Barge">Barge</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A towed or self-propelled flat-bottomed boat, built mainly for river, canal, and coastal transport of heavy goods.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  <i>Admiral<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s barge</i>: A boat at the disposal of an <a href="/wiki/Admiral" title="Admiral">admiral</a> for his or her use as transportation between a larger vessel and the shore or within a harbor.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bark" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bark</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Alternative spelling of <i>barque</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="barkentine" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Barkentine</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Alternative spelling of <i>barquentine</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="barque" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Barque" title="Barque">Barque</a> (also <i>bark</i>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sailing vessel of three or more masts, with all masts but the sternmost square-rigged, the sternmost being fore-and-aft-rigged.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="barquentine" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Barquentine" title="Barquentine">Barquentine</a> (also <i>barkentine</i>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sailing vessel with three or more masts; with a square-rigged foremast and all other masts fore-and-aft rigged.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="barrack_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Barrack ship</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship or craft designed to function as a floating <a href="/wiki/Barracks" title="Barracks">barracks</a> for housing military personnel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="barrelman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Barrelman" title="Barrelman">Barrelman</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sailor that was stationed in the crow's nest.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="batten" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Batten" title="Batten">Batten</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A stiff strip used to support the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_of_a_sail#The_roach" title="Parts of a sail">roach</a> of a sail, enabling increased sail area.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Any thin strip of material (wood, plastic etc) which can be used any number of ways</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="batten_down_the_hatches" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Batten down the hatches</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To prepare for inclement weather by securing the closed hatch covers with wooden battens so as to prevent water from entering from any angle.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="battle_stations" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/General_quarters" title="General quarters">Battle Stations</a> (also: <i>general quarters</i>, <i>action stations</i>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  An announcement made aboard a naval warship to signal the crew to prepare for battle, imminent damage, or a damage emergency (such as a fire).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Specific positions in a naval warship to which one or more crew are assigned when battle stations is called.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="battlecruiser" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Battlecruiser" title="Battlecruiser">Battlecruiser</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of large <a href="/wiki/Capital_ship" title="Capital ship">capital ship</a> of the first half of the 20th century, similar in size, appearance, and cost to a battleship and typically armed with the same kind of heavy guns, but much more lightly armored (on the scale of <a href="/wiki/Cruiser" title="Cruiser">cruiser</a>) and therefore faster than a battleship but more vulnerable to damage.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="battleship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Battleship" title="Battleship">Battleship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of large, heavily armored warship of the second half of the 19th century and first half of the 20th century armed with heavy-caliber guns, designed to fight other battleships in a <a href="/wiki/Line_of_battle" title="Line of battle">line of battle</a>. It was the successor to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ship-of-the-line" title="Ship-of-the-line">ship-of-the-line</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="beaching" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Beaching_(nautical)" title="Beaching (nautical)">Beaching</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Deliberately running a vessel <i>aground</i> to load and unload (as with <a href="/wiki/Landing_craft" title="Landing craft">landing craft</a>), or sometimes to prevent a damaged vessel sinking.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="beacon" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Beacon" title="Beacon">Beacon</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A lighted or unlighted fixed aid to navigation attached directly to the earth's surface. (Lights and daybeacons both constitute beacons.)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="beakhead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Beakhead" title="Beakhead">Beakhead</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The ram on the prow of a fighting <a href="/wiki/Galley" title="Galley">galley</a> of ancient and medieval times.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The protruding part of the foremost section of a sailing ship of the 16th to the 18th century, usually ornate, used as a working platform by sailors handling the sails of the <a href="/wiki/Bowsprit" title="Bowsprit">bowsprit</a>. It also housed the crew<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s heads (toilets).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="beam" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Beam_(nautical)" title="Beam (nautical)">Beam</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The width of a vessel at the widest point, or a point alongside the ship at the midpoint of its length.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="beam_ends" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Beam ends</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The sides of a ship. "On her beam ends" may mean the vessel is literally on her side and possibly about to capsize; more often, the phrase means the vessel is listing 45 degrees or more.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="beam_reach" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Beam reach</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Sailing with the wind coming across the vessel's beam. This is normally the fastest point of sail for a fore-and-aft rigged vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="beam_sea" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Beam sea</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sea where waves are moving perpendicular to the direction a ship is moving</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="beam_wind" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Beam wind</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A wind at right angles to the vessel's course.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bear" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bear</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Large squared off stone used with sand for scraping clean wooden decks.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bear_away" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bear away</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To steer (a vessel) away from the wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bear_down" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bear down or bear away</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Turn away from the wind, often with reference to a transit.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bear_up" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bear up</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Turn into the wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bearing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bearing_(navigation)" title="Bearing (navigation)">Bearing</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The horizontal direction of a line of sight between two objects on the surface of the earth. See also <i>absolute bearing</i> and <i>relative bearing</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="beat_to_quarters" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beat_to_quarters" title="Beat to quarters">Beat to quarters</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Prepare for battle (beat = beat the drum to signal the need for battle preparation)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="beating" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Points_of_sail" title="Points of sail">Beating or Beat to</a> <span id="Beat_to"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Sailing as close as possible towards the wind (perhaps only about 60°) in a zig-zag course to attain an upwind direction to which it is impossible to sail directly.(also <i>tacking</i>)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="beaufort_scale" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Beaufort_scale" title="Beaufort scale">Beaufort scale</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The scale describing wind force devised by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Beaufort" title="Francis Beaufort">Admiral Sir Francis Beaufort</a> in 1808, in which winds are graded by the effect of their force (originally, the amount of sail that a fully rigged frigate could carry).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="becalm" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Becalm</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To cut off the wind from a sailing vessel, either by the proximity of land or by another vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="becalmed" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Becalmed</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Unable to move due to lack of wind; said of a sailing vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="before_the_mast" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Before the mast</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Literally, the area of a ship before the foremast (the forecastle). Most often used to refer to men whose living quarters are located here, officers being quartered in the stern-most areas of the ship (near the quarterdeck). Officer-trainees lived between the two ends of the ship and become known as "midshipmen". Crew members who started out as seamen, then became midshipmen, and later, officers, were said to have gone from "one end of the ship to the other". (See also <i>hawsepiper</i>.)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="belay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Belay</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  To make fast a line around a fitting, usually a cleat or belaying pin.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To secure a climbing person in a similar manner.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  An order to halt a current activity or countermand an order prior to execution.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="belaying_pins" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Belaying_pin" title="Belaying pin">Belaying pins</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Short movable bars of iron or hard wood to which running rigging may be secured, or <i>belayed</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bell" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bell</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of buoy with a large bell and hanging hammers that sound by wave action.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> (See also <i>ship's bell</i>.)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="below" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Below</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">On or into a lower deck, e.g., <i>The captain has gone below</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="below_decks" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Below decks</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In or into any of the spaces below the main deck of a vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="belt_armor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Belt_armor" title="Belt armor">Belt armor</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A layer of heavy metal armor plated onto or within the outer hulls of warships, typically on battleships, battlecruisers, cruisers, and aircraft carriers, usually covering the warship from her main deck down to some distance below the <a href="/wiki/Waterline" title="Waterline">waterline</a>. If built within the hull, rather than forming the outer hull, the belt would be installed at an inclined angle to improve the warship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s protection from shells striking the hull.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bend" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/List_of_bend_knots" title="List of bend knots">Bend</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A knot used to join two ropes or lines. See also <i>hitch</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To attach a rope to an object<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  Fastening a sail to a yard<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging18482_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging18482-11">[11]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bermuda_rig" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bermuda_rig" title="Bermuda rig">Bermuda rig</a> or Bermudan rig</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A triangular mainsail, without any upper spar, which is hoisted up the mast by a single halyard attached to the head of the sail. This configuration, introduced to Europe about 1920, allows the use of a tall mast, enabling sails to be set higher where wind speed is greater.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bermuda_sloop" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bermuda_sloop" title="Bermuda sloop">Bermuda sloop</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A fore-and-aft rigged sailing vessel with <a href="/wiki/Bermuda_rig" title="Bermuda rig">Bermuda rig</a> developed in <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a> in the 17th century. In its purest form, it is single-masted, although Bermuda sloops can have up to three masts, three-masted ships being referred to as <i>schooners</i>. Originally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaff_rigged" title="Gaff rigged">gaff rigged</a>, but evolved to use <a href="/wiki/Bermuda_rig" title="Bermuda rig">Bermuda rig</a>. The Bermuda sloop is the basis of nearly all modern sailing <a href="/wiki/Yacht" title="Yacht">yachts</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="berth_.28moorings.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Berth_(moorings)" title="Berth (moorings)">Berth (moorings)</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A location in a port or harbour used specifically for mooring vessels while not at sea.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="berth_.28navigation.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Berth (navigation)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Safety margin of distance to be kept by a vessel from another vessel or from an obstruction, hence the phrase, "to give a wide berth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bathe_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bathe-12">[12]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="berth_.28sleeping.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Berth_(sleeping)" title="Berth (sleeping)">Berth (sleeping)</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A bed or sleeping accommodation on a boat or ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="best_bower" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Best bower (<a href="/wiki/Anchor" title="Anchor">anchor</a>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The larger of two anchors carried in the bow; so named as it was the last, <i>best</i> hope.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="between_the_devil_and_the_deep_blue_sea" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Between the devil and the deep blue sea</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>devil seam</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="between_wind_and_water" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Between wind and water</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The part of a ship's hull that is sometimes submerged and sometimes brought above water by the rolling of the vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bight" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bight <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  <a href="/wiki/Bight_(knot)" title="Bight (knot)">Bight</a>, a loop in rope or line – a hitch or knot tied <i>on the bight</i> is one tied in the middle of a rope, without access to the ends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  An indentation in a coastline.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bilander" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bilander" title="Bilander">Bilander</a> (also billander or be<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>landre) <span id="Billander"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">a small European merchant sailing ship with two masts, the mainmast lateen-rigged with a trapezoidal mainsail, and the foremast carrying the conventional square course and square topsail. Used in the Netherlands for coast and canal traffic and occasionally in the North Sea, but more frequently used in the Mediterranean Sea.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bilge" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bilge" title="Bilge">Bilge</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The compartment at the bottom of the hull of a ship or boat where water collects and must be pumped out of the vessel. The space between the botton hull planking and the ceiling of the hold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To spring a leak in the bilge.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  To break open a vessel′s bilge.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bilge_keels" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bilge_keel" title="Bilge keel">Bilge keels</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A pair of keels on either side of the hull, usually slanted outwards. In <a href="/wiki/Yacht" title="Yacht">yachts</a>, they allow the use of a drying mooring, the boat standing upright on the keels (and often a <i>skeg</i>) when the tide is out.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bilged_on_her_anchor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bilged on her anchor</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship that has run upon her own anchor, so the anchor cable runs under the hull.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bill" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bill</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The extremity of the arm of an anchor; the point of or beyond the fluke.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bimini_top" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bimini_top" title="Bimini top">Bimini top</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Open-front canvas top for the cockpit of a boat, usually supported by a metal frame.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bimmy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Flagellation" title="Flagellation">Bimmy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A punitive instrument</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="binnacle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Binnacle" title="Binnacle">Binnacle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The stand on which the ship's compass is mounted.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="binnacle_list" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Binnacle list</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship's sick list. The list of men unable to report for duty was given to the officer or mate of the watch by the ship's surgeon. The list was kept at the binnacle.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bird_farm" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bird farm</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">United States Navy slang for an <i>aircraft carrier</i> (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bite" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bite</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Verb used in reference to a rudder, as in "the rudder begins to bite." When a vessel has steerageway the rudder will act to steer the vessel, i.e. it has enough water flow past it to steer with. Physically this is noticeable with tiller or unassisted wheel steering by the rudder exhibiting resistance to being turned from the straight ahead – this resistance is the rudder "biting" and is how a helmsman first senses that the vessel has acquired steerageway.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bitt" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bitt or <a href="/wiki/Bitts" title="Bitts">bitts</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A post or pair mounted on the ship's bow, for fastening ropes or cables.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Strong vertical timbers or irons fastened through the deck beams used for securing ropes or hawsers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bitt_heads" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bit heads</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The tops of two massive timbers that support the windlass on a sailing barge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bitter_end" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Knot#Bitter_end" title="Knot">Bitter end</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The last part or loose end of a rope or cable. The anchor cable is tied to the bitts; when the cable is fully paid out, the bitter end has been reached.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="black_gang" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Black_gang_(ship)" title="Black gang (ship)">Black gang</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The engineering crew of the vessel, i.e., crew members who work in the vessel<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s engine room, fire room, and boiler room, so called because they would be covered in coal dust during the days of coal-fired steamships.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="block" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Block_(sailing)" title="Block (sailing)">Block</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A pulley with one or more sheaves (grooves), over which a rope is roved. It can be used to chage the direction of the rope, or in pairs used to form a tackle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-11"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="blockship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Blockship" title="Blockship">Blockship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel sunk deliberately to block a waterway to prevent the waterway′s use by an enemy.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="blue_peter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/International_maritime_signal_flags" title="International maritime signal flags">Blue Peter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A blue and white flag (the flag for the letter "P") hoisted at the foretrucks of ships about to sail. Formerly a white ship on a blue ground, but later a white square on a blue ground.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="board" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Board</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  To step onto, climb onto, or otherwise enter a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The side of a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  The distance a sailing vessel runs between <i>tacks</i> (q.v.) when working to <i>windward</i> (q.v.).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Boat" title="Boat">Boat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A small craft or vessel designed to float on, and provide transport over, or under, water.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Naval slang for a <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarine</a> of any size.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boat-hook" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Boat-hook</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A pole with a hook on the end, used to reach into the water to catch buoys or other floating objects.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boatswain" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Boatswain" title="Boatswain">Boatswain</a> or bosun (both <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">ən</span></span>/</a></span></span>) <span id="Bosun"></span><span id="bosun"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A non-commissioned officer responsible for the sails, ropes, rigging and boats on a ship who issues "piped" commands to seamen.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boatswain.27s_call" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bosun%27s_call" title="Bosun's call">Boatswain's call</a>, also bosun's call, boatswain's pipe, bosun's pipe, boatswain's whistle, or bosun's whistle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A high-pitched pipe or a non-diaphragm-type whistle used on naval ships by a <a href="/wiki/Boatswain" title="Boatswain">boatswain</a>, historically to pass commands to the crew but in modern times limited to ceremonial use.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boatswain.27s_chair" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bosun%27s_chair" title="Bosun's chair">Boatswain's chair</a> or bosun's chair</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A short board or swatch of heavy canvas, secured in a bridle of ropes, used to hoist a man aloft or over the ship's side for painting and similar work. Modern boatswain's chairs incorporate safety harnesses to prevent the occupant from falling.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boatswain.27s_pipe" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Boatswain's pipe</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>boatswain's call</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boatswain.27s_whistle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Boatswain's whistle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>boatswain's call</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boatwright" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Boatwright</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A maker of boats, especially of traditional wooden construction.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bob" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bob or bobfly</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A pennant or flag bearing the owner's colours, mounted on the Topsail trunk <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-12"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bobstay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bobstay" title="Bobstay">Bobstay</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A stay which holds the bowsprit downwards, counteracting the effect of the forestay and the lift of sails. Usually made of wire or chain to eliminate stretch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-13"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="body_plan" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Body plan</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a>, an end <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elevation_(view)" title="Elevation (view)">elevation</a> showing the contour of the sides of a ship at certain points of her length.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boiler_room" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Boiler room</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>fire room</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bolt_rope" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bolt_rope" title="Bolt rope">Bolt rope</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A rope, sewn on to reinforce the edges of a sail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-14"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bollard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bollard" title="Bollard">Bollard</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">From "bol" or "bole", the round trunk of a tree. A substantial vertical pillar to which lines may be made fast. Generally on the quayside rather than the ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bombay_runner" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bombay runner</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Large cockroach.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bonded_jacky" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bonded jacky</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of tobacco or sweet cake.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bonnet" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bonnet</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A strip of canvas secured to the foot of the course (square sail) to increase sail area in light airs.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="booby" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Booby" title="Booby">Booby</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of bird that has little fear and therefore is particularly easy to catch.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="booby_hatch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Booby hatch</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sliding hatch or cover.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boom_.28navigational_barrier.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Boom_(navigational_barrier)" title="Boom (navigational barrier)">Boom (navigational barrier)</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A floating barrier to control navigation into and out of rivers and harbours.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boom" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Boom_(sailing)" title="Boom (sailing)">Boom (sailing)</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A spar attached to the foot of a fore-and-aft sail.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boomer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Boomer</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Slang term in the US Navy for a <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile_submarine" title="Ballistic missile submarine">ballistic missile submarine</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boom_gallows" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Boom gallows</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A raised crossmember that supports a boom when the sail is lowered (obviates the need for a <a href="/wiki/Topping_lift" title="Topping lift">topping lift</a>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boomie" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Boomie or Booms'l rig</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ketch rigged barge with gaff (instead of spritsail) and boom on main and mizzen. Booms'l rig could also refer to cutter rigged early barges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-15"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boom_vang" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Boom_vang" title="Boom vang">Boom vang</a> or vang</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Sail" title="Sail">sail</a> control that lets one apply downward tension on a boom, countering the upward tension provided by the sail. The boom vang adds an element of control to sail shape when the sheet is let out enough that it no longer pulls the boom down. Boom vang tension helps control leech twist, a primary component of sail power.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boomkin" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Boomkin" title="Boomkin">Boomkin</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>bumpkin</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="booms" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Booms</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Masts or yards, lying on board in reserve.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bore" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bore, as in Bore up or Bore away</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To assume a position to engage, or disengage, the enemy ship(s)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bosun" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bosun</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>boatswain</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bosun.27s_call" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bosun's call</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>boatswain's call</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bosun.27s_chair" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bosun's chair</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>boatswain's chair</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bosun.27s_pipe" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bosun's pipe</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>boatswain's call</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bosun.27s_whistle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bosun's whistle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>boatswain's call</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bottlescrew" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Turnbuckle" title="Turnbuckle">Bottlescrew</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A device for adjusting tension in stays, shrouds and similar lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-16"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bottom" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bottom</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The underside of a vessel; the portion of a vessel that is always underwater.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A ship, most often a <a href="/wiki/Cargo_ship" title="Cargo ship">cargo ship</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  A cargo hold.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bottomry" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bottomry" title="Bottomry">Bottomry</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Pledging a ship as security in a financial transaction.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bow" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bow_(ship)" title="Bow (ship)">Bow</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The front of a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Either side of the front (or bow) of the vessel, i.e., the <i>port bow</i> and <i>starboard bow</i>. Something ahead and to the left of the vessel is "off the port bow", while something ahead and to the right of the vessel is "off the starboard bow." When "bow" is used in this way, the front of the vessel sometimes is called her <i>bows</i> (plural), a collective reference to her port and starboard bows synonymous with <i>bow</i> (singular) as described in Definition (1).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bow_chaser" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bow chaser</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>chase gun</i></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bowline" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bowline" title="Bowline">Bowline</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A type of knot, producing a strong loop of a fixed size, topologically similar to a sheet bend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-17"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">Also a rope attached to the side of a sail to pull it towards the bow (for keeping the windward edge of the sail steady).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-18"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup>.  {{{content}}}</dd>
<dd class="glossary">Also a rope attached to the foresail to hold it aback when tacking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-19"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup>.  {{{content}}}</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bowse" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bowse</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To pull or hoist.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bowsprit" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bowsprit" title="Bowsprit">Bowsprit</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A spar projecting from the bow used as an anchor for the forestay and other rigging. On a barge it may be pivoted so it may be steeved up in harbour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-20"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bow_thruster" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_thruster" title="Bow thruster">Bow thruster</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small propeller or water-jet at the bow, used for manoeuvring larger vessels at slow speed. May be mounted externally, or in a tunnel running through the bow from side to side.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boxing_the_compass" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boxing_the_compass" title="Boxing the compass">Boxing the compass</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To state all 32 points of the compass, starting at north, proceeding clockwise. Sometimes applied to a wind that is constantly shifting.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="boy_seaman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boy_Seaman" title="Boy Seaman">Boy Seaman</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">a young sailor, still in training</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="brace_abox" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Brace abox</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To bring the foreyards flat aback to stop the ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="brail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Brail" title="Brail">Brail</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To furl or truss a sail by pulling it in towards the mast, or the ropes used to do so. To brail up – to stow the sails.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-21"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="brake" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Brake</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The handle of the pump, by which it is worked.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="brass_monkey" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brass_monkey_(colloquial_expression)" title="Brass monkey (colloquial expression)">Brass monkey</a> or brass monkey weather</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Used in the expression "it is cold enough to freeze the balls off a brass monkey."</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="brass_pounder" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Brass pounder</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Early 20th-century slang term for a vessel<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s radio operator, so called because he repeatedly struck a brass key on his transmitter to broadcast in <a href="/wiki/Morse_code" title="Morse code">Morse code</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="break_bulk_cargo" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Break_bulk_cargo" title="Break bulk cargo">Break bulk cargo</a> (or breakbulk cargo)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Goods that must be loaded aboard a ship individually, and not in intermodal containers or in bulk, carried by a <i>general cargo ship</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="breakwater" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Breakwater</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A structure constructed on a coast as part of a coastal defense system or to protect an anchorage from the effects of weather and longshore drift.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A structure built on the forecastle of a ship intended to divert water away from the forward superstructure or gun mounts.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="breeches_buoy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Breeches_buoy" title="Breeches buoy">Breeches buoy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ring <a href="/wiki/Lifebuoy" title="Lifebuoy">lifebuoy</a> fitted with canvas breeches, functionally similar to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zip_line" title="Zip line">zip line</a>, used to transfer people from one ship to another or to rescue people from a wrecked or sinking ship by moving them to another ship or to the shore.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bridge" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bridge_(nautical)" title="Bridge (nautical)">Bridge</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A structure above the weather deck, extending the full width of the vessel, which houses a command centre, itself called by association, the bridge.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bridge_wing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bridge wing</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An open-air extension of the bridge to port or starboard, intended for use in signaling.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="brig" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Brig" title="Brig">Brig</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  (historically) A vessel with two square-rigged masts.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  (in the US) An interior area of the ship used to detain prisoners (possibly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prisoners-of-war" title="Prisoners-of-war">prisoners-of-war</a>, in war-time) and stowaways, and to punish delinquent crew members. Usually resembles a prison cell with bars and a locked, hinged door.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="brig_sloop" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brig_sloop" title="Brig sloop">Brig sloop</a></dfn></dt>
