<dd class="glossary">A type of <a href="/wiki/Sloop-of-war" title="Sloop-of-war">sloop-of-war</a> introduced in the 1770s which had two square-rigged masts like a <a href="/wiki/Brig" title="Brig">brig</a> (in contrast to <i>ship sloops</i> of the time, which had three masts).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="brigantine" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Brigantine" title="Brigantine">Brigantine</a> (also <i>hermaphrodite brig</i>) <span id="hermaphrodite_brig"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A two-masted vessel, square-rigged on the foremast, but fore-and-aft-rigged on the mainmast.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="brightwork" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Brightwork" title="Brightwork">Brightwork</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Exposed varnished wood or polished metal on a boat.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bring_to" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bring to</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Cause a ship to be stationary by arranging the sails.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="broach" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Broach_(sailing)" title="Broach (sailing)">Broach</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When a sailing vessel loses control of its motion and is forced into a sudden sharp turn, often heeling heavily and in smaller vessels sometimes leading to a capsize. The change in direction is called <i>broaching-to</i>. Occurs when too much sail is set for a strong gust of wind, or in circumstances where the sails are unstable.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="broad" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Broad</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Wide (<i>broad</i>) in appearance from the vantage point of a lookout or other person viewing activity in the vicinity of a ship, e.g., another ship off the starboard bow with her side facing the viewer's ship could be described as "<i>broad</i> on the starboard bow" of the viewer's ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="broadside" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Broadside</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  One side of a vessel above the <a href="/wiki/Waterline" title="Waterline">waterline</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  All the guns on one side of a warship or mounted (in rotating turrets or barbettes) so as to be able fire on the same side of a warship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  The simultaneous firing of all the guns on one side of a warship or able to fire on the same side of a warship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  <i>Weight of broadside</i>, the combined weight of all projectiles a ship can fire in a broadside, or the combined weight of all the shells a group of ships that have formed a line of battle collectively can fire on the same side.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="brow" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Brow</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>gangplank</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="buffer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Buffer</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The chief bosun's mate (in the Royal Navy), responsible for discipline.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bulbous_bow" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bulbous_bow" title="Bulbous bow">Bulbous bow</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A protruding bulb at the bow of a ship just below the waterline which modifies the way water flows around the hull, reducing drag and thus increasing speed, range, fuel efficiency, and stability.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bulk_cargo" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bulk_cargo" title="Bulk cargo">Bulk cargo</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Commodity cargo that is transported unpackaged in large quantities.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bulk_carrier" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bulk_carrier" title="Bulk carrier">Bulk carrier</a> (also <i>bulk freighter</i> or <i>bulker</i>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A merchant ship specially designed to transport unpackaged bulk cargo in its cargo holds.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bulkhead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bulkhead_(partition)" title="Bulkhead (partition)">Bulkhead</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An upright wall within the hull of a ship, particularly a watertight, load-bearing wall.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bulwark" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Special:Search/bulwark" title="wikt:Special:Search/bulwark">Bulwark or Bulward</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ʊ/: 'oo' in 'foot'">ʊ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span> in nautical use) <span id="bulward"></span><span id="Bulward"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bulwark2_(PSF).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="317" data-file-width="430" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Bulwark2_%28PSF%29.png/220px-Bulwark2_%28PSF%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Bulwark2_%28PSF%29.png/330px-Bulwark2_%28PSF%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/df/Bulwark2_%28PSF%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bulwark2_(PSF).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bulwark (or Bulward)</div>
</div>
</div>
The extension of the ship's side above the level of the weather deck.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bull_ensign" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bull ensign (also "boot ensign" or "George ensign")</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The senior ensign (<i>q.v.</i>) of a US Navy command (i.e., a ship, squadron, or shore activity). The bull ensign assumes additional responsibilities beyond those of other ensigns, such as teaching less-experienced ensigns about life at sea, planning and coordinating wardroom social activities, making sure that the officers' mess runs smoothly, and serving as an officer for Navy-related social organizations. The bull ensign also serves as the focal point for the unit's expression of spirit and pride.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bumboat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bumboat" title="Bumboat">Bumboat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A private boat selling goods.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bumpkin" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bumpkin or <a href="/wiki/Boomkin" title="Boomkin">boomkin</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A spar, similar to a bowsprit, but which projects from the stern. May be used to attach the backstay or mizzen sheets.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  An iron bar (projecting out-board from a ship's side) to which the lower and topsail brace blocks are sometimes hooked.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bunker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bunker</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A container for storing coal or fuel oil for a ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s engine.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bunker_fuel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Bunker fuel or bunkers <span id="Bunkers"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Fuel oil for a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="bunting_tosser" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bunting_tosser" title="Bunting tosser">Bunting tosser</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A signalman who prepares and flies flag hoists. Also known in the American Navy as a skivvy waver.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="buntline" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Clewlines_and_buntlines" title="Clewlines and buntlines">Buntline</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">One of the lines tied to the bottom of a square sail and used to haul it up to the yard when furling.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="buoy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Buoy" title="Buoy">Buoy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A floating object of defined shape and color, which is anchored at a given position and serves as an aid to navigation.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="buoyed_up" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Buoyed up</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Lifted by a buoy, especially a cable that has been lifted to prevent it from trailing on the bottom.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="burden" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Burden (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English_language" title="Early Modern English language">Early Modern English</a>: <span lang="en" xml:lang="en"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burthen" title="Burthen">Burthen</a></span>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_English_language" title="Middle English language">Middle English</a>: <span lang="enm" xml:lang="enm"><i>Byrthen</i></span>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The <a href="/wiki/Builder%27s_Old_Measurement" title="Builder's Old Measurement">Builder's Old Measurement</a>, expressed in "tons bm" or "tons BOM", a <i>volumetric</i> measurement of cubic cargo capacity, <i>not</i> of weight. This is the <a href="/wiki/Tonnage" title="Tonnage">tonnage</a> of a ship, based on the number of <a href="/wiki/Tun_(unit)" title="Tun (unit)">tuns</a> of wine that it could carry in its holds. One 252-gallon tun of wine takes up approximately 100 cubic feet – and, incidentally, weighs 2,240 lbs (1 <a href="/wiki/Long_ton" title="Long ton">long ton</a>, or Imperial ton).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="burgee" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Burgee" title="Burgee">Burgee</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small flag, typically triangular, flown from the masthead of a yacht to indicate yacht-club membership.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="by_and_large" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">By and large</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"><i>By</i> means into the wind, while <i>large</i> means with the wind. "By and large" is used to indicate all possible situations "<i>the ship handles well both by and large</i>".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="by_the_board" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">By the board</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Anything that has gone overboard.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="C">C</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: C">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="cabin" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cabin_(ship)" title="Cabin (ship)">Cabin</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">an enclosed room on a <a href="/wiki/Deck_(ship)" title="Deck (ship)">deck</a> or flat.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cabin_boy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cabin_boy" title="Cabin boy">Cabin boy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">attendant on passengers and crew. often a young man</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cable" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Wire_rope" title="Wire rope">Cable</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A large rope.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A <i>cable length</i> (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cable_length" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cable_length" title="Cable length">Cable length</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A measure of length or distance. Equivalent to (UK) 1/10 nautical mile, approx. 600 feet; (US) 120 fathoms, 720 feet (219 m); other countries use different values. Sometimes called simply a <i>cable</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="caboose" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Caboose_(ship%27s_galley)" title="Caboose (ship's galley)">Caboose</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">a small ship's kitchen, or galley on deck.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cabotage" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cabotage</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The transport of goods or passengers between two points in the same country, alongside coastal waters, by a vessel or an aircraft registered in another country.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="camels" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Camels</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Loaded vessels lashed tightly, one on each side of another vessel, and then emptied to provide additional buoyancy that reduces the draught of the ship in the middle.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="can" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Can</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A type of navigational buoy often a vertical drum, but if not, always square in silhouette, colored red in <a href="/wiki/Lateral_mark#Region_A" title="Lateral mark">IALA region A</a> or green in <a href="/wiki/Lateral_mark#Region_B" title="Lateral mark">IALA region B</a> (the Americas, Japan, Korea and the Philippines). In channel marking its use is opposite that of a "nun buoy".</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A shortened version of <i>tin can</i> (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="canal_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Canal boat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A specialized watercraft designed for operation on a canal.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="canister" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Canister</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">a type of antipersonnel cannon load in which lead balls or other loose metallic items were enclosed in a tin or iron shell. On firing, the shell would disintegrate, releasing the smaller metal objects with a shotgun-like effect.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="canoe_stern" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Canoe stern</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A design for the stern of a yacht which is pointed, like a bow, rather than squared off as a transom.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cape_horn_fever" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cape Horn fever</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The name of the fake illness a <a href="/wiki/Malingering" title="Malingering">malingerer</a> is pretending to suffer from.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="capital_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Capital_ship" title="Capital ship">Capital ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A navy<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s most important warships, generally possessing the heaviest firepower and armor and traditionally much larger than other naval vessels, but not formally defined. During the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a>, generally understood to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ships-of-the-line" title="Ships-of-the-line">ships-of-the-line</a>; during the second half of the 19th century and the 20th century, understood to be <a href="/wiki/Battleship" title="Battleship">battleships</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battlecruiser" title="Battlecruiser">battlecruisers</a>; and since the 1940s considered to include <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a>. Since the second half of the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile_submarine" title="Ballistic missile submarine">ballistic missile submarines</a> sometimes have been considered capital ships.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="capsize" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capsize" title="Capsize">Capsize</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When a ship or boat lists too far and rolls over, exposing the keel. On large vessels, this often results in the sinking of the ship. Compare <a href="/wiki/Turtling_(sailing)" title="Turtling (sailing)">Turtling</a>, infra.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="capstan" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Capstan_(nautical)" title="Capstan (nautical)">Capstan</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large winch with a vertical axis. A full-sized human-powered capstan is a waist-high cylindrical machine, operated by a number of hands who each insert a horizontal <i>capstan bar</i> in holes in the capstan and walk in a circle. Used to wind in anchors or other heavy objects; and sometimes to administer flogging over.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="captain" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Captain</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The person lawfully in command of a vessel. "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_(nautical)" title="Captain (nautical)">Captain</a>" is an informal title of respect given to the commander of a naval vessel regardless of his or her formal rank; aboard a merchant ship, the ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s <i>master</i> is her "captain."</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A <a href="/wiki/Captain_(naval)" title="Captain (naval)">naval officer</a> with a rank between <a href="/wiki/Commander" title="Commander">commander</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commodore_(rank)" title="Commodore (rank)">commodore</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  In the US Navy, US Coast Guard, and National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration Corps, a commissioned officer of a grade superior to a <a href="/wiki/Commander_(United_States)" title="Commander (United States)">commander</a> and junior to a <a href="/wiki/Rear_admiral_(United_States)" title="Rear admiral (United States)">rear admiral (lower half)</a>, equal in grade or rank to a US Army, US Marine Corps, or US Air Force colonel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="captain_of_the_port" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_of_the_Port" title="Captain of the Port">Captain of the Port</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  In the United Kingdom, a Royal Navy officer, usually a <a href="/wiki/Captain_(naval)" title="Captain (naval)">captain</a>, responsible for the day-to-day operation of a naval dockyard.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In the United States, a US Coast Guard officer, usually a <a href="/wiki/Captain_(United_States_O-6)" title="Captain (United States O-6)">captain</a>, responsible for enforcement of safety, security, and marine environmental protection regulations in a commercial port.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="captain.27s_daughter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Captain's daughter</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The <a href="/wiki/Cat_o%27_nine_tails" title="Cat o' nine tails">cat o' nine tails</a>, which in principle is only used on board on the captain's (or a court martial's) personal orders.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="car_carrier" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Car_carrier" title="Car carrier">Car carrier</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A cargo ship specially designed or fitted to carry large numbers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobiles</a> Modern <i>pure car carriers</i> have a fully enclosed, boxlike superstructure that extends along the entire length and across the entire breadth of the ship, enclosing the automobiles. The similar <i>pure car/truck carrier</i> also can accommodate trucks.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="car_float" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Car_float" title="Car float">Car float</a> (also <i>railroad car float</i> or <i>rail barge</i>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An unpowered barge with railroad tracks mounted on its deck, used to move railroad cars across water obstacles.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="caravel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Caravel" title="Caravel">Caravel</a> (also <i>caravelle</i>) <span id="Caravelle"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small, highly maneuverable sailing ship with <a href="/wiki/Lateen" title="Lateen">lateen</a> rig used by the Portuguese in the 15th and 16th centuries to explore along the West African coast and into the Atlantic Ocean.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cardinal" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cardinal</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Referring to the four main points of the compass: north, south, east and west. See also <i>bearing</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="careening" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Careening" title="Careening">Careening</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Tilting a ship on its side, usually when beached, to clean or repair the hull below the water line. Also known as to "heave down".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cargo_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cargo_ship" title="Cargo ship">Cargo ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Any sort of ship or vessel that carries cargo, goods, and materials from one port to another, including general cargo ships (designed to carry <a href="/wiki/Break_bulk_cargo" title="Break bulk cargo">break bulk cargo</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bulk_carrier" title="Bulk carrier">bulk carriers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Container_ship" title="Container ship">container ships</a>, multipurpose vessels, and <a href="/wiki/Tanker_(ship)" title="Tanker (ship)">tankers</a>. Tankers, however, although technically cargo ships, are routinely thought of as constituting a completely separate category.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="carpenter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Carpenter</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  In the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a>, a warrant officer responsible for the hull, masts, spars, and boats of a vessel, and whose responsibility was to sound the well to see if the vessel was making water.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A senior rating responsible for all the woodwork aboard a vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="carrack" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Carrack" title="Carrack">Carrack</a> (also <i>nau</i>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A three- or four-masted sailing ship used by Western Europeans in the Atlantic Ocean from the 15th through the early 17th century.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="carrier" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Carrier</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An <i>aircraft carrier</i> (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="carronade" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Carronade" title="Carronade">Carronade</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A short, smoothbore, cast iron naval cannon, used from the 1770s to the 1850s as a powerful, short-range anti-ship and anti-crew weapon.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="carvel_built" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Carvel_(boat_building)" title="Carvel (boat building)">Carvel built</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A method of constructing wooden hulls by fixing planks to a frame so that the planks butt up against each other. Cf. "clinker built".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  To prepare an anchor, after raising it by lifting it with a tackle to the <i>cat head</i>, prior to securing (<i>fishing</i>) it alongside for sea. (An anchor raised to the cat head is said to be <i>catted</i>.)</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The cat o' nine tails (see below).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  A <a href="/wiki/Cat_rigged" title="Cat rigged">cat-rigged</a> boat or <i><a href="/wiki/Catboat" title="Catboat">catboat</a></i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cat_o.27_nine_tails" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cat_o%27_nine_tails" title="Cat o' nine tails">Cat o' nine tails</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A short nine-tailed <a href="/wiki/Whip" title="Whip">whip</a> kept by the bosun's mate to <a href="/wiki/Flagellation" title="Flagellation">flog</a> sailors (and soldiers in the Army). When not in use, the cat was kept in a baize bag, this is a possible origin for the term "cat out of the bag," though livestock trade was more likely<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> where this phrase came from. "Not enough room to swing a cat" also derives from this.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="catamaran" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Catamaran" title="Catamaran">Catamaran</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel with two hulls.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="catboat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Catboat" title="Catboat">Catboat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Cat_rigged" title="Cat rigged">cat-rigged</a> vessel with a single mast mounted close to the bow, and only one sail, usually on a <a href="/wiki/Gaff_rig" title="Gaff rig">gaff</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="catharpin" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Catharpin" title="Catharpin">Catharpin</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A short rope or iron clamp used to brace in the shrouds toward the masts so as to give a freer sweep to the yards.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cathead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cathead" title="Cathead">Cathead</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A beam extending out from the hull used to support an anchor when raised in order to secure or 'fish' it.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cat.27s_paws" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cat%27s_paw_(wave)" title="Cat's paw (wave)">Cats paws</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Light variable winds on calm waters producing scattered areas of small waves.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ceiling" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ceiling</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A lining applied to the interior of a hull for both aesthetic reasons and to bar or insulate the ship's cargo from the cold hull surface. Often made of thin strips of wood, attached horizontally with a small gap between to allow air flow to the interior hull surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-22"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The inside planking forming the floor of a barges hold; at the lining was carried up to the inwale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-23"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="center_of_effort_.28or_centre_of_effort.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Center of effort (or centre of effort)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The point of origin of net aerodynamic force on sails, roughly located in the geometric center of a sail, but the actual position of the center of effort will vary with sail plan, sail trim or airfoil profile, boat trim, and point of sail. Also known as <i>center</i> (or <i>centre</i>) <i>of pressure</i></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="center_of_lateral_resistance_.28or_centre_of_lateral_resistance.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Center of lateral resistance (or centre of lateral resistance)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The point of origin of net hydrodynamic resistance on the submerged structure of a boat, especially a sailboat. This is the pivot point about which the boat turns when unbalanced external forces are applied, similar to the center of gravity. On a balanced sailboat the center of effort should align vertically with the center of lateral resistance. If this is not the case the boat will be unbalanced and exhibit either lee helm or weather helm and will be difficult to control.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="centerline" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Centerline (or centerline) <span id="Centreline"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An imaginary line down the center of a vessel lengthwise. Any structure or anything mounted or carried on a vessel that straddles this line and is equidistant from either side of the vessel is <i>on the centerline</i> (or <i>centreline</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="centreboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Centreboard" title="Centreboard">Centreboard</a> (or centerboard)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A board or plate lowered through the hull of a <a href="/wiki/Dinghy" title="Dinghy">dinghy</a> on the centreline to resist leeway.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="chafing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chafing_(nautical)" title="Chafing (nautical)">Chafing</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Wear on line or sail caused by constant rubbing against another surface.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="chafing_gear" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Chafing gear</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Material applied to a line or spar to prevent or reduce chafing. See <a href="/wiki/Baggywrinkle" title="Baggywrinkle">Baggywrinkle</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="chain_locker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Chain locker</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A space in the forward part of the ship, typically beneath the bow in front of the foremost collision bulkhead, that contains the anchor chain when the anchor is secured for sea.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="chain-shot" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chain-shot" title="Chain-shot">Chain-shot</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Cannon balls linked with chain used to damage rigging and masts.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="chain-wale_or_channel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Chain-wale or channel</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A broad, thick plank that projects horizontally from each of a ship's sides abreast a mast, distinguished as the fore, main, or mizzen channel accordingly, serving to extend the base for the shrouds, which supports the mast.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="chains" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chains_(nautical)" title="Chains (nautical)">Chains</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Small platforms built into the sides of a ship to spread the shrouds to a more advantageous angle. Also used as a platform for manual <a href="/wiki/Depth_sounding" title="Depth sounding">depth sounding</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="charley_noble" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Charley Noble</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The metal stovepipe chimney from a cook shack on the deck of a ship or from a stove in a galley.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="charthouse" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Charthouse</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A compartment, especially in the Royal Navy, from which the ship was navigated.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="chase_gun" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chase_gun" title="Chase gun">Chase gun</a>, chase piece, or chaser <span id="Chase_piece"></span><span id="Chaser"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannon</a> pointing forward or aft, often of longer range than other guns. Those on the bow (<i>bow chaser</i>) were used to fire upon a ship ahead, while those on the rear (<i>stern chaser</i>) were used to ward off pursuing vessels. Unlike guns pointing to the side, chasers could be brought to bear in a chase without slowing.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cheeks" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cheeks</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Wooden blocks at the side of a spar.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The sides of a block or gun-carriage.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="chine" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chine_(boating)" title="Chine (boating)">Chine</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  An angle in the hull.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A line formed where the sides of a boat meet the bottom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-24"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup> Soft chine is when the two sides join at a shallow angle, and hard chine is when they join at a steep angle.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="chock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Chock</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Hole or ring attached to the hull to guide a line via that point. An opening in a ships bulwark normally oval in shape designed to allow mooring lines to be fastened to cleats or bits mounted to the ship's deck. See also Panama chock and Dutchman's chock.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="chock-a-block" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Chock-a-block</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Rigging blocks that are so tight against one another that they cannot be further tightened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-25"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="chronometer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Marine_chronometer" title="Marine chronometer">Chronometer</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A timekeeper accurate enough to be used to determine <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a> by means of <a href="/wiki/Celestial_navigation" title="Celestial navigation">celestial navigation</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cigarette_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cigarette boat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">see <i>go-fast boat</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="citadel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Citadel" title="Citadel">Citadel</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A fortified safe room on a vessel to take shelter in the event of pirate attack. Previously, a fortified room to protect ammunition and machinery from damage.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="civil_red_ensign" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Civil <a href="/wiki/Red_Ensign" title="Red Ensign">Red Ensign</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The British Naval Ensign or Flag of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Merchant_Navy" title="British Merchant Navy">British Merchant Navy</a></i>, a red flag with the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Flag of the United Kingdom">Union Flag</a> in the upper left corner. Colloquially called the "red duster".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="class" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ship_class" title="Ship class">Class</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A group of naval ships of the same or similar design.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A standard of construction for merchant vessels, including standards for specific types or specialized capabilities of some types of merchant vessels. A ship meeting the standard is <i>in class</i>, one not meeting them is <i>out of class</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="clean_bill_of_health" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Clean bill of health</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A certificate issued by a port indicating that the ship carries no infectious diseases. Also called a <a href="/wiki/Pratique" title="Pratique">pratique</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="clean_slate" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Clean slate</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">At the helm, the watch keeper would record details of speed, distances, headings, etc. on a slate. At the beginning of a new watch the slate would be wiped clean.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="clear" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Clear</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To perform customs and immigration legalities prior to leaving port.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cleat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cleat_(nautical)" title="Cleat (nautical)">Cleat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A stationary device used to secure a rope aboard a vessel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-26"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="clench" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Clench</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A method of fixing together two pieces of wood, usually overlapping planks, by driving a nail through both planks as well as a washer-like rove. The nail is then burred or riveted over to complete the fastening.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="clew" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Clew</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The lower corners of square sails or the corner of a triangular sail at the end of the boom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-27"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="clew-lines" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Clewlines_and_buntlines" title="Clewlines and buntlines">Clew-lines</a> <span id="Clewlines"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Used to truss up the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_of_a_sail#The_corners" title="Parts of a sail">clews</a>, the lower corners of square sails. Used to reduce and stow a barge's topsail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-28"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="clinker_built" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Clinker_(boat_building)" title="Clinker (boat building)">Clinker built</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A method of constructing hulls that involves overlapping planks, and/or plates, much like Viking longships, resulting in speed and flexibility in small boat hulls. Cf. "carvel built".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="clipper" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Clipper" title="Clipper">Clipper</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A very fast sailing ship of the 19th century that had three or more masts, a square rig, a long, low hull, and a sharply raked stem.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="close_aboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Close aboard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Near a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="close-hauled" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Points_of_sail" title="Points of sail">Close-hauled</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Of a vessel <i>beating</i> as close to the wind direction as possible.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="clove_hitch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Clove hitch</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A bend used to attach a rope to a post or bollard. Also used to finish tying off the foresail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-29"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="club_hauling" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Club hauling</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The ship drops one of its anchors at high speed to turn abruptly. This was sometimes used as a means to get a good firing angle on a pursuing vessel. See <i>kedge</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="coal_hulk" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Coal hulk</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hulk_(ship)" title="Hulk (ship)">hulk</a> used to store coal.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="coal_trimmer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Coal_trimmer" title="Coal trimmer">Coal trimmer</a>, or Trimmer <span id="Trimmer"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">person responsible for ensuring that a coal-fired vessel remains in 'trim' (evenly balanced) as coal is consumed on a voyage.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="coaming" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Coaming" title="Coaming">Coaming</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The raised edge of a hatch, cockpit or skylight to help keep out water.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="coaster" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Coaster (or <i><a href="/wiki/Coastal_trading_vessel" title="Coastal trading vessel">coastal trading vessel</a></i>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A shallow-hulled ship used for trade between locations on the same island or continent.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cockbill" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cockbill)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Used of spars, to stow by swinging askew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-30"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cockpit" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cockpit_(sailing)" title="Cockpit (sailing)">Cockpit</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The seating area (not to be confused with Deck). The area towards the stern of a small decked vessel that houses the rudder controls.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cog" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cog_(ship)" title="Cog (ship)">Cog</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of sailing ship with a single mast and square-rigged single sail first developed in the 10th century and widely used, particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> region, in seagoing trade from the 12th through the 14th century.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="collier" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Collier_(ship)" title="Collier (ship)">Collier</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A bulk cargo ship designed to carry coal, especially such a ship in naval use to supply coal to coal-fired warships.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="comber" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Comber</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A long, curving wave breaking on the shore.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="come_about" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Come about</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  To tack.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To change tack.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  To manoeuver the bow of a sailing vessel across the wind so that the wind changes from one side of the vessel to the other.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  To position a vessel with respect to the wind after tacking.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="come_to" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Come to</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To stop a sailing vessel, especially by turning into the wind.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="commission" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ship_commissioning" title="Ship commissioning">Commission</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To formally place (a naval vessel) into active service, after which the vessel is said to be <i>in commission</i>. Sometimes used less formally to mean placing a commercial ship into service.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="commodore" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Commodore</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  <a href="/wiki/Commodore_(rank)" title="Commodore (rank)">Commodore (rank)</a>, a military rank used in many navies that is superior to a navy captain, but below a rear admiral. Often equivalent to the rank of "flotilla admiral" or sometimes "counter admiral" in non-English-speaking navies.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convoy_Commodore" title="Convoy Commodore">Convoy Commodore</a>, a civilian put in charge of the good order of the merchant ships in British <a href="/wiki/Convoy" title="Convoy">convoys</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, but with no authority over naval ships escorting the convoy.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commodore_(yacht_club)" title="Commodore (yacht club)">Commodore (yacht club)</a>, an officer of a yacht club.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commodore_(Sea_Scouts)" title="Commodore (Sea Scouts)">Commodore (Sea Scouts)</a>, a position in the Boy Scouts of America's Sea Scout program.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="communication_tube" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Communication tube, <a href="/wiki/Speaking_tube" title="Speaking tube">speaking tube</a>, or voice tube <span id="Voice_tube"></span><span id="Speaking_tube"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An air-filled tube, usually armored, allowing speech between the <a href="/wiki/Conning_tower" title="Conning tower">conning tower</a> with the below-decks control spaces in a warship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="companionway" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Companionway" title="Companionway">Companionway</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A raised and windowed hatchway in the ship's <a href="/wiki/Deck_(ship)" title="Deck (ship)">deck</a>, with a ladder leading below and the hooded entrance-hatch to the main cabins.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="compass" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">Compass</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Navigational instrument showing the direction of the vessel in relation to the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Geographical_pole" title="Geographical pole">geographical poles</a> or <a href="/wiki/Poles_of_astronomical_bodies" title="Poles of astronomical bodies">magnetic poles</a>. Commonly consists of a magnet aligned with the Earth's magnetic field, but other technologies have also been developed, such as the <a href="/wiki/Gyrocompass" title="Gyrocompass">gyrocompass</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="complement" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Complement</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The number of persons in a ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s crew, including officers.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="comprise" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Comprise</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To include or contain: As applied to a naval task force, the listing of all assigned units for a single transient purpose (mission). "The Task Force <i>comprises</i> Ship A, Ship B, and Ship C." 'Comprise' means exhaustive inclusion – there aren't any other parts to the task force, and each ship has a permanent squadron existence, independent of the task force.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="conn" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Conn_(nautical)" title="Conn (nautical)">Conn</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">(Also written <b>con</b>, <b>conne</b>, <b>conde</b>, <b>cunde</b>, or <b>cun</b>) To direct a ship or submarine from a position of command. While performing this duty, an officer is said to <i>have the conn</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="conning_officer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Conning officer</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An officer on a naval vessel responsible for instructing the helmsman on the course to steer. While performing this duty, the officer is said to <i>have the conn</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="conning_tower" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Conning_tower" title="Conning tower">Conning tower</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The armoured control tower of an iron or steel warship built between the mid-19th and mid-20th centuries from which the ship was navigated in battle.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A tower-like structure on the dorsal (topside) surface of a submarine, serving in submarines built before the mid-20th century as a connecting structure between the bridge and pressure hull and housing instruments and controls from which the periscopes were used to direct the submarine and launch <a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">torpedo</a> attacks. Since the mid-20th century, it has been replaced by the <i><a href="/wiki/Sail_(submarine)" title="Sail (submarine)">sail</a></i> (United States usage) or <i>fin</i> (European and British Commonwealth usage), a structure similar in appearance which no longer plays a function in directing the submarine.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="consort" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Consort_(nautical)" title="Consort (nautical)">Consort</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Unpowered <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> vessels, usually a fully loaded <a href="/wiki/Schooner" title="Schooner">schooner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barge" title="Barge">barge</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steamer</a> barge, towed by a larger steamer that would often tow more than one barge. The consort system was used in the Great Lakes from the 1860s to around 1920.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="constant_bearing.2C_decreasing_range" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Constant_bearing,_decreasing_range" title="Constant bearing, decreasing range">Constant bearing, decreasing range</a> (CBDR) <span id="CBDR"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When two boats are approaching each other from any angle and this angle remains the same over time (constant bearing) they are on a collision course. Because of the implication of disaster (ships might collide) it has come to mean a problem or an obstacle which is heading your way. Often used in the sense of a warning, as in "watch out for this problem you might not see coming."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="container_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Container_ship" title="Container ship">Container ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A cargo ship that carries all of her cargo in truck-size <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermodal_containers" title="Intermodal containers">intermodal containers</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="convoy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Convoy" title="Convoy">Convoy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A group of ships traveling together for mutual support and protection.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="corinthian" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Corinthian</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An <a href="/wiki/Amateur_sports" title="Amateur sports">amateur</a> <a href="/wiki/Yachting" title="Yachting">yachter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="corrector" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Corrector" title="Corrector">Corrector</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A device to correct the ship's compass, for example counteracting errors due to the magnetic effects of a steel hull.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="corsair" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Corsair</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A French privateer, especially from the port of St-Malo.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Any privateer or pirate.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  A ship used by privateers or pirates, especially of French nationality.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  <i><a href="/wiki/Corsair_(dinghy)" title="Corsair (dinghy)">Corsair</a></i>, a class of 16-foot (4.9-meter) three-handed sailing dinghy.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="corvette" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Corvette" title="Corvette">Corvette</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A flush-decked sailing warship of the 17th, 18th, and 19th centuries having a single tier of guns, ranked next below a <a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">frigate</a>. Called in the US Navy a <i><a href="/wiki/Sloop-of-war" title="Sloop-of-war">sloop-of-war</a></i>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A lightly armed and armored warship of the 20th and 21st centuries, smaller than a <a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">frigate</a>, capable of trans-oceanic duty.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cottonclad" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cottonclad_warship" title="Cottonclad warship">Cottonclad</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A steam-powered wooden warship protected from enemy fire by bales of <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a> lining its sides, most commonly associated with some of the warships employed by the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States of America</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> (1861–1865).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="counter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Counter</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The part of the stern above the waterline that extends beyond the rudder stock culminating in a small transom. A long counter increases the waterline length when the boat is heeled, so increasing hull speed.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="counterflood" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Counterflood</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To deliberately flood compartments on the opposite side from already flooded ones. Usually done to reduce a <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_list" title="Angle of list">list</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="course" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Course</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The direction in which a vessel is being steered, usually given in degrees.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="courses" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Courses</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">the lowest square sail on each mast – The mainsail, <a href="/wiki/Foresail" title="Foresail">foresail</a>, and the mizzen on a four masted ship (the after most mast usually sets a gaff driver or spanker instead of a square sail).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cowl" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cowl</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s ventilator with a bell-shaped top which can be swivelled to catch the wind and force it below.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A vertical projection of a ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s funnel which directs the smoke away from the bridge.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="coxswain" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Coxswain" title="Coxswain">Coxswain</a> or cockswain <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">ən</span></span>/</a></span></span><span id="Cockswain"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The helmsman or crew member in command of a boat.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="crab" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Crab</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A winch used for raising the leeboard which has a barrel fir pulling in the staysail sheets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-31"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="crance.2Fcrans.2Fcranze_iron" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Crance/Crans/Cranze iron</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A fitting, mounted at the end of a bowsprit to which stays are attached.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="crane_vessel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Crane_vessel" title="Crane vessel">Crane vessel</a> or crane ship</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship with a crane specialized in lifting heavy loads.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="crazy_ivan" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Crazy Ivan</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">US Navy slang for a maneuver in which a submerged Soviet or Russian submarine suddenly turns 180 degrees or through 360 degrees to detect submarines following it.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="crew" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Crew" title="Crew">Crew</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  On warships and merchant ships, those members of a ship's company who are not officers</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  On leisure vessels with no formal chain of command, those persons who are not the skipper (q.v.) or passengers.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="crew_management" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Crew_management" title="Crew management">Crew management</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Otherwise known as crewing, are the services rendered by specialised shipping companies to manage the human resources and manning of all types of vessels, including recruitment, deployment to vessel, scheduling, training, as well as the ongoing management and administrative duties of seafarers, such as payroll, travel arrangements, insurance and health schemes, overall career development, as well as their day-to-day welfare</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cringle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cringle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A rope loop, usually at the corners of a sail, for fixing the sail to a spar. They are often reinforced with a metal eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-32"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cro.27jack" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cro'jack or crossjack <span id="Crossjack"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">a square yard used to spread the foot of a topsail where no course is set, e.g. on the foremast of a topsail <a href="/wiki/Schooner" title="Schooner">schooner</a> or above the driver on the mizzen mast of a ship rigged vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="crosstrees" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Crosstrees" title="Crosstrees">Crosstrees</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">two horizontal struts at the upper ends of the topmasts of sailboats, used to anchor the shrouds from the topgallant mast. Lateral spraders for the topmast shrouds (standing back stays) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-33"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="crow_flies.2C_as_the" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Crow flies, as the</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">see <i>as the crow flies</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="crow.27s_nest" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Crow%27s_nest" title="Crow's nest">Crow's nest</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Specifically a masthead constructed with sides and sometimes a roof to shelter the lookouts from the weather, generally by whaling vessels, this has become a generic term for what is properly called masthead. See <i>masthead</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cruise_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">Cruise ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A passenger ship used for pleasure voyages, where the voyage itself and the ship's amenities are part of the experience, as well as the different destinations along the way. Transportation is not the prime purpose, as cruise ships operate mostly on routes that return passengers to their originating port. A cruise ship contrasts with a <i>passenger liner</i>, which is a passenger ship that provides a scheduled service between published ports primarily as a mode of transportation. Large, prestigious passenger ships used for either purpose sometimes are called <i>ocean liners</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cruiser" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cruiser" title="Cruiser">Cruiser</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  From the mid-19th century to the mid-20th century, a classification for a wide variety of gun- and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">torpedo</a>-armed warships, usually but not always armored, intended for independent scouting, raiding, or commerce protection; some were designed also to provide direct support to a battlefleet. Cruisers carried out functions performed previously by the cruising ships (sailing frigates and sloops) of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  From the early 20th century to the mid-20th century, a type of armored warship with varying armament and of various sizes, but always smaller than a <a href="/wiki/Battleship" title="Battleship">battleship</a> and larger than a <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">destroyer</a>, capable of both direct support of a battle fleet and independent operations, armed with guns and sometimes torpedoes.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  After the mid-20th century, various types of warships of intermediate size armed with guided missiles and sometimes guns, intended for air defense of <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a> and associated task forces or for anti-ship missile attack against such forces; virtually indistinguishable from large destroyers since the late 20th century.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="crutches" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Crutches</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Metal Y shaped pins to hold oars whilst rowing.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cuddy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cuddy_(cabin)" title="Cuddy (cabin)">Cuddy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small cabin in a boat; a cabin, for the use of the captain, in the after part of a sailing ship under the poop deck.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cunningham" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cunningham_(sailing)" title="Cunningham (sailing)">Cunningham</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A line invented by <a href="/wiki/Briggs_Cunningham" title="Briggs Cunningham">Briggs Cunningham</a>, used to control the shape of a sail.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cunt_splice" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Rope_splicing#Types_of_splices" title="Rope splicing">Cunt splice</a> or cut splice <span id="Cut_splice"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A join between two lines, similar to an eye-splice, where each rope end is joined to the other a short distance along, making an opening which closes under tension.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cuntline" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cuntline</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The "valley" between the strands of a rope or cable. Before serving a section of laid rope e.g. to protect it from chafing, it may be "wormed" by laying yarns in the cuntlines, giving that section an even cylindrical shape.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cut_and_run" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cut and run</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When wanting to make a quick escape, a ship might cut lashings to sails or cables for anchors, causing damage to the rigging, or losing an anchor, but shortening the time needed to make ready by bypassing the proper procedures.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cut_of_his_jib" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cut of his jib</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The "cut" of a sail refers to its shape. Since this would vary between ships, it could be used both to identify a familiar vessel at a distance, and to judge the possible sailing qualities of an unknown one. Also used figuratively of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeans_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeans-17">[17]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cutter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cutter_(boat)" title="Cutter (boat)">Cutter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A small single-masted boat, fore-and-aft rigged, with two or more headsails and often a bowsprit. The mast is set farther back than on a <a href="/wiki/Sloop" title="Sloop">sloop</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A small boat serving a larger vessel, used to ferry passengers or light stores between larger vessels and the shore.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  In the 20th and 21st centuries, a small- or medium-sized vessel whose occupants exercise official authority, such as harbor <a href="/wiki/Maritime_pilot" title="Maritime pilot">pilots'</a> cutters, US Coast Guard Cutters, and <a href="/wiki/UK_Border_Agency" title="UK Border Agency">UK Border Agency</a> cutters.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="cutwater" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Cutwater</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The forward curve of the stem (<i>q.v.</i>) of a ship</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="D">D</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: D">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="daggerboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Daggerboard" title="Daggerboard">Daggerboard</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of light <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centerboard" title="Centerboard">centerboard</a> that is lifted vertically; often in pairs, with the leeward one lowered when beating.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dandy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dandy</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A rig with a small mizzen abaft the steering post.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-34"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In British usage, an alternative term for a <a href="/wiki/Yawl" title="Yawl">yawl</a> (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  In British usage, a small after-sail on a yawl.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dart" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dart</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To run dart, to run dead before the wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-35"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="davit" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Davit" title="Davit">Davit</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A spar formerly used on board ships as a crane to hoist the flukes of the anchor to the top of the bow, without injuring the sides of the ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A crane, often working in pairs and usually made of steel, used to lower things over the side of a ship, including launching a lifeboat over the side of a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="davy_jones.27_locker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Davy_Jones%27_Locker" title="Davy Jones' Locker">Davy Jones' Locker</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An <a href="/wiki/Idiom" title="Idiom">idiom</a> for the bottom of the sea.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="day_beacon" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Day_beacon" title="Day beacon">Day beacon</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An unlighted fixed structure which is equipped with a dayboard for daytime identification.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="day-blink" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Day-blink</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Moment at dawn where, from some point on the mast, a lookout can see above low lying mist which envelops the ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dayboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dayboard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The daytime identifier of an aid to navigation presenting one of several standard shapes (square, triangle, rectangle) and colors (red, green, white, orange, yellow, or black).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dead_ahead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dead ahead</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Exactly ahead, directly ahead, directly in front.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dead_in_the_water" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dead in the water</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Not moving (used only when a vessel is afloat and neither tied up nor anchored). Term is abbreviated to DIW by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Navy" title="US Navy">US Navy</a>. It is often used to indicate a pirate or drug runner vessel has been immobilised.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dead_run" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dead run</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>running</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dead_wake" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dead wake</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The trail of a fading disturbance in the water. See also <i>wake</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="deadeye" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Deadeye" title="Deadeye">Deadeye</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A wooden block with three holes (but no pulleys) which is spliced to a shroud. It is used to adjust the tension in the <a href="/wiki/Standing_rigging" title="Standing rigging">standing rigging</a> of large sailing vessels, by lacing through the holes with a lanyard to the deck. Performs the same job as a turnbuckle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-36"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="deadhead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Deadhead</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <i>snag</i> (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="deadlight" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Deadlight</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A strong shutter fitted over a porthole or other opening that can be closed in bad weather.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="deadrise" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Deadrise</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The design angle of the hull at the keel (q.v.) relative to horizontal when viewing the cross section, or the distance of rise from the keel to a defined point on the hull.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="deadwood" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Deadwood</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A wooden part (vertical timbers or planking) of the centerline structure of a boat, usually between the sternpost and amidships. It is used to "fill the spaces where, owing to the shape of the vessel, the floor-timbers have to be discontinued."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luce_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luce-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="death_roll" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Death_roll" title="Death roll">Death roll</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In a <a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">keel</a> boat, a <b>death roll</b> is the act of <a href="/wiki/Broach_(sailing)" title="Broach (sailing)">broaching</a> to windward, putting the <a href="/wiki/Spinnaker_pole" title="Spinnaker pole">spinnaker pole</a> into the water and causing a crash-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jibe_(sailing)" title="Jibe (sailing)">gybe</a> of the boom and mainsail, which sweep across the deck and plunge down into the water. During a death roll, the boat <a href="/wiki/Ship_motions#Rotation_motions" title="Ship motions"><i>rolls</i></a> from side to side, becoming gradually more unstable until either it capsizes or the skipper reacts correctly to prevent it.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="debarcation_or_disembarkation" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Debark_(ship)" title="Debark (ship)">Debarcation</a> or disembarkation</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The process of leaving a ship or aircraft, or removing goods from a ship or aircraft.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="debunk" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Debunk</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The process of removing fuel from a vessel. After a ship wreck, a "debunkering" operation will be performed in an effort to minimize damage and protect the environment from fuel spills.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Deck_(ship)" title="Deck (ship)">Decks</a> <span id="Decks"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The top of the boat; the surface is removed to accommodate the seating area. The structures forming the approximately horizontal surfaces in the ship's general structure. Unlike flats, they are a structural part of the ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="deck_hand" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Deck hand or decky <span id="Decky"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A person whose job involves aiding the deck supervisor in (un)mooring, anchoring, maintenance, and general evolutions on deck.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="deck_supervisor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Deck supervisor</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The person in charge of all evolutions and maintenance on deck; sometimes split into two groups: forward deck supervisor, aft deck supervisor.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="deckhead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Deckhead" title="Deckhead">Deckhead</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The under-side of the deck above. The inside of the boat is normally paneled over to hide the structure, pipes, electrical wires. It can be in thin wood planks, often covered with a vinyl lining, or in thin PVC or now even in fiberglass planks.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="decks_awash" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Decks awash</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A situation in which the deck of the vessel is partially or wholly submerged, possibly as a result of excessive listing or a loss of buoyancy.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="decommission" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ship_decommissioning" title="Ship decommissioning">Decommission</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To formally take (a naval vessel) out of active service, after which the vessel is said to be <i>out of commission</i> or <i>decommissioned</i>. Sometimes used less formally to mean taking a commercial ship out of service.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="depot_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Depot_ship" title="Depot ship">Depot ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship which acts as a mobile or fixed base for other ships and submarines or supports a naval base.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="depth_of_hold" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Depth of hold</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The height from the lowest part of the hull inside the ship, at its midpoint, to the ceiling that is made up of the uppermost full length deck. For old warships it is to the ceiling that is made up of the lowermost full length deck.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="derrick" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Derrick" title="Derrick">Derrick</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A lifting device composed of one mast or pole and a boom or jib which is hinged freely at the bottom.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="despatch_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Despatch boat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Alternative spelling of <i>dispatch boat</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="destroyer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">Destroyer</a> (originally <i>torpedo boat destroyer</i>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of fast and maneuverable small warship introduced in the 1890s to protect <a href="/wiki/Capital_ship" title="Capital ship">capital ships</a> from <a href="/wiki/Torpedo_boat" title="Torpedo boat">torpedo boat</a> attack, since increased in size and capabilities to become a long-endurance warship intended to escort larger vessels in a fleet, convoy, or battle group and defend them against submarines, surface ships, aircraft, and missiles.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="destroyer_escort" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Destroyer_escort" title="Destroyer escort">Destroyer escort</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">US Navy term for a smaller, lightly armed warship built in large numbers during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> (and in smaller numbers thereafter), cheaper, slower, and less-well-armed than a <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">destroyer</a> but larger and more heavily armed than a <a href="/wiki/Corvette" title="Corvette">corvette</a> and designed to escort convoys of merchant ships or naval auxiliaries or second-line naval forces. Employed primarily for anti-submarine warfare, but also provided some protection against aircraft and smaller surface ships. Generally known as "<a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">frigates</a>" in other navies, and designated as such in the US Navy as well by the 1970s.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="destroyer_leader" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Destroyer_leader" title="Destroyer leader">Destroyer leader</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large destroyer suitable for commanding a flotilla of destroyers or other small warships; a type of <i><a href="/wiki/Flotilla_leader" title="Flotilla leader">flotilla leader</a></i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="devil_seam" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Devil seam</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The devil was possibly a slang term for the garboard seam, hence "between the devil and the deep blue sea" being an allusion to keel hauling, but a more popular version seems to be the seam between the waterway and the <a href="/wiki/Stanchion" title="Stanchion">stanchions</a> which would be difficult to get at, requiring a cranked caulking iron, and a restricted swing of the caulking mallet.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="devil_to_pay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Devil to pay (or Devil to pay, and no pitch hot)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">"Paying" the devil is sealing the <i>devil seam</i>. It is a difficult and unpleasant job (with no resources) because of the shape of the seam (up against the <a href="/wiki/Stanchion" title="Stanchion">stanchions</a>) or if the devil refers to the garboard seam, it must be done with the ship slipped or careened.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dhow" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dhow" title="Dhow">Dhow</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">the generic name of a number of traditional sailing vessels with one or more masts with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lateen_sails" title="Lateen sails">lateen sails</a> used in the Red Sea and Indian Ocean region, typically weighing 300 to 500 tons, with a long, thin hull.
