They are trading vessels primarily used to carry heavy items, like fruit, fresh water or merchandise. Crews vary from about thirty to around twelve, depending on the size of the vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dinghy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dinghy" title="Dinghy">Dinghy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A type of small boat, often carried or towed as a ship's boat by a larger vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Also a small racing yacht or recreational open sailing boat, often used for beginner training rather than sailing full-sized yachts.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  Utility dinghies are usually rowboats or have an outboard motor, but some are rigged for sailing.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="directional_light" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Directional light</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A light illuminating a sector or very narrow angle and intended to mark a direction to be followed.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dipping_the_eye" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dipping the eye</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Multiple_mooring_correct.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="800" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Multiple_mooring_correct.png/170px-Multiple_mooring_correct.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Multiple_mooring_correct.png/255px-Multiple_mooring_correct.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Multiple_mooring_correct.png/340px-Multiple_mooring_correct.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Multiple_mooring_correct.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dipping the eye</div>
</div>
</div>
Method of attaching more than one hawser to a single bollard, so that each can be lifted off without disturbing the other(s). The second hawser is passed under the first, then up through the eye of the first (hence the name), before being secured over the bollard.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dispatch_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dispatch_boat" title="Dispatch boat">Dispatch boat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel ranging in size from a small boat to a large ship tasked to carry military dispatches from ship to ship, from ship to shore, or, occasionally, from shore to shore.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="displacement" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Displacement_(ship)" title="Displacement (ship)">Displacement</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The weight of water displaced by the immersed volume of a ship's hull, exactly equivalent to the weight of the whole ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="displacement_hull" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Displacement <a href="/wiki/Hull_(watercraft)" title="Hull (watercraft)">hull</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A hull designed to travel through the water, rather than planing over it.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="disrate" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Disrate</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To reduce in rank or rating; demote.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="distinguishing_mark" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distinguishing_mark" title="Distinguishing mark">Distinguishing mark</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A flag flown to distinguish ships of one seagoing service of a given country from ships of the country<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s other seagoing service(s) when ships of more than one of the country<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s seagoing services fly the same <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ensign_(flag)" title="Ensign (flag)">ensign</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dock_(maritime)" title="Dock (maritime)">Dock</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  In American usage, a fixed structure attached to shore to which a vessel is secured when in port, generally synonymous with <i>pier</i> and <i>wharf</i>, except that <i>pier</i> tends to refer to structures used for tying up commercial ships and to structures extending from shore for use in fishing, while <i>dock</i> refers more generally to facilities used for tying up ships or boats, including recreational craft.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In British usage, the body of water between two piers or wharves which accommodates vessels tied up at the piers or wharves.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  To tie up along a pier or wharf.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dockyard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dockyard" title="Dockyard">Dockyard</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A facility where ships or boats are built and repaired. Routinely used as a synonym for <i>shipyard</i>, although <i>dockyard</i> sometimes is associated more closely with a facility used for maintenance and basing activities, while <i>shipyard</i> sometimes is associated more closely with a facility used in construction.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dodger" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dodger</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A hood forward of a hatch or cockpit to protect the crew from wind and spray. Can be soft or hard.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dog_watch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dog_watch" title="Dog watch">Dog watch</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A short <a href="/wiki/Watch_system" title="Watch system">watch</a> period, generally half the usual time (e.g. a two-hour watch rather than a four-hour one). Such watches might be included in order to rotate the system over different days for fairness, or to allow both watches to eat their meals at approximately normal times.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="doghouse" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Doghouse</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A slang term (in the US, mostly) for a raised portion of a ship's deck. A doghouse is usually added to improve headroom below or to shelter a hatch.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dogvane" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dogvane</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small <a href="/wiki/Weather_vane" title="Weather vane">weather vane</a>, sometimes improvised with a scrap of cloth, yarn or other light material mounted within sight of the helmsman. (See <i>tell-tale</i>)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="doldrums" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Doldrums" title="Doldrums">Doldrums</a> or equatorial calms</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The equatorial trough, with special reference to the light and variable nature of the winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMS_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMS-20">[20]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dolly_winch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dolly winch</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small winch mounted on the windlass which is used as an alternative to the brails winch when that is obstructed in some way. (stack, deck cargo, for example)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-37"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dolphin" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dolphin_(structure)" title="Dolphin (structure)">Dolphin</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A structure consisting of a number of piles driven into the seabed or riverbed as a marker.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="donkey_engine" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donkey_engine" title="Donkey engine">Donkey engine</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small auxiliary engine used either to start a larger engine or independently, <i>e.g.</i> for pumping water on steamships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="donkeyman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Donkeyman</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">One of a ship's engineering crew. Often a crewman responsible for maintaining a <a href="/wiki/Steam_donkey" title="Steam donkey">Steam donkey</a>, or any machinery other than the main engines. On some ships, the Petty Officer in charge of engineroom ratings.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dory" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dory" title="Dory">Dory</a> or doree, dori, or (Royal Navy) dorey <span id="Dorey"></span><span id="Dori"></span><span id="Doree"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A shallow-draft, lightweight boat, about 5 to 7 metres (16 to 23 feet) long, with high sides, a flat bottom and sharp bows. Traditionally used as fishing boats, both in coastal waters and in the open sea.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="double-shotted" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Double-shotted</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The practice of loading smooth-bore cannon with two cannon-balls.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dover_cliffs" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dover cliffs</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Very rough seas with large white capped waves.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="downbound" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downbound" title="Downbound">Downbound</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A vessel traveling downstream.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Eastward-traveling vessels in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> region (terminology as used by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Lawrence_Seaway_Development_Corporation" title="St. Lawrence Seaway Development Corporation">St. Lawrence Seaway Development Corporation</a>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="downhaul" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Downhaul" title="Downhaul">Downhaul</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A line used to control either a mobile <a href="/wiki/Spar_(sailing)" title="Spar (sailing)">spar</a>, or the shape of a sail. A downhaul can also be used to retrieve a sail back on deck.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="drabbler" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Drabbler</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An extra strip of canvas secured below a <i>bonnet</i> (q.v.), further to increase the area of a course</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="draft" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Draft_(hull)" title="Draft (hull)">Draft</a> or draught (both <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>) <span id="Draught"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The depth of a ship's keel below the waterline.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dragon_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dragon_boat" title="Dragon boat">Dragon boat</a> (also dragonboat)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">One of a family of traditional paddled long boats of various designs and sizes found throughout Asia, Africa and the Pacific Islands. For competitive events, they are generally rigged with decorative Chinese dragon heads and tails. Dragon boat races are traditionally held during the annual summer solstice festival.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dreadnought" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dreadnought" title="Dreadnought">Dreadnought</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of battleship designed with an "all-big-gun" armament layout in which the ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s primary gun power resided in a primary battery of its largest guns intended for use at long range, with other gun armament limited to small weapons intended for close-range defense against <a href="/wiki/Torpedo_boat" title="Torpedo boat">torpedo boats</a> and other small warships. Most, but not all, dreadnoughts also had steam turbine propulsion. Predominant from 1906, dreadnoughts differed from earlier steam battleships, retroactively dubbed "<a href="/wiki/Pre-dreadnought_battleship" title="Pre-dreadnought battleship">predreadnoughts</a>", which had only a few large guns, relied on an intermediate secondary battery used at shorter ranges for most of their offensive power, and had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triple-expansion_steam_engine" title="Triple-expansion steam engine">triple-expansion steam engines</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dress_overall" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dress_overall" title="Dress overall">Dress overall</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To string <a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Signals" title="International Code of Signals">International Code of Signals</a> flags, arranged at random, from <a href="/wiki/Stem_(ship)" title="Stem (ship)">stemhead</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mast_(ship)" title="Mast (ship)">masthead</a>, between mastheads (if the vessel has more than one mast), and then down to the <a href="/wiki/Taffrail" title="Taffrail">taffrail</a>, on a ship in harbor as a sign of celebration of a national, local, or personal anniversary, event, holiday, or occasion. When a ship is properly dressed overall, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ensign_(flag)" title="Ensign (flag)">ensigns</a> fly at each masthead unless displaced by another flag – for example, that of a <a href="/wiki/Flag_officer" title="Flag officer">flag officer</a> on board – in addition to the ensign flown in the usual position at the <a href="/wiki/Stern" title="Stern">stern</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dressing_down" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dressing down</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Treating old sails with oil or wax to renew them.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A verbal reprimand.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dressing_lines" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dressing lines</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Lines running from <a href="/wiki/Stem_(ship)" title="Stem (ship)">stemhead</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mast_(ship)" title="Mast (ship)">masthead</a>, between mastheads, and then down to the <a href="/wiki/Taffrail" title="Taffrail">taffrail</a>, to which flags are attached when a ship is <a href="/wiki/Dressing_overall" title="Dressing overall">dressed overall</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="drifter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Drifter_(fishing_boat)" title="Drifter (fishing boat)">Drifter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of fishing boat designed to catch <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a> in a long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drift_net" title="Drift net">drift net</a>, long used in the Netherlands and Great Britain.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="drink" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Drink</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Overboard and into the water, as it fell into the drink.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="driver" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Driver</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The large sail flown from the mizzen gaff.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="driver-mast" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Driver-mast</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The fifth mast of a six-masted barquentine or gaff schooner. It is preceded by the jigger mast and followed by the spanker mast. The sixth mast of the only seven-masted vessel, the gaff schooner <i><a href="/wiki/Thomas_W._Lawson_(ship)" title="Thomas W. Lawson (ship)">Thomas W. Lawson</a></i>, was normally called the pusher-mast.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="drogue" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Drogue" title="Drogue">Drogue</a> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span></span>/</a></span></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A device to slow a boat down in a storm so that it does not speed excessively down the slope of a wave and crash into the next one. It is generally constructed of heavy flexible material in the shape of a cone. See also <i>sea anchor</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="drudging" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Drudging&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Drudging (page does not exist)">Drudging</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A technique of maintaining steerage-way when going downstream with neither engine nor wind to sail. The vessel uses its anchor to draw itself head-to-stream, then lifts the anchor and drifts stern-first downstream, ferry gliding to maintain position within the stream. As steerage begins to reduce, the vessel anchors again and then repeats the whole procedure as required.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="drydock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drydock" title="Drydock">Drydock</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A narrow basin or vessel used for the construction, maintenance, and repair of ships, boats, and other watercraft that can be flooded to allow a load to be floated in, then drained to allow that load to come to rest on a dry platform.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dunnage" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dunnage <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/ʌ/: 'u' in 'cut'">ʌ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/dʒ/: 'j' in 'jam'">dʒ</span></span>/</a></span></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Loose packing material used to protect a ship's cargo from damage during transport. (See also <i>fardage</i>)</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Personal baggage.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="dunsel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dunsel</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A part on a ship that has no use.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="E">E</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: E">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="earings" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Earings</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Small lines, by which the uppermost corners of the largest sails are secured to the yardarms.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="east_indiaman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/East_Indiaman" title="East Indiaman">East Indiaman</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Any ship operating under charter or license to the East India Company (England), or to the <a href="/wiki/Danish_East_India_Company" title="Danish East India Company">Danish East India Company</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_East_India_Company" title="French East India Company">French East India Company</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch East India Company</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_East_India_Company" title="Portuguese East India Company">Portuguese East India Company</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Swedish_East_India_Company" title="Swedish East India Company">Swedish East India Company</a> from the 17th to the 19th centuries.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="echo_sounding" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Echo_sounding" title="Echo sounding">Echo sounding</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Measuring the depth of the water using a <a href="/wiki/Sonar" title="Sonar">sonar</a> device. See also <i>sounding</i> and <i>swinging the lead</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="embayed" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Embayed</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The condition where a sailing vessel (especially one which sails poorly to windward) is confined between two capes or headlands by a wind blowing directly onshore.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="en_echelon" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><i>En echelon</i></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An arrangement of gun turrets whereby the turret on one side of the ship is placed further aft than the one on the other side, so that both turrets can fire to either side.
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Minas_Geraes-class_battleships.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="884" data-file-width="1169" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Minas_Geraes-class_battleships.jpg/220px-Minas_Geraes-class_battleships.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Minas_Geraes-class_battleships.jpg/330px-Minas_Geraes-class_battleships.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Minas_Geraes-class_battleships.jpg/440px-Minas_Geraes-class_battleships.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Minas_Geraes-class_battleships.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diagram showing the <i>Minas Geraes</i>-class battleship with its central guns arranged <i>en echelon</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="engine_order_telegraph" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Engine_order_telegraph" title="Engine order telegraph">Engine order telegraph</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A communications device used by the pilot to order engineers in the engine room to power the vessel at a certain desired speed. Also <b>Chadburn</b>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="engine_room" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Engine_room" title="Engine room">Engine room</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">One of the machinery spaces of a vessel, usually the largest one, containing the ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s prime mover (usually a diesel or steam engine or a gas or steam turbine). Larger vessels may have more than one engine room.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ensign" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ensign</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  <i><a href="/wiki/Ensign" title="Ensign">Ensign</a></i>, the principal flag or banner flown by a ship to indicate her nationality.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  <i><a href="/wiki/Ensign_(rank)" title="Ensign (rank)">Ensign</a></i>, the lowest grade of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commissioned_officer" title="Commissioned officer">commissioned officer</a> in the US Navy.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="escort_carrier" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Escort_carrier" title="Escort carrier">Escort carrier</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a>, smaller and slower than a <a href="/wiki/Fleet_carrier" title="Fleet carrier">fleet carrier</a>, used by some navies in World War II to escort convoys, ferry aircraft, and provide air support for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphibious_operation" title="Amphibious operation">amphibious operations</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="extremis" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Extremis</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">(also known as "in extremis") the point under International Rules of the Road (<a href="/wiki/International_Regulations_for_Preventing_Collisions_at_Sea" title="International Regulations for Preventing Collisions at Sea">Navigation Rules</a>) at which the privileged (or stand-on) vessel on collision course with a burdened (or give-way) vessel determines it must maneuver to avoid a collision. Prior to extremis, the privileged vessel must maintain course and speed and the burdened vessel must maneuver to avoid collision.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="eye_splice" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Eye_splice" title="Eye splice">Eye splice</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A closed loop or eye at the end a line, rope, cable, etc. It is made by unraveling its end and joining it to itself by intertwining it into the lay of the line. Eye <a href="/wiki/Rope_splicing" title="Rope splicing">splices</a> are very strong and compact and are employed in moorings and docking lines among other uses.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="F">F</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: F">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="factory_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Factory_ship" title="Factory ship">Factory ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large ocean-going vessel with extensive on-board facilities for processing and freezing caught fish or whales. Some also serve as <i>mother ships</i> (q.v.) for smaller fishing or whaling vessels. Those used for processing fish are also known as <i>fish processing vessels</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fair" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fair</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A smooth curve, usually referring to a line of the hull which has no deviations.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To make something flush.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  A <i>line</i> is fair when it has a clear run.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  A wind or current is fair when it offers an advantage to a boat.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fair_winds_and_following_seas" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fair winds and following seas</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A blessing wishing the recipient a safe journey and good fortune.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fairlead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Fairlead" title="Fairlead">Fairlead</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ring, hook or other device used to keep a line or chain running in the correct direction or to prevent it rubbing or fouling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-38"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fairwater" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fairwater</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A structure that improves the streamlining of a vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fake" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fake</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To coil a rope down on the deak to enable it to pay out without fouling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-39"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="falku.C5.A1a" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Falku%C5%A1a" title="Falkuša">Falkuša</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A traditional fishing boat with a lateen sail on a single mast used by fishermen from the town of Komiža on the Adriatic island of Vis.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fall" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fall</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The part of the tackle that is hauled upon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-40"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fall_off" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fall off</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To change the direction of sail so as to point in a direction that is more down wind. To bring the bow leeward. Also bear away, bear off or head down. This is the opposite of pointing up or heading up.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fantail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fantail</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Aft end of the ship, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Poop_deck" title="Poop deck">Poop deck</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fardage" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fardage</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Wood placed in bottom of ship to keep cargo dry. (See also <i>dunnage</i>)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fashion_boards" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fashion boards</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Loose boards that slide in grooves to close off a companionway or cabin entrance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-41"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fast" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fast</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Fastened or held firmly (<i>fast aground</i>: stuck on the seabed; <i>made fast</i>: tied securely).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-42"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fathom" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Fathom" title="Fathom">Fathom</a> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="/ð/: 'th' in 'thy'">ð</span><span title="/əm/: 'm' in 'rhythm'">əm</span></span>/</a></span></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A unit of length equal to 6 feet (1.8 m), roughly measured as the distance between a man's outstretched hands. Particularly used to measure depth.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fathometer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fathometer" title="Fathometer">Fathometer</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A depth finder that uses sound waves to determine the depth of water.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="felucca" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Felucca" title="Felucca">Felucca</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A traditional wooden sailing boat with a rig consisting of one or two lateen sails, used in protected waters of the Red Sea and eastern Mediterranean and particularly along the Nile in Egypt and Sudan, and also in Iraq.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fend_off" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fend off</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A command given to the crew to stop what they are now doing and to immediately manually prevent the boat from banging into the docks or other boats.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fender" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Fender_(boating)" title="Fender (boating)">Fender</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A flexible bumper used in boating to keep boats from banging into docks or each other. Often an old car tyre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-43"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ferry_glide" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ferry glide</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  To hold a vessel against and at an angle to the current/stream such that the vessel moves sideways over the bottom due to the effect of the current operating on the upstream side of the vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fetch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fetch</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The distance across water which a wind or waves have traveled.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To reach a mark without tacking.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fid" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fid</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A tapered wooden tool used for separating the strands of rope for splicing.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A bar used to fix an upper mast in place.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fife_rail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Fife_rail" title="Fife rail">Fife rail</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A freestanding pinrail surrounding the base of a mast and used for securing that mast's sails' halyards with a series of belaying pins.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fifie" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Fifie" title="Fifie">Fifie</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sailing boat with two masts with a standard rig consisting of a main dipping <a href="/wiki/Lug_sail" title="Lug sail">lug sail</a> and a mizzen standing lug sail developed in Scotland; used for commercial fishing from the 1850s until the 20th century.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fig" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fig</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">US Navy slang for a guided-missile <a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">frigate</a>, especially of the <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Hazard_Perry-class_frigate" title="Oliver Hazard Perry-class frigate"><i>Oliver Hazard Perry</i> class</a>, derived from its class designation ("FFG").</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fighting_top" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fighting top</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An enlarged <a href="/wiki/Top_(sailing_ship)" title="Top (sailing ship)">top</a> designed to allow gunfire downward onto an enemy ship. A fighting top could have small guns installed in it or could serve as a platform for <a href="/wiki/Sniper" title="Sniper">snipers</a> armed with <a href="/wiki/Musket" title="Musket">muskets</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rifle" title="Rifle">rifles</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="figure_of_eight" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Figure of eight</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A stopper knot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-44"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="figurehead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Figurehead_(object)" title="Figurehead (object)">Figurehead</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A symbolic image at the head of a traditional sailing ship or early steamer.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fin" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fin</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A term used in European and British Commonwealth countries for a tower-like structure on the dorsal (topside) surface of a submarine; called a <i><a href="/wiki/Sail_(submarine)" title="Sail (submarine)">sail</a></i> in the United States.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fine" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fine</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Narrow (<i>fine</i>) in appearance from the vantage point of a lookout or other person viewing activity in the vicinity of a ship, e.g., another ship off the starboard bow with her bow or stern facing the viewer's ship could be described as "<i>fine</i> on the starboard bow" of the viewer's ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fire_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Fire_ship" title="Fire ship">Fire ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship loaded with flammable materials and explosives and sailed into an enemy port or fleet either already burning or ready to be set alight by its crew (who would then abandon it) in order to collide with and set fire to enemy ships.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fire_room" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Fire_room" title="Fire room">Fire room</a>, also <i>boiler room</i></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The compartment in which the ship's boilers or furnaces are stoked and fired.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="first-rate" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/First-rate" title="First-rate">First-rate</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The classification for the largest sailing warships of the 17th through 19th centuries. They had 3 masts, 850+ crew and 100+ guns.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="first_lieutenant" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/First_lieutenant" title="First lieutenant">First lieutenant</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  In the Royal Navy, the senior <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant" title="Lieutenant">lieutenant</a> on board; responsible to the <a href="/wiki/Commanding_officer" title="Commanding officer">commanding officer</a> for the domestic affairs of the ship's company. Also known as 'Jimmy the One' or 'Number One'. Removes his cap when visiting the mess decks as token of respect for the privacy of the crew in those quarters. Officer in charge of cables on the <a href="/wiki/Forecastle" title="Forecastle">forecastle</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In the US Navy, the officer on a ship serving as the senior person in charge of all deck hands.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="first_mate" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chief_mate" title="Chief mate">First mate</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The second-in-command of a commercial ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fish" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fish</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  To repair a mast or spar with a fillet of wood.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To secure an anchor on the side of the ship for sea (otherwise known as "catting".)</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  A slang term for a self-propelled <a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">torpedo</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fisherman.27s_reef" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fisherman's reef</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sailing tactic for handling winds too strong for the sail area hoisted when reefing the sails is not feasible or possible. The headsail is set normally while the mainsail is let out till it is constantly luffing. This creates loss of force on the main and also reduces the efficiency of the headsail while still retaining sailing control of the vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fitting-out" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Fitting-out" title="Fitting-out">Fitting-out</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The period after a ship is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ship_naming_and_launching" title="Ship naming and launching">launched</a> during which all the remaining construction of the ship is completed and she is readied for <a href="/wiki/Sea_trial" title="Sea trial">sea trials</a> and delivery to her owners.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fixed_propeller" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fixed <a href="/wiki/Propeller" title="Propeller">propeller</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A propeller mounted on a rigid shaft protruding from the hull of a vessel, usually driven by an inboard motor; steering must be done using a rudder. See also <i>outboard motor</i> and <i>sterndrive</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flag_hoist" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Flag hoist</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A number of signal flags strung together to convey a message, e.g., 'England expects...'</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flag_of_convenience" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Flag_of_convenience" title="Flag of convenience">Flag of convenience</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The business practice of registering a merchant ship in a sovereign state different from that of the ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s owners, and flying that state<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s civil ensign on the ship. The practice allows the ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s owner to reduce operating costs or avoid the regulations of the owner<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s country.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flag_officer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Flag_officer" title="Flag officer">Flag officer</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A commissioned officer senior enough to be entitled to fly a flag to mark the ship or installation from which he or she exercises command, in English-speaking countries usually referring to the senior officers of a navy, specifically those who hold any of the <a href="/wiki/Admiral" title="Admiral">admiral</a> ranks and in some cases to those holding the rank of <a href="/wiki/Commodore_(rank)" title="Commodore (rank)">commodore</a>. In modern American usage, additionally applied to US Coast Guard and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration_Commissioned_Corps" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration Commissioned Corps">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration Commissioned Corps</a> officers and general officers in the US Army, US Air Force, and US Marine Corps entitled to fly their own flags.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A formal rank in the mid-19th-century US Navy, conveyed temporarily upon senior captains in command of squadrons of ships, soon rendered obsolete by the creation of the ranks of <a href="/wiki/Commodore_(United_States)" title="Commodore (United States)">commodore</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rear_Admiral_(United_States)" title="Rear Admiral (United States)">rear admiral</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flagship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Flagship" title="Flagship">Flagship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A vessel used by the commanding officer of a group of naval ships. The term derives from the custom of the commander of such a group of ships, characteristically a <a href="/wiki/Flag_officer" title="Flag officer">flag officer</a>, flying a distinguishing flag aboard the ship on which he or she is embarked.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Used more loosely, the lead ship in a fleet of naval or commercial vessels, typically the first, largest, fastest, most heavily armed, or, in terms of media coverage, best-known.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flank" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Flank_speed" title="Flank speed">Flank</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The maximum speed of a ship. Faster than "full speed".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flare" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Flare</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A curvature of the topsides outward towards the gunwale.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A pyrotechnic signalling device, usually used to indicate distress.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flatback" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Flatback</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A Great Lakes slang term for a vessel without any self-unloading equipment.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flattop" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Flattop</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Slang term for an <i><a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a></i> (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flemish" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Flemish</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To coil a line that is not in use so that it lies flat on the deck.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flettner_rotor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Flettner rotor</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A spinning cylinder that uses the <a href="/wiki/Magnus_effect" title="Magnus effect">Magnus effect</a> to harness wind power to propel a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flight_deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Flight_deck" title="Flight deck">Flight deck</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A flat deck used for the launch and recovery of aircraft.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="floor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Floor</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The transverse structural timbers to which the longitudinal bottom planking is attached. The equivalent side timbers are called the frames. The keelson is fastened on top of the floors, bolting them to the keel. The planking is the exterior of the hull, while the ceiling is attached on top of the floors, and it forms the base of the hold. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-45"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flotilla_leader" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Flotilla_leader" title="Flotilla leader">Flotilla leader</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A warship suitable for commanding a flotilla of destroyers or other small warships, typically a small cruiser or a large destroyer, in the latter case known as a <i>destroyer leader</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flotsam" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flotsam_and_jetsam" title="Flotsam and jetsam">Flotsam</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Debris or cargo that remains afloat after a shipwreck. See also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flotsam_and_jetsam" title="Flotsam and jetsam">jetsam</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fluke" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Special:Search/fluke" title="wikt:Special:Search/fluke">Fluke</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The wedge-shaped part of an anchor's arms that digs into the bottom.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flush_deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Flush_deck" title="Flush deck">Flush deck</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An upper deck of a vessel that extends unbroken from stem to stern.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flush_decker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flush_decker" title="Flush decker">Flush decker</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A US Navy destroyer of the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>-era <a href="/wiki/Caldwell-class_destroyer" title="Caldwell-class destroyer"><i>Caldwell</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Wickes-class_destroyer" title="Wickes-class destroyer"><i>Wickes</i></a>, or <a href="/wiki/Clemson-class_destroyer" title="Clemson-class destroyer"><i>Clemson</i> class</a>, produced in very large numbers.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Any ship with a <a href="/wiki/Flush_deck" title="Flush deck">flush deck</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="flushing_board" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Flushing board</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Board inserted vertically in a cabin entrance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-46"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fluyt" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Fluyt" title="Fluyt">Fluyt</a> (also <i>fluit</i> or <i>flute</i>) <span id="Flute"></span><span id="Fluit"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A Dutch transoceanic sailing cargo vessel, square-rigged with two or three masts that were much taller than the masts of a <a href="/wiki/Galleon" title="Galleon">galleon</a>, developed in the 16th century and widely used in the 17th and 18th centuries.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fly_by_night" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fly-by-night" title="wikt:fly-by-night">Fly by night</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large sail used only for sailing downwind, requiring little attention.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="folding_propeller" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Folding_propeller" title="Folding propeller">Folding propeller</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A propeller with folding blades, furling to reduce drag on a sailing vessel when not in use.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="following_sea" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Following_sea" title="Following sea">Following sea</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Wave or tidal movement going in the same direction as a ship</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="foo-foo_band" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Foo-foo band</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An impromptu musical band on late 19th-century sailing vessels, made up from members of the ship's crew<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="foot" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leach_(sail)" title="Leach (sail)">Foot</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The lower edge of any sail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-47"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The bottom of a mast.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  A measurement of 12 inches.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="footloose" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Footloose</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">If the foot of a sail is not secured properly, it is footloose, blowing around in the wind.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="footboat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Footboat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A barges boat or dinghy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-48"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="footrope" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Footrope" title="Footrope">Footrope</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Each yard on a square rigged sailing ship is equipped with a footrope for sailors to stand on while setting or stowing the sails</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="force" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Force</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>Beaufort scale</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fore" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fore, forward (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/ɒr/: 'or' in 'moral'">ɒr</span><span title="/ər/: 'er' in 'letter'">ər</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>, and often written "for'ard") <span id="Forward"></span><span id="For.27ard"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Towards the bow (of the vessel).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fore-and-aft_rig" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Fore-and-aft_rig" title="Fore-and-aft rig">Fore-and-aft rig</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sailing rig consisting mainly of sails that are set along the line of the keel rather than perpendicular to it. Such sails are referred to as "fore-and-aft rigged."</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fore-and-afters" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fore-and-afters</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">removable wooded beams running along the centre of the hold openings, beneath the hatches that they support<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-49"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fore_horse" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Fore horse</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Transverse wooden or iron beam afore the main mast to which the foresail sheet is attached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-50"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="forecastle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Forecastle" title="Forecastle">Forecastle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A partial deck, above the upper deck and at the head of the vessel; traditionally the sailors' living quarters. Pronounced <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/əl/: 'le' in 'bottle'">əl</span></span>/</a></span></span>. The name is derived from the castle fitted to bear archers in time of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-51"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="forefoot" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Forefoot</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The lower part of the stem of a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="foremast_jack" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Foremast jack</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An enlisted sailor, one who is housed before the foremast.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="forepeak" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Forepeak</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The part of the hold of a ship within the angle of the bow.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="foresail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Foresail" title="Foresail">Foresail</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fore_and_aft_sail" title="Fore and aft sail">fore and aft sail</a> set on the foremast.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The lowest sail set on the foremast of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Full_rigged_ship" title="Full rigged ship">full rigged ship</a> or other vessel which is square-rigged.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="forestays" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Forestay" title="Forestay">Forestays</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Long lines or cables, reaching from the bow of the vessel to the mast heads, used to support the mast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-52"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="forestaysail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Forestaysail</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Triangular sail set on the forestay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-53"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup> <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/forestaysail" title="wikt:forestaysail">→ Forestaysail (Wiktionary)</a></i></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="foul" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Foul_(nautical)" title="Foul (nautical)">Foul</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Having freedom of motion interfered with by collision or entanglement; entangled; the opposite of clear. For instance, a rope is foul when it does not run straight or smoothly, and an anchor is foul when it is caught on an obstruction.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A breach of racing rules.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  An area of water treacherous to navigation due to many shallow obstructions such as reefs, sandbars, or many rocks, etc.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  <i>Foul the range</i>: To block another vessel from firing her guns at a target.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="foulies" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Foulies</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Slang for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oilskins" title="Oilskins">oilskins</a>, the foul-weather clothing worn by sailors. See also <i>oilskins</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="founder" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Founder</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To fill with water and sink <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/founder" title="wikt:founder">→ Founder (Wiktionary)</a></i></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="four_piper" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Four_piper" title="Four piper">Four piper</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A term sometimes used to refer to <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> four-funneled <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">destroyers</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bainbridge-class_destroyer" title="Bainbridge-class destroyer"><i>Bainbridge</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Paulding-class_destroyer" title="Paulding-class destroyer"><i>Paulding</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Wickes-class_destroyer" title="Wickes-class destroyer"><i>Wickes</i></a>, and <a href="/wiki/Clemson-class_destroyer" title="Clemson-class destroyer"><i>Clemson</i></a> classes, all built for service in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fourth_rate" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourth_rate" title="Fourth rate">Fourth rate</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In the British <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>, a fourth rate was, during the first half of the 18th century, a <a href="/wiki/Ship_of_the_line" title="Ship of the line">ship of the line</a> mounting from 46 up to 60 guns.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="frame" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Frame</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A transverse structural member which gives the hull strength and shape. Wooden frames may be sawn, bent or laminated into shape. Planking is then fastened to the frames. A bent frame is called a timber.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="freeboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freeboard_(ship)" title="Freeboard (ship)">Freeboard</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The height of a ship's hull (excluding superstructure) above the waterline. The vertical distance from the current waterline to the lowest point on the highest continuous watertight deck. This usually varies from one part to another.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="freighter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cargo_ship" title="Cargo ship">Freighter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Cargo_ship" title="Cargo ship">cargo ship</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="frigate" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">Frigate</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  In the 17th century, any warship built for speed and maneuverability.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In the 18th and early 19th centuries, a sailing warship with a single continuous gun deck, typically used for patrolling, blockading, etc., but not in line of battle.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  In the second half of the 19th century, a type of warship combining sail and steam propulsion, typically of ironclad timber construction, with all guns on one deck.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  In the 20th and 21st centuries, a warship, smaller than a destroyer, originally introduced during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> as an anti-submarine vessel but now general-purpose.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">5.  In the US Navy from the 1950s until the 1970s, a type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guided-missile" title="Guided-missile">guided-missile</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiaircraft" title="Antiaircraft">antiaircraft</a> ship built on a <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">destroyer</a>-sized hull, all reclassified as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guided-missile_cruiser" title="Guided-missile cruiser">guided-missile cruisers</a>" in 1975.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="full_and_by" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Full and by</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Sailing into the wind (<i>by</i>), but not as close-hauled as might be possible, so as to make sure the sails are kept <i>full</i>. This provides a margin for error to avoid being taken aback (a serious risk for square-rigged vessels) in a tricky sea. Figuratively it implies getting on with the job but in a steady, relaxed way, without undue urgency or strain.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="full-rigged_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Full-rigged_ship" title="Full-rigged ship">Full-rigged ship</a> <span id="Full-rigged"></span><span id="Full_rigged"></span><span id="Full_rigged_ship"></span><span id="Full_rigging"></span><span id="Fully-rigged"></span><span id="Fully_rigged"></span><span id="Fully-rigged_ship"></span><span id="Fully_rigged_ship"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sailing vessel with three or more masts, all of them square-rigged. A full-rigged ship is said to have a "ship rig".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="funnel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Funnel_(ship)" title="Funnel (ship)">Funnel</a> (also <i>stack</i>) <span id="Stack"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The smokestack of a ship, used to expel boiler steam and smoke or engine exhaust.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="furl" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Furl</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To roll or gather a sail against its mast or spar.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="fusta" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Fusta" title="Fusta">Fusta</a> (also <i>fuste</i>, <i>foist</i>, or <i>galliot</i>) <span id="Fuste"></span><span id="Foist"></span><span id="Galliot"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A narrow, light, and fast ship with shallow draft, powered by both oars and sail, with a single mast carrying a lateen sail; a favorite of North African corsairs during the 16th and 17th centuries.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="futtocks" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Futtocks</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Pieces of timber that make up a large transverse frame.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="G">G</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: G">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="gaff" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gaff</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  <a href="/wiki/Gaff_rig" title="Gaff rig">Gaff rig</a>: The spar that holds the upper edge of a four-sided fore-and-aft mounted sail.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  <a href="/wiki/Fishing_gaff" title="Fishing gaff">Fishing gaff</a>: A hook on a long pole to haul fish in.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gaff_rigged" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Gaff_rig" title="Gaff rig">Gaff rigged</a> <span id="Gaff_rig"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A boat rigged with a four-sided fore-and-aft sail with its upper edge supported by a spar or <i>gaff</i> which extends aft from the mast.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gaff_vang" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gaff vang</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A line rigged to the end of a gaff and used to adjust a gaff sail's trim.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="galleass" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Galleass" title="Galleass">Galleass</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  An oared warship of the 1500s equipped with a gundeck, larger and equipped with more sails than a <a href="/wiki/Galley" title="Galley">galley</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A flat-bottom commercial sailing vessel of the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> and western <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="galleon" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Galleon" title="Galleon">Galleon</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large, multi-decked sailing ship used primarily by European states from the 16th to 18th centuries.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="galley" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Galley</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  <a href="/wiki/Galley_(kitchen)" title="Galley (kitchen)">Galley</a>, the kitchen of a ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  <a href="/wiki/Galley" title="Galley">Galley</a>, a type of ship propelled by <a href="/wiki/Oar" title="Oar">oars</a> used especially in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> for warfare, piracy, and trade from the 700s BC to the 1500s AD, with some in use until the early 1800s.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  A type of oared <a href="/wiki/Gunboat" title="Gunboat">gunboat</a> built by the United States in the late 18th century, akin to a <a href="/wiki/Brigantine" title="Brigantine">brigantine</a> but termed "galley" for administrative and funding purposes.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="galliot" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Galliot</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>fusta</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gam" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gam</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A meeting of two (or more) whaling ships at sea. The ships each send out a boat to the other, and the two captains meet on one ship, while the two chief mates meet on the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gammon_iron" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gammon iron</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The bow fitting which clamps the bowsprit to the stem.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gangplank" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gangplank</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A movable bridge used in boarding or leaving a ship at a pier; also known as a "brow".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gangway" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gangway</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An opening in the bulwark of the ship to allow passengers to board or leave the ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="garbling" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Garbling</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The (illegal) practice of mixing cargo with garbage.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="garboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Garboard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The strake closest to the keel (from Dutch <i>gaarboard</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="garboard_planks" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Garboard planks</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The planks immediately either side of the keel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gash" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gash</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Any refuse or rubbish which is discarded into a refuse container or dustbin which is known as "gash fanny" (South African Navy).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gasket" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gasket</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A rope used to secure a sail (particularly the topsail) when stowed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-54"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gear" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gear</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessels sails and rigging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-55"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="general_quarters" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">General Quarters</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>Battle Stations</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gennaker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Gennaker" title="Gennaker">Gennaker</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large, lightweight sail used for sailing a fore-and-aft rig down or across the wind, intermediate between a genoa and a spinnaker.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="genoa" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Genoa_(sail)" title="Genoa (sail)">Genoa</a> or genny (both <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/dʒ/: 'j' in 'jam'">dʒ</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/iː/ or /ɪ/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>) <span id="Genny"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large <i>jib</i>, strongly overlapping the mainmast.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ghost" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ghost</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To sail slowly when there is apparently no wind.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gibe" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gibe</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>gybe</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gig" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Captain%27s_gig" title="Captain's gig">Gig</a> (<i>Captain<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s gig</i>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A boat on naval ships at the disposal of the ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s captain for his or her use in transportation to other ships or to the shore.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gin-pole" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gin-pole</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A pole that is attached perpendicular to the mast, to be used as a lever for raising the mast. Also jin-pole.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="give-way_.28vessel.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Give-way (vessel)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Where two vessels are approaching one another so as to involve a risk of collision, this is the vessel which is directed to keep out of the way of the other.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="glass" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Barometer" title="Barometer">Glass</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A marine barometer. (Older barometers used mercury-filled glass tubes to measure and indicate barometric pressure.)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="global_positioning_system" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">Global Positioning System</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">(GPS) A satellite based radionavigation system providing continuous worldwide coverage. It provides navigation, position, and timing information to air, marine, and land users.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="go-fast_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Go-fast_boat" title="Go-fast boat">Go-fast boat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">is a small, fast boat designed with a long narrow platform and a planing hull to enable it to reach high speeds – also called 'a rum-runner', or, more recently, 'a cigarette boat'.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="goat_locker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Goat_locker" title="Goat locker">Goat locker</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A mess hall reserved for <a href="/wiki/Chief_petty_officer" title="Chief petty officer">Chief petty officers</a> in the US Navy</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="going_about" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Going about or tacking</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Changing from one tack to another by going through the wind (see also <i>gybe</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gondola" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Gondola" title="Gondola">Gondola</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A traditional, flat-bottomed Venetian rowing boat.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gooseneck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gooseneck</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Fitting that attaches the boom to the mast, allowing it to move freely.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="goosewinged" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Goosewinged</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Of a fore-and-aft rigged vessel sailing directly away from the wind, with the sails set on opposite sides of the vessel – for example with the mainsail to port and the jib to starboard, to maximize the amount of canvas exposed to the wind. See also <i>running</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="grapeshot" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Grapeshot" title="Grapeshot">Grapeshot</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Small balls of lead fired from a <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannon</a>, analogous to <a href="/wiki/Shotgun" title="Shotgun">shotgun</a> shot but on a larger scale. Similar to <a href="/wiki/Canister_shot" title="Canister shot">canister shot</a> but with larger individual shot. Used to injure personnel and damage rigging more than to cause structural damage.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="grave" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Grave</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To clean a ship's bottom.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="graving_dock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graving_dock" title="Graving dock">Graving dock</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A narrow basin, usually made of earthen berms and concrete, closed by gates or by a <a href="/wiki/Caisson_(engineering)" title="Caisson (engineering)">caisson</a>, into which a vessel may be floated and the water pumped out, leaving the vessel supported on blocks; the classic form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drydock" title="Drydock">drydock</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="green-to-green" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Green-to-green</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A passage of two vessels moving in the opposite direction on their starboard sides, so called because the green navigation light on one of the vessels faces the green light on the other vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="great_circle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Great_circle" title="Great circle">Great circle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The line that follows the shortest distance between two points on the globe.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gripe" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gripe</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Temporary eye in a line (rope).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gripie" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gripie</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Cockney (London dialect) name for a barge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-56"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="grog" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Grog" title="Grog">Grog</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Watered-down pusser's rum consisting of half a gill with equal part of water, issued to all seamen over twenty. (CPOs and POs were issued with neat rum) From the British <a href="/wiki/Edward_Vernon" title="Edward Vernon">Admiral Vernon</a> who, in 1740, ordered the men's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rum_Ration" title="Rum Ration">ration of rum</a> to be watered down. He was called "Old Grogram" because he often wore a <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_textile_manufacturing" title="Glossary of textile manufacturing">grogram</a> coat, and the watered rum came to be called 'grog'. Often used (illegally) as currency in exchange for favours in quantities prescribed as 'sippers' and 'gulpers'. Additional issues of grog were made on the command '<a href="/wiki/Splice_the_mainbrace" title="Splice the mainbrace">splice the mainbrace</a>' for celebrations or as a reward for performing especially onerous duties. The RN discontinued the practice of issuing rum in 1970. A sailor might repay a colleague for a favour by giving him part or all of his grog ration, ranging from "sippers" (a small amount) via "gulpers" (a larger quantity) to "grounders" (the entire tot).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="groggy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Groggy</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Drunk from having consumed a lot of grog.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ground" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ground</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The bed of the sea.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="grounding" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Grounding</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When a ship (while afloat) touches the bed of the sea, or goes "aground" (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="growler" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Growler</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small iceberg or ice floe which is barely visible above the surface of the water.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="guard_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Guard_ship" title="Guard ship">Guard boat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A boat which makes the rounds of a fleet at anchor to see that due watch is kept at night.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="guard_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Guard_ship" title="Guard ship">Guard ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A warship stationed at a port or harbour to act as a guard there.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In former times in the British Royal Navy, a ship which received men impressed for naval service, often the flagship of the admiral commanding along the coast.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  In Soviet and Russian terminology, a guard ship (<i>storozhevoj korabl<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span></i>) is a small, general purpose patrol or escort vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gun_deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Gun_deck" title="Gun deck">Gun deck</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Up through the 19th century, a deck aboard a ship that was primarily used for the mounting of cannon to be fired in broadsides.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  On smaller vessels (of frigate size or smaller) up through the 19th century, the completely covered level under the upper deck, even though in such smaller ships it carried none of the ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s guns.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  On marine seismic survey vessels, the lowest deck on the ship, which carries the seismic source arrays, consisting of air guns arranged in clusters.</dd>
