<dd class="glossary">4.  In naval slang, to fabricate or falsify something; in modern usage, meaning especially to falsify documentation in order to avoid doing work or make present conditions seem acceptable without having made a real effort to improve them.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gunner.27s_daughter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gunner's daughter</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">see <i>Kissing the gunner's daughter</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gunport" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gunport</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The opening in the side of the ship or in a turret through which the gun fires or protrudes.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gunter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Gunter" title="Gunter">Gunter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A wire that leads from one point near the end of a gaff to a point near the other end; a block travels along the wire and a halyard is attached to the block, allowing the gaff to be raised to the vertical by a single halyard, though another halyard is required at the gaff jaws to control height.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Hoops or parrel beads which secure a gaff loosely to a mast in a vertical position; a halyard is bent to the gaff jaws to control height.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gunter_rig" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gunter rig</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel with a gunter-rigged mainsail.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gunter-rigged" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Gunter-rigged</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sail raised by means of a gunter.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gunwale" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Gunwale" title="Gunwale">Gunwale</a> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/ʌ/: 'u' in 'cut'">ʌ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/əl/: 'le' in 'bottle'">əl</span></span>/</a></span></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Upper edge of the hull. The top timber on the rail round the outer edge of the deck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-57"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="gybe" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jibe_(sailing)" title="Jibe (sailing)">Gybe</a> or jibe (both <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/dʒ/: 'j' in 'jam'">dʒ</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span></span>/</a></span></span>) <span id="Jibe"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To change from one tack to the other away from the wind, with the stern of the vessel turning through the wind. (See also <i>going about</i> and <i>wearing ship</i>.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-58"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="H">H</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: H">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="half-breadth_plan" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Half-breadth plan</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a>, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elevation_(view)" title="Elevation (view)">elevation</a> of the lines of a ship, viewed from above and divided lengthwise.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="halyard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Halyard" title="Halyard">Halyard</a> or halliard <span id="Halliard"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Originally, ropes used for hoisting a spar with a sail attached; today, a line used to raise the head of any sail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-59"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hammock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Hammock" title="Hammock">Hammock</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Canvas sheets, slung from the <a href="/wiki/Deckhead" title="Deckhead">deckhead</a> in <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Messdecks&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Messdecks (page does not exist)">messdecks</a>, in which seamen slept. "Lash up and stow" a piped command to tie up hammocks and stow them (typically) in racks inboard of the ship's side to protect crew from splinters from shot and provide a ready means of preventing flooding caused by damage.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hand" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hand</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To furl a sail.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hand_bomber" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hand bomber</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship using coal-fired boilers shoveled in by hand.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hand_over_fist" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hand over fist</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To climb steadily upwards, from the motion of a sailor climbing shrouds on a sailing ship (originally "hand over hand").</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="handsomely" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Handsomely</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">With a slow even motion, as when hauling on a line "handsomely".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="handy_billy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Handy billy</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A loose block and tackle with a hook or tail on each end, which can be used wherever it is needed. Usually made up of one single and one double block.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hangar_deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hangar deck</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An enclosed deck, usually beneath the <a href="/wiki/Flight_deck" title="Flight deck">flight deck</a>, on an aircraft carrier intended for use as a <a href="/wiki/Hangar" title="Hangar">hangar</a> in servicing and storing aircraft.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hank" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hank</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A fastener attached to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luff_(sail)" title="Luff (sail)">luff</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Headsail" title="Headsail">headsail</a> that attaches the <a href="/wiki/Headsail" title="Headsail">headsail</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Forestay" title="Forestay">forestay</a>. Typical designs include a bronze or plastic hook with a spring-operated gate, or a strip of cloth webbing with a snap fastener.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="harbour" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Harbor" title="Harbor">Harbour</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A harbour or harbor (US), or haven, is a place where ships may shelter from the weather or are stored. Harbours can be man-made or natural.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="harbor_of_refuge" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Harbor of refuge</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A place where ships in transit can find shelter from a storm. These are often man-made jetty enclosed areas along a featureless coastline where no nearby natural deep water harbors exist.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A section of otherwise muddy shoreline suitable for mooring or hauling out.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hard-a-lee" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hard-a-lee</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>lee-oh</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="harden_in" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Harden in</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Haul in the sheet and tighten the sails.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-60"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="harden_up" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Harden up</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Turn towards the wind; sail closer to the wind.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="harness_cask" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Harness cask</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large usually round tub lashed to a vessel's deck and containing dried and salted provisions for daily use.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="harness_tub" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Harness tub</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See "harness cask".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hardtack" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Hardtack" title="Hardtack">Hardtack</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A hard and long-lasting dry biscuit, used as food on long journeys. Also called <i>ship's biscuit</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hatchway" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hatchway, hatch <span id="Hatch"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A covered opening in a ship's deck through which cargo can be loaded or access made to a lower deck; the cover to the opening is called a hatch.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="haul" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Haul</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  To steer (a vessel) closer to the direction of the wind.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To shift forward, i.e., more toward the bow of the vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hauling_wind" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hauling wind</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Pointing the ship towards the direction of the wind; generally not the fastest point of travel on a sailing vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hawsepipe" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hawsepipe, hawsehole or hawse <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɔː/: 'au' in 'fraud'">ɔː</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span></span>/</a></span></span><span id="Hawsehole"></span><span id="Hawse"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The shaft or hole in the side of a vessel's bow through which the anchor chain passes.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hawsepiper" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Hawsepiper" title="Hawsepiper">Hawsepiper</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An informal term for a merchant ship's officer who began their career as an unlicensed merchant seaman, and so did not attend a traditional maritime academy to earn their officer's licence (see also <i>before the mast</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hawser" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Hawser" title="Hawser">Hawser</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Large rope used for mooring or towing a vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="head" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Head_(watercraft)" title="Head (watercraft)">Head</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The forwardmost or uppermost portion of the ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The forwardmost or uppermost portion of any individual part of the ship, e.g., the <i>masthead</i>, the <i>beakhead</i>, the <i>stemhead</i>, etc.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  The top edge of a sail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-61"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  The <a href="/wiki/Toilet" title="Toilet">toilet</a> or <a href="/wiki/Latrine" title="Latrine">latrine</a> of a vessel, which in sailing ships projected from the bows and therefore was located in the "head" of the vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="head_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Head boat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A fishing boat that takes recreational fishermen out for a fee paid individually by each person (i.e., per head). A <i>head boat</i> differs from a <i>charter boat</i>, which is a fishing boat that a party of fishermen hires for an agreed-upon period.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="head_of_navigation" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Head_of_navigation" title="Head of navigation">Head of navigation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The farthest point above the mouth of a river that can be navigated by ships.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="head_rail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Head rail</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A curved rail that extends from the <a href="/wiki/Figurehead_(object)" title="Figurehead (object)">figurehead</a> to the bow of a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="head_rope" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Head rope</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Part of the bolt rope, at the head of a sail, running from the mast to the sprit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-62"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="head_sea" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Head sea</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sea where waves are directly opposing the motion of the ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="header" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Header</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A change in the wind direction which forces the helmsman of a close hauled sailboat to steer away from its current course to a less favorable one. This is the opposite of a lift.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="heading" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Course_(navigation)#Course.2C_track.2C_route_and_heading" title="Course (navigation)">Heading</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The direction a thing's nose is pointing.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="headsail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Headsail" title="Headsail">Headsail</a> and Headsails.</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Any sail flown in front of the most forward <a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">mast</a>. Headsails the collective name for all sails afore the mast. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-63"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="headstick" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Headstick</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The spar laced to the head of the topsail. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-64"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="heave" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Heave</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel's transient, vertical, up-and-down motion.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="heave_down" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Heave down</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Turn a ship on its side (for cleaning). Also known as <a href="/wiki/Careening" title="Careening">Careening</a></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="heaving_to" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Heaving_to" title="Heaving to">Heaving to</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Or <i>Hove to</i>. Stopping a sailing vessel by lashing the helm in opposition to the sails. The vessel will gradually drift to leeward, the speed of the drift depending on the vessel's design.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="heel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heeling_(sailing)" title="Heeling (sailing)">Heel</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The lean caused by the wind's force on the sails of a sailing vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The inclination or canting of a vessel to one side or the other from the vertical as she maneuvers, as <i>The ship heeled to port as she turned to starboard</i>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  The lowest or last part of something, such as <i>the heel of the mast</i> or <i>the heel of the vessel</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="helm" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Helm</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship's steering mechanism; see <a href="/wiki/Tiller" title="Tiller">tiller</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ship%27s_wheel" title="Ship's wheel">ship's wheel</a>. The wheel and/or wheelhouse area. See also <i>wheelhouse</i>. Take the helm, means take over the steering of the vessel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-65"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="helmsman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Helmsman" title="Helmsman">Helmsman</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A person who steers a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="herring_buss" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Herring_buss" title="Herring buss">Herring buss</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of seagoing fishing vessel used by Dutch and Flemish herring fishermen from the 15th through the early 19th century.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="highfield_lever" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Highfield lever</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A particular type of tentioning lever, usually for running backstays. Their use allows the leeward backstay to be completely slackened so that the boom can be let fully out.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hitch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/List_of_hitch_knots" title="List of hitch knots">Hitch</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A knot used to tie a rope or line to a fixed object. See also <i>bend</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-66"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hog" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hog</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A fore-and-aft structural member of the hull fitted over the keel to provide a fixing for the garboard planks.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A rough flat scrubbing brush for cleaning a ship's bottom under water.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hogging" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Hogging_and_sagging" title="Hogging and sagging">Hogging</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When the peak of a wave is amidships, causing the hull to bend so the ends of the <a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">keel</a> are lower than the middle. The opposite of <i>sagging</i>. Also refers to a permanent distortion of the hull in the same manner caused, over time, by the bow and stern of a ship being less buoyant than the midships section. During the Age of Sail, shipwrights employed a number of different designs of braces to stiffen ships' hulls against this warping.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hoist" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hoist</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The height of a fore-and-aft sail as measured next to the mast or stay.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hold" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hold_(ship)" title="Hold (ship)">Hold</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In earlier use, below the orlop deck, the lower part of the interior of a ship's hull, especially when considered as storage space, as for cargo. In later merchant vessels it extended up through the decks to the underside of the weather deck.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="holiday" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Holiday</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A gap in the coverage of newly applied paint, slush, tar or other preservative.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="holystone" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Holystone" title="Holystone">Holystone</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A chunk of <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> used to scrub the decks. The name comes from both the kneeling position sailors adopt to scrub the deck (reminiscent of genuflection for prayer), and the stone itself (which resembled a Bible in shape and size).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="home_port" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Home_port" title="Home port">Home port</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The port at which a vessel is based. Often confused with the ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_of_registry" title="Port of registry">port of registry</a>, which is the port listed in the vessel<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s registration documents and lettered on her stern and which may differ from her home port. In the <a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">cruise ship</a> industry, the term "home port" often is mistakenly used to refer to a ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s port of departure.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="homeward_bounder" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Homeward bounder</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Deep sea expression for a sail repair, done with large herringbone stitches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-67"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hoop" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hoop</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Wooden or metal hoops used to secure the topsail to the topmast so it can be speedily raised or lowered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-68"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="horn" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Horn</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sound signal which uses electricity or compressed air to vibrate a disc diaphragm.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="horns" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Horns</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Shaped ends to the chocks where the main horse is bolted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-69"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="horn_timber" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Horn timber</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A fore-and-aft structural member of the hull sloping up and backwards from the keel to support the counter.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="horse" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Horse</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Sand lying mid-channel.<span id="horse"></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-70"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Prominent wooden or iron beams lying across the deck of a sailing barge taking the foresheet and mainsheet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-71"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  Attachment of sheets to deck of vessel (main-sheet horse).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  (v.) To move or adjust sail by brute hand force rather than using running rigging.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hounds" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hounds</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Attachments of stays to masts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-72"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hotel_load" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hotel load</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The <a href="/wiki/Electrical_load" title="Electrical load">electrical load</a> for all non-propulsion systems on a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hoy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hoy</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Barge making regular passages on a fixed route with mixed third-party cargoes. aka Passage barge, goods barge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-73"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hufflers" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Hufflers</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Men employed to help the barges crew along tortuous channels or through bridges. See shooting a bridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-74"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hulk" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hulk_(ship)" title="Hulk (ship)">Hulk</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A ship, often an old ship or one that has become obsolete or uneconomical to operate, that has had its rigging or internal equipment removed and is incapable of going to sea, but that is afloat and continues to serve a useful function, such as providing living, office, training, storage, or prison space.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To convert a ship into such a hulk.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  Less commonly, a ship that has been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ship_naming_and_launching" title="Ship naming and launching">launched</a> but not completed.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  Also less commonly, an abandoned wreck or shell of a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hull" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Hull_(watercraft)" title="Hull (watercraft)">Hull</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The shell and framework of the basic flotation-oriented part of a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hull-down" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Hull-down" title="Hull-down">Hull-down</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Of a vessel when only its upper parts are visible over the horizon.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hull_speed" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Hull_speed" title="Hull speed">Hull speed</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The maximum efficient speed of a displacement-hulled vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="hydrofoil" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Hydrofoil" title="Hydrofoil">Hydrofoil</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A boat with wing-like foils mounted on struts below the hull, lifting the hull entirely out of the water at speed and allowing water resistance to be greatly reduced.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="I">I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="icebreaker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">Icebreaker</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A special-purpose ship or boat designed to move and navigate through ice-covered waters.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="icing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Icing_(nautical)" title="Icing (nautical)">Icing</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A serious hazard where cold temperatures (below about −10°C) combined with high wind speed (typically force 8 or above on the <a href="/wiki/Beaufort_scale" title="Beaufort scale">Beaufort scale</a>) result in spray blown off the sea freezing immediately on contact with the ship</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="idlers" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Idlers</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Members of a ship's company not required to serve watches. These were in general specialist tradesmen such as the carpenter and the sailmaker.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="in_ballast_.28or_in_ballast_condition.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">In ballast (or "in ballast condition")</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"><i>Of a vessel</i>: Having only ballast (<i>q.v.</i>) – and no cargo – as a load.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="in_irons" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">In Irons/In stays</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When a sailing vessel has lost its forward momentum while heading into the wind, rendering it unable to steer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-75"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="in_ordinary" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/In_ordinary" title="In ordinary">In ordinary</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An 18th- and 19th-century term originally used to refer to a naval vessel out of service for repair or maintenance, later coming to mean naval ships in reserve with no more than a caretaker crew.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="in-water_survey" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-water_Survey" title="In-water Survey">In-water Survey</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A method of surveying the underwater parts of a <a href="/wiki/Ship" title="Ship">ship</a> while it is still afloat instead of having to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drydock" title="Drydock">drydock</a> it for examination of these areas as was conventionally done.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="in_way_of" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">In way of</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In the vicinity of; in the area of.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="inboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Inboard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Situated within a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Situated within a vessel and positioned close (or closer than another item, when contrasted with that item) to her centerline (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  Situated outside a vessel but nearer to her hull, e.g., <i>The larger boat was tied up alongside the ship inboard of the smaller boat.</i></dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  Nearer the pier or shore, e.g., <i>The tanker and cargo ship were tied up at the pier alongside one another with the tanker inboard of the cargo ship.</i></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="inboard_motor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Inboard_motor" title="Inboard motor">Inboard motor</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An engine mounted within the hull of a vessel, usually driving a fixed propeller by a shaft protruding through the stern. Generally used on larger vessels. See also <i>sterndrive</i> and <i>outboard motor</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="inboard-outboard_drive_system" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Inboard-Outboard drive system</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>sterndrive</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="inglefield_clip" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Inglefield_clip" title="Inglefield clip">Inglefield clip</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of clip for attaching a flag to a flag halyard.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="inshore" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Inshore</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Near (especially in sight of) or toward the shore.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Of a wind, blowing from the sea to the land.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="iron_mike" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Iron Mike</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A slang term for autopilot.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="iron_topsail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Iron topsail</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An auxiliary motor on a schooner.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="iron_wind" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Iron wind</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">What sailors call inboard engines.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ironclad" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ironclad_warship" title="Ironclad warship">Ironclad</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A steam-propelled warship protected by iron or steel armor plates of the period from 1859 until the 1890s (when the term "ironclad" fell out of use).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="island" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Island</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The <a href="/wiki/Superstructure" title="Superstructure">superstructure</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a> that extends above the flight deck. A carrier that lacks one is said to be <a href="/wiki/Flush_deck" title="Flush deck">flush-decked</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="J">J</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: J">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="jack" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Jack</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A sailor. Also <i>jack tar</i> or just <i>tar</i>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A flag. Typically the flag was talked about as if it were a member of the crew. Strictly speaking, a flag is only a "jack" if it is worn at the jackstaff at the bow of a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jackass-barque" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Jackass-barque" title="Jackass-barque">Jackass-barque</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Sometimes spelled jackass bark, is a <a href="/wiki/Sailing_ship" title="Sailing ship">sailing ship</a> with three (or more) masts, of which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foremast" title="Foremast">foremast</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Square-rigged" title="Square-rigged">square-rigged</a> and the main is partially square-rigged (<a href="/wiki/Topsail" title="Topsail">topsail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Topgallant_sail" title="Topgallant sail">topgallant</a>, etc.) and partially <a href="/wiki/Fore-and-aft_rig" title="Fore-and-aft rig">fore-and-aft rigged</a> (course). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mizzen_mast" title="Mizzen mast">mizzen mast</a> is fore-and-aft rigged.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jack_dusty" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Jack Dusty</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A naval stores clerk.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jack_tar" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Jack_Tar" title="Jack Tar">Jack Tar</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sailor dressed in 'square rig' with square collar. Formerly with a tarred pigtail.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jackline" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Jackline" title="Jackline">Jacklines</a> or jack stays <span id="Jacklines"></span><span id="Jackstays"></span><span id="Jack_stays"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Lines, often steel wire with a plastic jacket, from the bow to the stern on both port and starboard. A crewmember clips his safety harness to a jackline, allowing him to walk along the deck while still being safely attached to the vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jackstay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Jackstay</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Iron rod bolted clear of the mainmast, to which the luff of the mainsail is laced. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-76"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jenny" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Jenny</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>genoa</i></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jetty" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Jetty</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A man-made wall in open water rising several feet above high tide made of rubble and rocks used to create a breakwater, shelter, erosion control, a channel, or other such purpose.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jetsam" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flotsam_and_jetsam" title="Flotsam and jetsam">Jetsam</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Debris ejected from a ship that sinks or washes ashore. See also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flotsam" title="Flotsam">Flotsam</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jib" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Jib" title="Jib">Jib</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A triangular <a href="/wiki/Staysail" title="Staysail">staysail</a> at the front of a ship. More specifically, one mounted between bowsprit and the head if the mainmast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-77"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jibboom" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Jibboom</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A spar used to extend the bowsprit.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jibe" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Jibe</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>gybe</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jibe-ho" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Jibe-ho</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>gybe-oh</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jigger-mast" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Jigger-mast</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The fourth mast, although ships with four or more masts were uncommon, or the aft most mast where it is smallest on vessels of less than four masts.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jollies" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Jollies</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Traditional Royal Navy nickname for the Royal Marines.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jolly_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Jolly boat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">On a barge, the ship′s boat used to ferry crew and stores when the barge is moored off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-78"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="joggle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Joggle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">a slender triangular recess cut into the faying surface of a frame or steamed timber to fit over the land of <a href="/wiki/Clinker_(boat_building)" title="Clinker (boat building)">clinker</a> planking, or cut into the faying edge of a plank or rebate to avoid feather ends on a <a href="/wiki/Strake" title="Strake">strake</a> of planking. The feather end is cut off to produce a nib. The joggle and nib in this case is made wide enough to allow a <a href="/wiki/Caulking" title="Caulking">caulking</a> iron to enter the seam.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jonah" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Jonah#Jonah_in_sailors.27_superstition" title="Jonah">Jonah</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A person (either a sailor or a passenger) who carries a jinx, one whose presence on board brings bad luck and endangers the ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="junk" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Junk</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Old cordage past its useful service life as lines aboard ship. The strands of old junk were teased apart in the process called picking <a href="/wiki/Oakum" title="Oakum">oakum</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A sailing ship of classic Chinese design with characteristic full batten sails that span the masts usually on unstayed rigs.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="jury_rig" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jury_rig" title="Jury rig">Jury rig</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Both the act of rigging a temporary mast and sails and the name of the resulting rig. A jury rig would be built at sea when the original rig was damaged, then it would be used to sail to a harbor or other safe place for permanent repairs.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="K">K</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: K">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="kaep" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Kaep" title="Kaep">Kaep</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">a type of <a href="/wiki/Proa" title="Proa">Proa</a> native to <a href="/wiki/Palau" title="Palau">Palau</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="kedge" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kedge_anchor#Kedging" title="Kedge anchor">Kedge</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A technique for moving or turning a ship by using a relatively light anchor known as a kedge. The kedge anchor may be dropped while in motion to create a pivot and thus perform a sharp turn. The kedge anchor may also be carried away from the ship in a smaller boat, dropped, and then weighed, pulling the ship forward.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="keel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">Keel</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The central structural basis of the hull.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="keelhauling" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Keelhauling" title="Keelhauling">Keelhauling</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Maritime punishment: to punish by dragging under the keel of a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="kellet" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Kellet</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>anchor sentinel</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="kelson" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Kelson" title="Kelson">Keelson</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A baulk of timber or steel girder immediately above the keel that forms the backbone of a wooden ship. Chine keelson of more modest proportions are fitted at the junction of the floors and frames.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-79"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="kentledge" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Kentledge" title="Kentledge">Kentledge</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Weights (often scrap or <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a>) used as permanent high-density <a href="/wiki/Sailing_ballast" title="Sailing ballast">ballast</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ketch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ketch" title="Ketch">Ketch</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A two-masted fore-and-aft rigged sailboat with the aft mast (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mizzen" title="Mizzen">mizzen</a>) mounted (<a href="/wiki/Mast_Stepping" title="Mast Stepping">stepped</a>) afore (in front of) the rudder.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="killick" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Killick</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small <a href="/wiki/Anchor" title="Anchor">anchor</a>. A fouled killick is the substantive badge of non-commissioned officers in the RN. Seamen promoted to the first step in the promotion ladder are called 'Killick'. The badge signifies that here is an able seaman skilled to cope with the awkward job of dealing with a fouled anchor.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="kicking_strap" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Kicking strap</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A rope, tackle or hydraulic ram running from the mast at or just above deck level to a point part-way along the boom of a yacht's mainsail or mizzen. Its function is to pull the boom down, flattening the sail in strong winds, reducing twist and preventing the boom from kicking up when running.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A chain rigged from the rudder to quarter, tightened when anchored, it relieve the pressure on the gudgeon pin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-80"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="king_plank" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">King plank</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The centerline plank of a laid deck. Its sides are often recessed, or nibbed, to take the ends of their parallel curved deck planks.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="kingston_valve" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Kingston_valve" title="Kingston valve">Kingston valve</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A valve, which can be opened from the inside of the ship, that connects the sea to internal fuel, water, or ballast tanks (see also <i>seacock</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="kissing_the_gunner.27s_daughter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cat_o%27_nine_tails#Boys.27_punishment" title="Cat o' nine tails">Kissing the gunner's daughter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Bending over the barrel of a gun for punitive beating with a cane or cat.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="kitchen_rudder" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Kitchen_rudder" title="Kitchen rudder">Kitchen rudder</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Hinged cowling around a fixed propeller, allowing the drive to be directed to the side or forwards to manoeuvre the vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="knee" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Knee</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Connects two parts roughly at right angles, e.g. deck beams to frames.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A vertical rubber fender used on pushboats or piers, sometimes shaped like a human leg bent slightly at the knee</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="knighthead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Knighthead</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A mitred backing timber which extends the after line of the rabbet in the stem to give extra support to the ends of the planks and the bowsprit.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A bollard or bitt.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  Either of two timbers rising from the keel of a sailing ship and supporting the inner end of the bowsprit.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="knock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Knock</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>Header</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="knockdown" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Knockdown</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The condition of a sailboat being pushed abruptly to horizontal, with the mast parallel to the water surface.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="knot" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Knot_(unit)" title="Knot (unit)">Knot</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A unit of speed: 1 nautical mile (1.8520 km; 1.1508 mi) per hour. Originally speed was measured by paying out a line from the stern of a moving boat; the line had a knot every 47 feet 3 inches (14.40 m), and the number of knots passed out in 30 seconds gave the speed through the water in nautical miles per hour. Sometimes "knots" is mistakenly stated as "knots per hour," but the latter is a measure of acceleration (i.e., "nautical miles per hour per hour") rather than of speed.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="know_the_ropes" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Know the ropes</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sailor who 'knows the ropes' is familiar with the miles of cordage and ropes involved in running a ship.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="L">L</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: L">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="lace" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lace</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To attach a sail to a spar by passing a rope through eyelet holes and around the spar or its jackstay. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-81"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ladder" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ladder</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">On board a ship, all "stairs" are called ladders, except for literal staircases aboard passenger ships. Most "stairs" on a ship are narrow and nearly vertical, hence the name. Believed to be from the Anglo-Saxon word hiaeder, meaning ladder.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lagan" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lagan</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Debris that has sunk to the seabed.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="laid_up" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Laid up</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To be placed in <a href="/wiki/Reserve_fleet" title="Reserve fleet">reserve</a> or <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mothball" title="wikt:mothball">mothballed</a>. The latter usage is used in modern times and can refer to a specific set of procedures used by the US Navy to preserve ships in good condition.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="laker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Laker</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> slang for a vessel which spends all her time on the five Great Lakes.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="land_lubber" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Land lubber</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A person unfamiliar with being on the sea.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="landsman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Landsman_(rank)" title="Landsman (rank)">Landsman</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Military_rank" title="Military rank">military rank</a> for a naval recruit, used in the United Kingdom in the eighteenth century and and first half of the nineteenth century and the in the United States in the nineteenth century and in the early twentieth century.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lanyard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Lanyard" title="Lanyard">Lanyard</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A rope that ties something off.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="larboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_(nautical)" title="Port (nautical)">Larboard</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Obsolete term for the left side of a ship. Derived from "lay-board" providing access between a ship and a quay, when ships normally docked with the left side to the wharf. Replaced by <i>port side</i> or <i>port</i>, to avoid confusion with <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starboard" title="Starboard">starboard</a></i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="large" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Large</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>By and large</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lateen_sail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lateen_sail" title="Lateen sail">Lateen sail</a> or Latin-rig <span id="Latin-rig"></span><span id="Latin_rig"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A fore-and-aft triangular sail set on a long yard mounted at an angle to the mast.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lateral_system" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lateral system</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A system of aids to navigation in which characteristics of buoys and beacons indicate the sides of the channel or route relative to a conventional direction of buoyage (usually upstream).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="launch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Launch</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Traditionally, a <i><a href="/wiki/Launch_(boat)" title="Launch (boat)">launch</a></i> was the largest ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s boat carried by a warship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In modern usage, a large <a href="/wiki/Motorboat" title="Motorboat">motorboat</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ship_naming_and_launching" title="Ship naming and launching">To dispatch a ship</a> down a <a href="/wiki/Slipway" title="Slipway">slipway</a>, prior to <a href="/wiki/Fitting-out" title="Fitting-out">fitting-out</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ship_commissioning" title="Ship commissioning">commissioning</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lay</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To come and go, used in giving orders to the crew, such as "lay forward" or "lay aloft". To direct the course of vessel. Also, to twist the strands of a rope together. To make it to a mark, buoy, or harbor, such as "We will lay the mark".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lay_day" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lay day</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An unexpected delay time during a voyage often spent at anchor or in a harbor. It is usually caused by bad weather, equipment failure or needed maintenance.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="laying_down" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Laying down</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keel-laying" title="Keel-laying">Laying the keel</a> of a ship in a <a href="/wiki/Shipyard" title="Shipyard">shipyard</a> to begin her construction.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lazaret" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lazaret (also <i>Lazarette</i> or <i>Lazaretto</i>) <span id="Lazarette"></span><span id="Lazaretto"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A small stowage locker at the aft end of a boat.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A ship or building used for quarantine of sick patients.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  An area on some merchant ships where provisions are stored.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  In modern shipbuilding and on powerboats of all sizes, the location of the steering gear equipment for the vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lazy_jacks" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Lazy_jack" title="Lazy jack">Lazy jacks</a>, lazyjacks <span id="Lazyjacks"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A network of cordage rigged to a point on the mast and to a series of points on either side of the boom that cradles and guides the sail onto the boom when the sail is lowered.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="leach" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Leach</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The after edge of a sail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-82"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lead</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A plummet or mass of lead attached to a line, used in sounding depth at sea.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In former usage, to estimate velocity in knots.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="leadline" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Leadline (also <i>sounding line</i>) <span id="Sounding_line"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An instrument used in navigation to measure water depth; the line attached to a lead.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="leadsman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Leadsman</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sailor who takes soundings with a lead, measuring the depth of water.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="league" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/League_(unit)" title="League (unit)">League</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A unit of length, normally equal to three <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nautical miles</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lee_helm" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Lee_helm" title="Lee helm">Lee helm</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The tendency of a sailboat to turn to leeward in a strong wind when there is no change in the rudder's position. This is the opposite of weather helm and is the result of a dynamically unbalanced condition. See also <i>Center of lateral resistance</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lee_side" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lee side</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The side of a ship sheltered from the wind (cf. weather side).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lee_shore" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Lee_shore" title="Lee shore">Lee shore</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A shore downwind of a ship. A ship which cannot sail well to windward risks being blown onto a lee shore and grounded.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="leeboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Leeboard" title="Leeboard">Leeboard</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large fan shaped wooden board or fin mounted in pairs on the side of a boat. They can be lowered on the lee side of the ship to reduce leeway (similarly to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centerboard" title="Centerboard">centerboardon</a> a dinghy).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-83"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="leeboard_irons" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Leeboard irons</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The iron bars that run from the mainmast case to the head of each leeboard, which they support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-84"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="leeboard_pendant" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Leeboard pendant</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A wire connecting the fan of the leeboard, to a winch on the barges quarter. They control the fall of the leeboard. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-85"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="leech" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Leech</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The aft or trailing edge of a fore-and-aft sail; the leeward edge of a spinnaker; a vertical edge of a square sail. The leech is susceptible to twist, which is controlled by the boom vang, mainsheet and, if rigged with one, the gaff vang. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-86"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lee-ho" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lee-oh or hard-a-lee <span id="hard-a-lee"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The command given to come about (tack through the wind) on a sailing boat.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="leeward" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Windward_and_leeward" title="Windward and leeward">Leeward</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span><span title="/ər/: 'er' in 'letter'">ər</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span> in nautical use)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In the direction that the wind is blowing towards.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="leeway" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Leeway" title="Leeway">Leeway</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The amount that a ship is blown leeward by the wind. Also the amount of open free sailing space available to leeward before encountering hazards. See also <i>weatherly</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-87"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="leg" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Leg" title="Leg">Leg</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In navigation, a segment of a voyage between two waypoints.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="length_between_perpendiculars" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Length_between_perpendiculars" title="Length between perpendiculars">Length between perpendiculars</a>, also p/p, p.p., pp, LPP, LBP or Length BPP <span id="p.2Fp"></span><span id="p.p."></span><span id="pp"></span><span id="LPP"></span><span id="LBP"></span><span id="Length_BPP"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The length of a vessel along the <a href="/wiki/Waterline" title="Waterline">waterline</a> from the forward surface of the stem, or main bow perpendicular member, to the after surface of the sternpost, or main stern perpendicular member. Believed to give a reasonable idea of the vessel<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s carrying capacity, as it excludes the small, often unusable volume contained in her overhanging ends.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="length_overall" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Length_overall" title="Length overall">Length overall</a>, or LOA <span id="LOA"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The maximum length of a vessel<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s hull measured parallel to the waterline, usually measured on the hull alone, and including overhanging ends that extend beyond the main bow and main stern perpendicular members. For sailing vessels, this may exclude the bowsprit and other fittings added to the hull, but sometimes bowsprits are included.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="let_go_and_haul" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Let go and haul</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An order indicating that the ship is now on the desired course relative to the wind and that the sails should be trimmed ('hauled') to suit.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="letter_of_marque_and_reprisal" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Letter_of_marque" title="Letter of marque">Letter of marque and reprisal</a> or just Letter of marque <span id="Letter_of_marque"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A warrant granted to a <a href="/wiki/Privateer" title="Privateer">privateer</a> condoning specific acts of piracy against a target as a redress for grievances.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="liberty" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Liberty</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A relatively short period when a sailor is allowed ashore for recreation. See also <i>shore leave</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lie_to" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lie to</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To have the ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s sails arranged so as to counteract each other. A ship in this condition or in the process of achieving this condition is <i>lying to</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lifebelt" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Personal_flotation_device" title="Personal flotation device">Lifebelt</a>, lifebuoy, lifejacket, life preserver, personal flotation device <span id="Lifejacket"></span><span id="Life_preserver"></span><span id="Lifebuoy"></span><span id="Personal_flotation_device"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A device such as a buoyant ring or inflatable jacket which keeps a person afloat in the water.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lifeboat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lifeboat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  <a href="/wiki/Lifeboat_(shipboard)" title="Lifeboat (shipboard)">Shipboard lifeboat</a>, kept on board a vessel and used to take crew and passengers to safety in the event of the ship being abandoned.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  <a href="/wiki/Lifeboat_(rescue)" title="Lifeboat (rescue)">Rescue lifeboat</a>, usually launched from shore, used to rescue people from the water or from vessels in difficulty.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="liferaft" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Lifeboat_(shipboard)" title="Lifeboat (shipboard)">Liferaft</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An inflatable, covered raft, used in the event of a vessel being abandoned.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lift" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lift</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An enabling wind shift that allows a close hauled sailboat to point up from its current course to a more favorable one. This is the opposite of a header.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="light_irons" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Light irons</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Iron bars mounted near the main shrouds which support the navigation lights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-88"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="light_screens" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Light screens</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Boards on which the navigation lights are hooked, that shield the direction that the red or green light shows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-89"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lighter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Lighter_(barge)" title="Lighter (barge)">Lighter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A flat-bottomed barge used to transfer goods and passengers to and from moored ships, traditionally unpowered and moved and steered using "sweeps" (long <a href="/wiki/Oar" title="Oar">oars</a>), with their motive power provided by water currents.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lightering" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Lightering" title="Lightering">Lightering</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The process of transferring cargo from one vesel to another to reduce the draft of the first vessel. Done to allow a vessel to enter a port with limited depth or to help free a grounded vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lightvessel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Lightvessel" title="Lightvessel">Lightvessel</a> or lightship</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A permanently anchored vessel performing the functions of a lighthouse, typically in a location where construction of the latter is impractical. These have largely been replaced by buoys or, as construction techniques have improved, actual lighthouses.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="line" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Line</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The correct nautical term for the majority of the cordage or "ropes" used on a vessel. A line will always have a more specific name, such as <a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">mizzen</a> <a href="/wiki/Topsail" title="Topsail">topsail</a> <a href="/wiki/Halyard" title="Halyard">halyard</a>, that specifies its use.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="line_astern" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Line astern</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In naval warfare, a <a href="/wiki/Line_of_battle" title="Line of battle">line of battle</a> formed behind a flagship</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="liner" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Liner</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  During the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ship-of-the-line" title="Ship-of-the-line">ship-of-the-line</a>, a major warship capable of taking its place in the main battleline of fighting ships.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Any cargo or passenger ship running scheduled service along a specific route with published ports of call, excluding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferryboat" title="Ferryboat">ferries</a> and other vessels engaged in short-sea trading. When referring to cargo ships, <i>liner</i> in this sense contrasts with <i>tramp</i>, which refers to a ship engaged in spot-market trade that does not follow a regular schedule or make regular calls at specific ports. When referring to passenger ships, <i>liner</i> in this sense refers to ships providing scheduled transportation between regular ports of call and excludes <a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">cruise ships</a>, which voyage merely for recreational purposes and not primarily as a form of transportation between ports.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  <i><a href="/wiki/Ocean_liner" title="Ocean liner">Ocean liner</a></i>: Any large and prestigious passenger ship, including cruise ships.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="list" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Angle_of_list" title="Angle of list">List</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel's angle of lean or tilt to one side, in the direction called roll. Typically refers to a lean caused by flooding or improperly loaded or shifted cargo (as opposed to 'heeling', which see).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lizard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lizard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">short length of rope with an eye, used to hold another rope in position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-90"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="loaded_to_the_gunwales" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Loaded to the gunwales</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Literally, having cargo loaded as high as the ship's rail; also means extremely drunk.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lofting" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Lofting" title="Lofting">Lofting</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The technique used to convert a scaled drawing to full size used in boat construction.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="loggerhead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Loggerhead</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An iron ball attached to a long handle, used for driving caulking into seams and (occasionally) in a fight. Hence: 'at loggerheads'.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lolling" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Angle_of_loll" title="Angle of loll">Lolling</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An uncontrollable list caused by inadequate transverse stability in the upright condition.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="long_stay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Long stay</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The relative slackness of an anchor chain; this term means taut and extended.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="longboat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Longboat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  In the Age of Sail, a double-banked open boat carried by a sailing ship, rowed by eight or ten oarsmen, two per thwart, although designed also to be rigged for sailing; more seaworthy than a cutter or dinghy and with a beam greater than that of a gig. Eventually supplanted by the <a href="/wiki/Whaleboat" title="Whaleboat">whaleboat</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The largest, and thus the most capable, of boats carried on a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="longship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Longship</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of ship invented and used by the Vikings for trade, commerce, exploration, and warfare, evolving over several centuries and appearing in its complete form between the 9th and 13th centuries.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lookout" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lookout</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A member of the crew specifically assigned to watch surrounding waters for other vessels, land, objects in the water, hazards, threats, etc. Lookouts usually have duty stations high on a vessel's superstructure or in her rigging in order to enhance their field of view.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="loose_cannon" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Loose cannon</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An irresponsible and reckless individual whose behavior (either intended or unintended) endangers the group he or she belongs to. A loose cannon, weighing thousands of pounds, would crush anything and anyone in its path, and possibly even break a hole in the hull, thus endangering the seaworthiness of the whole ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="loose_footed" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Loose footed</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A mainsail that is not connected to a boom along its foot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-91"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lower_deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lower deck</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The deck of a ship immediately above the hold.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In British usage, those members of a ship's company who are not officers, often used in the plural (<i>the lower decks</i>)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lowers" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lowers</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The lower brails on the mainsail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-92"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lubber.27s_hole" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lubber's hole</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A port cut into the bottom of a <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Masthead_(sailing_ship)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Masthead (sailing ship) (page does not exist)">masthead</a> or <a href="/wiki/Top_(sailing_ship)" title="Top (sailing ship)">top</a> (crow's-nest) allowing easy entry and exit. It was considered "un-seamanlike" to use this method rather than going over the side from the shrouds, and few sailors would risk the scorn of their shipmates by doing so (at least if there were witnesses). In practice, it is actually quicker and easier for a fit sailor to climb outside the masthead rather than through the lubber's hole.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lubber.27s_line" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lubber's line</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vertical line inside a compass case indicating the direction of the ship's head.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="luff" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luff_(sail)" title="Luff (sail)">Luff</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The forward edge of a sail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-93"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Process of pointing a sailing vessel closer to the wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-94"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="luff_and_touch_her" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Luff and touch her</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To bring the vessel so close to wind that the sails shake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naval_encyclopedia_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Naval_encyclopedia-7">[7]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="luff_up" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Luff up</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To steer a sailing vessel more towards the direction of the wind until the pressure is eased on the [sheet].</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="luffing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Luffing" title="Luffing">Luffing</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  When a sailing vessel is steered far enough to <a href="/wiki/Windward_and_leeward" title="Windward and leeward">windward</a> that the sail is no longer completely filled with wind (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luff_(sail)" title="Luff (sail)">luff</a> of a fore-and-aft sail begins to flap first).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Loosening a <a href="/wiki/Sheet_(sailing)" title="Sheet (sailing)">sheet</a> so far past optimal trim that the sail is no longer completely filled with wind.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  The flapping of the sail(s) which results from having no wind in the sail at all.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lying_ahull" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Lying_ahull" title="Lying ahull">Lying ahull</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Waiting out a storm by dousing all sails and simply letting the boat drift.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lumber_hooker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Lumber_hooker" title="Lumber hooker">Lumber hooker</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> ship designed to carry her own deck load of lumber and to tow one or two barges. The barges were big old schooners stripped of their <a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">masts</a> and running gear to carry large cargoes of lumber.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lugger" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Lugger" title="Lugger">Lugger</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small sailing vessel with <a href="/wiki/Lug_sail" title="Lug sail">lug sails</a> set on two or more masts and perhaps lug topsails, widely used as traditional fishing boats, particularly off the coasts of France, England and Scotland.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lug_sail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Lug_sail" title="Lug sail">Lug sail</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A four-sided <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fore-and-aft" title="Fore-and-aft">fore-and-aft</a> sail supported by a spar along the top that is fixed to the mast at a point some distance from the center of the spar.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="lying_to" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Lying to</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>Lie to</i>.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="M">M</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: M">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
