<dt class="glossary" id="mae_west" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Personal_flotation_device" title="Personal flotation device">Mae West</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A Second World War <a href="/wiki/Personal_flotation_device" title="Personal flotation device">personal flotation device</a> used to keep people afloat in the water; named after the 1930s actress <a href="/wiki/Mae_West" title="Mae West">Mae West</a>, well known for her large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bosom" title="Bosom">bosom</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="magnetic_bearing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Magnetic_bearing" title="Magnetic bearing">Magnetic bearing</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An absolute bearing (<i>q.v.</i>) using magnetic north.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="magnetic_north" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Magnetic_declination" title="Magnetic declination">Magnetic north</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The direction towards the <a href="/wiki/North_Magnetic_Pole" title="North Magnetic Pole">North Magnetic Pole</a>. Varies slowly over time.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="main_deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Main_deck" title="Main deck">Main deck</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The uppermost continuous <a href="/wiki/Deck_(ship)" title="Deck (ship)">deck</a> extending from <a href="/wiki/Bow_(ship)" title="Bow (ship)">bow</a> to <a href="/wiki/Stern" title="Stern">stern</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mainbrace" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Mainbrace</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">One of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Braces_(sailing)" title="Braces (sailing)">braces</a> attached to the <a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">mainmast</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mainmast" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">Mainmast</a> (or Main) <span id="Main"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The tallest mast on a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mains" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Mains</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The main brails on the mainsail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-95"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mainsheet" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sheet_(sailing)" title="Sheet (sailing)">Mainsheet</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Sail control line that allows the most obvious effect on <a href="/wiki/Mainsail" title="Mainsail">mainsail</a> trim. Primarily used to control the angle of the boom, and thereby the mainsail, this control can also increase or decrease downward tension on the <a href="/wiki/Boom_(sailing)" title="Boom (sailing)">boom</a> while sailing upwind, significantly affecting sail shape. For more control over downward tension on the boom, use a <a href="/wiki/Boom_vang" title="Boom vang">boom vang</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mainstay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Mainstay</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The <a href="/wiki/Stays_(nautical)" title="Stays (nautical)">stay</a> running from the top of the mainmast to the bottom of the foremast, or from the top of the foremast to the ship's stem.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="making_way" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Making way</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When a vessel is moving under its own power.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="man-of-war" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Man-of-war" title="Man-of-war">Man-of-war</a> or man o' war <span id="Man_o.27_war"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">a <a href="/wiki/Warship" title="Warship">warship</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="man_overboard.21" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Man overboard!</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An emergency call that alerts the crew (q.v.) that someone aboard (q.v.) has gone overboard (q.v.) and must be rescued.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="man_the_rails" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manning_the_rails" title="Manning the rails">Man the rails</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To station the crew of a naval vessel along the rails and superstructure of the vessel as a method of saluting or rendering honors.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="man_the_yards" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man_the_yards" title="Man the yards">Man the yards</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To have all of the crew of a sailing vessel not required on deck to handle the ship go aloft and spread out along the <a href="/wiki/Yard_(sailing)" title="Yard (sailing)">yards</a>. Originally used in harbors to display the whole crew to the harbor authorities and the other ships present to show that the vessel<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s guns were not manned and hence her intentions were peaceful, <i>manning the yards</i> has since became a display used in harbor during celebrations and other special events.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="manifest" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Manifest_(transportation)" title="Manifest (transportation)">Manifest</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A document listing the cargo, passengers, and crew of a ship for the use of customs and other officials.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="marconi_rig" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Bermuda_rig" title="Bermuda rig">Marconi rig</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Another term for Bermudan rig. The mainsail is triangular, rigged fore-and-aft with the lead edge fixed to the mast. Refers to the similarity of the tall mast to a radio aerial.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="marina" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Marina" title="Marina">Marina</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">a docking facility for small ships and yachts.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="marine" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_(military)" title="Marine (military)">Marine</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A soldier trained for service afloat in a (primarily) infantry force that specializes in naval campaigns and subordinated to a navy or a separate naval branch of service rather than to an army. Often capitalized (e.g., "a <i>Marine</i>," or "the <i>Marines</i>"). Notable examples are the United Kingdom's <a href="/wiki/Royal_Marines" title="Royal Marines">Royal Marines</a>, formed as the Duke of York and Albany's Maritime Regiment of Foot in 1664 with many and varied duties including providing guard to ship's officers should there be mutiny aboard, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Marine_Corps" title="US Marine Corps">US Marine Corps</a>, formed in 1775 as a separate naval service alongside the US Navy. It is incorrect, and often viewed by marines as offensive, to refer to a marine as a "soldier" or "infantryman," as these terms refer to personnel of an army rather than those of a marine force. It also is incorrect, and sometimes considered offensive by both merchant mariners and marines, to refer to <i>merchant mariners</i> (<i>q.v.</i>) as "<i>merchant marines</i>," because merchant mariners are civilian sailors responsible for operating merchant ships and are not marines. Marines sometimes are thought by seamen to be rather gullible, hence the phrase "<i>tell it to the marines</i>," meaning that one does not believe what is being said.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  An alternative term for a <i>navy</i>, uncommon in English, but common in other languages.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  Of, or pertaining to, the sea (e.g, marine <i>biology</i>, marine <i>insurance</i>, marine <i>life</i>, marine <i>salvage</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  A painting representing a subject related to the sea.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mariner" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Mariner</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sailor.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="maritime" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Maritime</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Of or related to the sea (e.g., maritime <i>activities</i>, maritime <i>law</i>, maritime <i>strategy</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Bordering on the sea (e.g., maritime <i>provinces</i>, maritime <i>states</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  Living in or near the sea (e.g., maritime <i>animals</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  Of or relating to a mariner or sailor.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="marlinspike" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Marlinspike" title="Marlinspike">Marlinspike</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A tool used in <a href="/wiki/Ropework" title="Ropework">ropework</a> for tasks such as unlaying rope for splicing, untying knots, or forming a makeshift handle.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mast" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">Mast</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vertical pole on a ship which supports sails or rigging. If a wooden, multi-part mast, this term applies specifically to the lowest portion.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mast_case" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Mast case</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A yatchsmans tabernacle. The iron fitting in which the heel of the mast is mounted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-96"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mast_stepping" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Mast stepping</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The process of raising the mast.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="masthead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Masthead</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small platform partway up the mast, just above the height of the mast's main yard. A lookout is stationed here, and men who are working on the main yard will embark from here. See also <i>crow's nest</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="master" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Master</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_(nautical)" title="Captain (nautical)">captain</a> of a commercial vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A senior officer of a naval sailing ship in charge of routine seamanship and navigation but not in command during combat.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  <i><a href="/wiki/Master_(naval)" title="Master (naval)">Master</a></i>, a former naval rank.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="master-at-arms" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Master-at-arms" title="Master-at-arms">Master-at-arms</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Non-commissioned_officer" title="Non-commissioned officer">non-commissioned officer</a> responsible for discipline on a naval ship. Standing between the officers and the crew, commonly known in the Royal Navy as 'the Buffer'.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="matelot" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Matelot</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A traditional Royal Navy term for an ordinary sailor.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="material" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Material</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Military equipages of all descriptions for the naval services. The bombs, blankets, beans and bulletins of the Navy and Marine Corps. Taken from Nelson's British navy as the US services became professional. Related: <i>Materiel</i> – the military equipages of the Army and Air Force, taken from Napoleon's French army as the US services became professional.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="merchant_marine" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merchant_marine" title="Merchant marine">Merchant marine</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A collective term for all merchant ships registered in a given country and the civilians (especially those of that nationality) who man them; the ships and personnel in combination are said to constitute that country's <i>merchant marine</i>. Called the <i>merchant navy</i> in the United Kingdom and some other countries.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="merchant_mariner" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Merchant mariner</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A civilian officer or sailor who serves in the <i>merchant marine</i> (<i>q.v.</i>). Sometimes such personnel are incorrectly called "<i>merchant marines</i>," but both merchant mariners and marines frown on this term; although merchant mariners are part of the <i>merchant marine</i>, they are civilians and are not in any way <i>marines</i> (<i>q.v.</i>), which are a specialized type of military personnel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="merchant_navy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Merchant_navy" title="Merchant navy">Merchant navy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A name bestowed upon the <i>merchant marine</i> (<i>q.v.</i>) of the United Kingdom by King <a href="/wiki/George_V" title="George V">George V</a>, and since adopted by some other countries as well; the merchant navy's personnel are civilians, and the term "merchant navy" does not imply that they or their ships are a part of the navy. Synonymous with the term <i>merchant marine</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="merchantman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Merchantman</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Any non-naval passenger- or cargo-carrying vessel, including <a href="/wiki/Cargo_ship" title="Cargo ship">cargo ships</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanker_(ship)" title="Tanker (ship)">tankers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Passenger_ship" title="Passenger ship">passenger ships</a> but excluding <a href="/wiki/Troopship" title="Troopship">troopships</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mess" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Mess" title="Mess">Mess</a> or messdeck</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  An eating place aboard ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A group of crew who live and eat together.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mess_deck_catering" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Mess deck catering</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A system of catering in which a standard ration is issued to a mess supplemented by a money allowance which may be used by the mess to buy additional victuals from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ship%27s_pusser" title="Ship's pusser">pusser</a>'s stores or elsewhere. Each mess was autonomous and self-regulating. Seaman cooks, often members of the mess, prepared the meals and took them, in a tin canteen, to the <a href="/wiki/Galley" title="Galley">galley</a> to be cooked by the ship's cooks. As distinct from "<a href="/wiki/Cafeteria" title="Cafeteria">cafeteria</a> messing" where food is issued to the individual hand, which now the general practice.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="metacenter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metacenter" title="Metacenter">Metacenter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The midway point between a vessel<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_of_buoyancy" title="Center of buoyancy">center of buoyancy</a> when upright and her center of buoyancy when tilted.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="metacentric_height" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Metacentric_height" title="Metacentric height">Metacentric height</a> (also GM) <span id="GM"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A measurement of the initial static stability of a vessel afloat, calculated as the distance between her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centre_of_gravity" title="Centre of gravity">centre of gravity</a> and her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metacenter" title="Metacenter">metacenter</a>. A vessel with a large metacentric height rolls more quickly and therefore more uncomfortably for people on board; a vessel with a small metacentric height will roll sluggishly and may face a greater danger of capsizing.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="middles" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Middles</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The middle brails on the mainsail, higher than the lowers, and lower than the mains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-97"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="midships" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midships" title="Midships">Midships</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The middle section of a vessel with reference to the longitudinal plane, as distinguished from fore or aft. (Compare <i>Amidships</i>.)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="midshipman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Midshipman" title="Midshipman">Midshipman</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  During the 17th century, a naval rating for an experienced seaman.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  From the 18th century, a naval commissioned officer candidate.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  From the 1790s, an apprentice naval officer.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  From the 19th century, an officer cadet at a naval academy.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">5.  In contemporary British usage, a <a href="/wiki/Non-commissioned_officer" title="Non-commissioned officer">non-commissioned officer</a> below the rank of <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant" title="Lieutenant">lieutenant</a>. Usually regarded as being "in training" to some degree. Also known as 'Snotty'. 'The lowest form of rank in the Royal Navy' where he has authority over and responsibility for more junior ranks, yet, at the same time, relying on their experience and learning his trade from them.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">6.  In contemporary American usage, a cadet of either sex at the US Merchant Marine Academy or the US Naval Academy. When plural (<i>Midshipmen</i>), the term refers to the student body of either academy, and more formally as "the Regiment of <i>Midshipmen</i>" for the US Merchant Marine Academy and "the Brigade of <i>Midshipmen</i>" for the US Naval Academy. <i>Midshipmen</i> also is the name of the US Naval Academy's sports teams.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="midshipman.27s_hitch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Midshipman's hitch</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An alternative to the <a href="/wiki/Blackwall_hitch" title="Blackwall hitch">Blackwall hitch</a>, preferred if the rope is greasy. Made by first forming a Blackwall hitch and then taking the underneath part and placing over the bill of the hook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="midshipman.27s_nuts" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Midshipman's nuts</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Broken pieces of biscuit as dessert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smyth_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-smyth-27">[27]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="midshipman.27s_roll" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Midshipman's roll</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A slovenly method of rolling up a hammock transversely, and lashing it endways by one clue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smyth_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-smyth-27">[27]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mile" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Mile</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">see <i>nautical mile</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="military_mast" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Military mast</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Hollow tubular masts used in warships in the last third of the 19th century, often equipped with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fighting_top" title="Fighting top">fighting top</a> armed with light-caliber guns.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mine" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">Mine</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A self-contained explosive device intended to damage or sink surface ships or submarines, designed to be placed in water and left to wait until they are triggered by the approach of, proximity of, or contact with, a surface ship or submarines.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="minehunter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Minehunter" title="Minehunter">Minehunter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel designed or equipped to detect and destroy individual mines. It differs from a minesweeper (<i>q.v.</i>), which is designed or equipped to clear areas of water of mines without necessarily detecting them first.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="minelayer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Minelayer#Naval_minelayers" title="Minelayer">Minelayer</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel designed or equipped to deploy ("lay") mines.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="minesweeper" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minesweeper_(ship)" title="Minesweeper (ship)">Minesweeper</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel designed or equipped to clear areas of water of mines without necessaril detecting them first. It differs from a minehunter (<i>q.v.</i>), which is designed or equipped to detect and destroy individual mines.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mizzen" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Mizzen</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A mast on a <a href="/wiki/Ketch" title="Ketch">ketch</a> or <a href="/wiki/Yawl" title="Yawl">yawl</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thames_barge" title="Thames barge">spritsail barge</a> It can be sloop, gaff of spritsail rig, its posioing affore of abaft the steering post defines the difference between a yawl and a ketch. On a barge its rig determines if she is a muffie or a mulie.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-98"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A <a href="/wiki/Staysail" title="Staysail">staysail</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Ketch" title="Ketch">ketch</a> or <a href="/wiki/Yawl" title="Yawl">yawl</a>, usually lightweight, set from, and forward of, the mizzen mast while reaching in light to moderate air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-99"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mizzenmast" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">Mizzenmast</a> (or Mizzen) <span id="Mizzen"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The third mast, or mast aft of the mainmast, on a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mole" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Mole_(architecture)" title="Mole (architecture)">Mole</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A massive structure, usually of stone or concrete, used as a pier, a breakwater, or a causeway between places separated by water. May have a wooden structure built upon it and resemble a wooden <a href="/wiki/Pier" title="Pier">pier</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wharf" title="Wharf">wharf</a>, but a mole differs from a pier, quay, or <a href="/wiki/Wharf" title="Wharf">wharf</a> in that water cannot flow freely underneath it.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="monitor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monitor_(ship)" title="Monitor (ship)">Monitor</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A turreted ironclad warship of the second half of the 19th century characterized by low freeboard, shallow draft, poor seaworthiness, and heavy guns, intended for riverine and coastal operations.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In occasional 19th-century usage, any turreted warship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  A shallow-draft armored shore bombardment vessel of the first half of the 20th century, designed to provide fire support to ground troops, often mounting heavy guns.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  <a href="/wiki/Breastwork_monitor" title="Breastwork monitor">Breastwork monitor</a>: A 19th-century monitor designed with a breastwork to improve seaworthiness.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">5.  <a href="/wiki/River_monitor" title="River monitor">River monitor</a>: A monitor specifically designed for riverine operations, used during the 19th and 20th centuries and more recently than other types of monitor. River monitors generally are smaller and lighter than other monitors.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="monkey_bridge" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Monkey bridge</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A high platform above the wheelhouse offering better visibility to the operator while maneuvering.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="monkey.27s_fist" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Monkey%27s_fist" title="Monkey's fist">Monkey's fist</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">a ball woven out of line used to provide heft to heave the line to another location. The monkey fist and other heaving-line knots were sometimes weighted with lead (easily available in the form of foil used to seal e.g. tea chests from dampness) although <a href="/wiki/The_Ashley_Book_of_Knots" title="The Ashley Book of Knots">Clifford W. Ashley</a> notes that there was a "definite sporting limit" to the weight thus added.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="moor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Mooring_(watercraft)" title="Mooring (watercraft)">Moor</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  To attach a boat to a mooring buoy or post.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To dock a ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  To secure a vessel with a cable or anchor.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mooring" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Mooring_(watercraft)" title="Mooring (watercraft)">Mooring</a>, also <b>Moorings</b></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A place to moor a vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mother_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Mother_ship" title="Mother ship">Mother ship</a> (also <i>mothership</i> and <i>mother-ship</i>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel which leads, serves, or carries smaller vessels, in the latter case either releasing them and then proceeding independently or also recovering them after they have completed a mission or operation. A <i>mother ship</i> sometimes contrasts with a <i>tender</i> (q.v.), which often (but not necessarily) is a vessel that supports or cares for <i>larger</i> vessels.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mould" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Mould</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A template of the shape of the hull in transverse section. Several moulds are used to form a temporary framework around which a hull is built.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mould_loft" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Mould loft</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Where the lines of the ship are drawn out full-size and the templates for the timbers are made.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mousing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Mousing</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Several turns of light line around the mouth of a hook, to prevent unhooking accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-100"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="mulie" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Mulie</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A barge rigged with a spritsail main, and a large gaff rigged mizzen afore the steering wheel. It is sheeted to the saddle chock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-101"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="multipurpose_vessel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Multipurpose vessel</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A cargo ship that has fittings to carry standard shipping containers and retractable tweendecks that can be moved out of the way so that the ship can carry bulk cargo.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="muster_station" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Muster Station</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The location on a vessel a person goes either during an emergency or a drill to prepare for one. i.e. A <b>Muster Drill.</b> If a person is believed missing, all hands would report to their muster station for a head count.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="muzzle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Muzzle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Iron ban around the mast to hold the heel of the sprit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-102"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="m.v." style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">M.V. (or MV) <span id="MV"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Prefix for "<a href="/wiki/Motor_ship" title="Motor ship">Motor Vessel</a>", used before a ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s name.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="m.y." style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">M.Y. (or MY) <span id="MY"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Prefix for "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motor_yacht" title="Motor yacht">Motor yacht</a>", used before a yacht's name.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="N">N</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: N">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="narrows" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Narrows" title="Narrows">Narrows</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A narrow part of a navigable waterway.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="nautical_mile" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">Nautical mile</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A unit of length corresponding approximately to one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minute_of_arc" title="Minute of arc">minute of arc</a> of <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> along any <a href="/wiki/Meridian_arc" title="Meridian arc">meridian arc</a>. By international agreement it is exactly 1,852 metres (approximately 6,076 feet).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="naval_programme" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Naval Programme</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The British system of authorizing naval construction by an annual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bill_(proposed_law)" title="Bill (proposed law)">bill</a> in <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="naval_infantry" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Naval infantry</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Sailors subordinated to a navy trained and equipped to operate ashore temporarily as an organized infantry force, but at other times responsible for the normal duties of sailors aboard ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A specialized, permanent force of troops subordinated to a navy and responsible for infantry operations ashore. Although more specialized than sailors trained to operate temporarily as naval infantry and bearing similarities to a <i>marine</i> (<i>q.n.</i>) force or <i>marine corps</i>, such permanent naval infantry forces often lack the full capabilities of a marine force. Naval infantry forces also usually differ from marine forces in being subordinated directly to a navy rather than to a separate branch of naval service such as a marine corps.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="navigation_rules" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/International_Regulations_for_Preventing_Collisions_at_Sea" title="International Regulations for Preventing Collisions at Sea">Navigation rules</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Traffic#Rules_of_the_road" title="Traffic">Rules of the road</a> that provide guidance on how to avoid collision and also used to assign blame when a collision does occur.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="nay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Nay</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">"no"; the opposite of "aye".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="nipper" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Nipper</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Short rope used to bind a cable to the "messenger" (a moving line propelled by the capstan) so that the cable is dragged along too (used where the cable is too large to be wrapped round the capstan itself). During the raising of an anchor the nippers were attached and detached from the (endless) messenger by the ship's boys. Hence the term for small boys: "nippers".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="nock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Nock</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The throat of the mainsail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-103"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="no_room_to_swing_a_cat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">No room to swing a cat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The entire ship's company was expected to witness floggings, assembled on deck. If it was very crowded, the bosun might not have room to swing the "cat o' nine tails" (the whip).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="non-self-sustaining" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Non-self-sustaining</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"><i>See Self-sustaining</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="nun" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Nun</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of navigational buoy often cone shaped, but if not, always triangular in silhouette, colored green in <a href="/wiki/Lateral_mark#Region_A" title="Lateral mark">IALA region A</a> or red in <a href="/wiki/Lateral_mark#Region_B" title="Lateral mark">IALA region B</a> (the Americas, Japan, Korea and the Philippines). In channel marking its use is opposite that of a "can buoy".</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="O">O</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: O">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="oakum" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Oakum</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Material used for caulking hulls. Often hemp picked from old untwisted ropes.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ocean_liner" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ocean liner</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>Liner</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="offing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Offing" title="wikt:Offing">Offing</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">the more distant part of the sea as seen from the shore and generally beyond anchoring ground.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="offshore" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Offshore</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Moving away from the shore.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Of a wind, blowing from the land to the sea.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  At some distance from the shore; located in the sea away from the coast.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="oiler" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Oiler</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A naval auxiliary ship with fuel tanks and dry cargo holds designed to replenish other ships with fuel and supplies while underway on the high seas. <i>See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oiler_(ship)" title="Oiler (ship)">Oiler (ship)</a></i>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The job title of a seaman holding a junior position in a ship's engineering crew, senior only to the engine room <a href="/wiki/Wiper_(occupation)" title="Wiper (occupation)">wiper</a>. <i>See <a href="/wiki/Oiler_(occupation)" title="Oiler (occupation)">Oiler (occupation)</a></i></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="oilskin" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Oilskin" title="Oilskin">Oilskins</a> or oilies <span id="Oilies"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Foul-weather clothing worn by sailors.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="old_man.2C_.28the.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Old man, (The)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Crew<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s slang for the captain (master or commanding officer) of a vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="old_salt" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Old salt</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Slang for an experienced mariner.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="on_board_.28sometimes_onboard.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">On board (sometimes "onboard")</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"><i>See "aboard"</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="on_station" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">On station</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship's destination, typically an area to be patrolled or guarded.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="on_the_hard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">On the hard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A boat that has been hauled and is now sitting on dry land.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="open_registry" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_registry" title="Open registry">Open registry</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An organization that will register merchant ships owned by foreign entities, generally to provide a <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_convenience" title="Flag of convenience">flag of convenience</a> (q.v.).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ordinary" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ordinary</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>in ordinary</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="oreboat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Oreboat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Great Lakes term for a vessel primarily used in the transport of iron ore.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="orlop_deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Orlop_deck" title="Orlop deck">Orlop deck</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The lowest deck of a ship of the line. The deck covering in the hold.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="outboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Outboard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Situated outside the hull of a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Situated within a vessel but positioned away (or farther away, when contrasted with another item) from her centerline (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  Farther from the hull, e.g., <i>The larger boat was tied up alongside the ship outboard of the smaller boat.</i></dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  Farther from the pier or shore, e.g., <i>The tanker and cargo ship were tied up at the pier alongside one another with the tanker outboard of the cargo ship.</i></dd>
<dd class="glossary">5.  An outboard motor (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">6.  A vessel fitted with an outboard motor.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="outboard_motor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Outboard_motor" title="Outboard motor">Outboard motor</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A motor mounted externally on the transom of a small boat. The boat may be steered by twisting the whole motor, instead of or in addition to using a rudder.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="outdrive" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Outdrive</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The lower part of a sterndrive (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="outhaul" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Outhaul" title="Outhaul">Outhaul</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A line used to control the shape of a sail.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="outward_bound" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Outward bound</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To leave the safety of port, heading for the open ocean.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="over-canvassed" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Over-canvassed" title="Over-canvassed">Over-canvassed</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To have too great a sail area up to safely maneuver in the current wind conditions.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="over-reaching" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Over-reaching</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When tacking, holding a course too long.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="over_the_barrel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Barrel" title="Barrel">Over the barrel</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Adult sailors were flogged on the back or shoulders while tied to a grating, but boys were beaten instead on the posterior (often bared), with a cane or cat, while bending, often tied down, over the barrel of a gun, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kissing_the_gunner%27s_daughter" title="Kissing the gunner's daughter">Kissing the gunner's daughter</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="overbear" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Overbear</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To sail downwind directly at another ship, stealing the wind from its sails.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="overboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Overboard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Off or outside a vessel. If something or someone falls, jumps, or is thrown off of a vessel into the water, the object or person is said to have gone overboard. See "Man overboard!"</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="overfalls" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Overfalls</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Dangerously steep and breaking seas due to opposing currents and wind in a shallow area, or strong currents over a shallow rocky bottom.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="overhead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Overhead</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The ceiling of any enclosed space below decks in a vessel, essentially the bottom of the deck above you.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="overhaul" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Overhaul</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Hauling the buntline ropes over the sails to prevent them from chafing.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="overwhelmed" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Overwhelmed</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Capsized or foundered.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="owner" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Owner</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Traditional Royal Navy term for the Captain, a survival from the days when privately owned ships were often hired for naval service.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ox-eye" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ox-eye</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A cloud or other weather phenomenon that may be indicative of an upcoming storm.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="P">P</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: P">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="packet" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Packet, Packet boat, or packet ship <span id="Packet_boat"></span><span id="Packet_ship"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Originally, a vessel employed to carry post office mail packets to and from British embassies, colonies and outposts.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Later, any regularly scheduled ship, carrying passengers, as in <a href="/wiki/Packet_trade" title="Packet trade">packet trade</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="packet_trade" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Packet_trade" title="Packet trade">Packet trade</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Any regularly scheduled cargo, passenger and mail trade conducted by ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="packetman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Packetman</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A seaman aboard a ship engaged in <a href="/wiki/Packet_trade" title="Packet trade">packet trade</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="painter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Painter</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A rope attached to the bow of a dinghy, usually used to tow dingy or handle it at dockside, or in water.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="panting" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Panting_(ship_construction)" title="Panting (ship construction)">Panting</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The pulsation in and out of the bow and stern plating as the ship alternately rises and plunges deep into the water</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Paravane</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  <a href="/wiki/Paravane_(weapon)" title="Paravane (weapon)">Paravane (weapon)</a> - A device stabilized by vanes that functions as an underwater glider and which is streamed from (usually) the bow of a vessel and is towed alongside the vessel so that the cable attaching it to the vessel cuts the moorings of submerged mines.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  <a href="/wiki/Paravane_(water_kite)" title="Paravane (water kite)">Paravane (water kite)</a> - A towed underwater object with hydrofoils, of use in commercial and sport fishing, water sports, marine exploration, marine industry, and military operations, sometimes equipped with sensors and also of use in exerting a sideward holding force on a vessel. Also called a <i>water kite</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="parbuckle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Parbuckle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Parbuckle (page does not exist)">Parbuckle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A method of lifting a roughly cylindrical object such as a spar. One end of a rope is made fast above the object, a loop of rope is lowered and passed around the object, which can be raised by hauling on the free end of rope.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="parley" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Parley" title="Parley">Parley</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A discussion or conference, especially between enemies, over terms of a truce or other matters.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="parrel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Parrel_beads" title="Parrel beads">Parrel</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A movable loop or collar, used to fasten a yard or gaff to its respective mast. Parrel still allows the spar to be raised or lowered and swivel around the mast. Can be made of wire or rope and fitted with beads to reduce friction.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="part_brass_rags" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Part brass rags</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Fall out with a friend. From the days when cleaning materials were shared between sailors.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="passageway" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Passageway</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Hallway of a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="passenger-cargoman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Passenger-cargoman</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A merchant ship configured primarily for the transportation of cargo but also for the transportation of at least some passengers.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pawls" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Pawls</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Iron fingers that click into notches in the barrel on a winch to prevent roll-back.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-104"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pay_off" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Pay off</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To let a vessel's head fall off from the wind (to leeward.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-105"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="paying" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Paying</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Filling a seam (with caulking or pitch), lubricating the running rigging; paying with slush (q.v.), protecting from the weather by covering with slush. See also: <i>the devil to pay</i>. (French from <i>paix</i>, pitch)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="paymaster" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Paymaster</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The officer responsible for all money matters in RN ships including the paying and provisioning of the crew, all stores, tools and spare parts. See also: purser.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="peak" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Peak</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The upper aftermost corner of a fore-and-aft sail; used in many combinations, such as <i>peak-halyards</i>, <i>peak-brails</i>, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-106"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The narrow part of a vessel's bow, or the hold within it.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  The extremity of an anchor fluke; the bill.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="peaks" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Peaks</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The uppermost brails on the mainsail. Upper and lower peaks are normal, but a barge may carry a third set too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-107"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pelagic" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Pelagic</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Living in the open ocean rather than coastal or inland waters, (e.g., " a pelagic <i>shark</i>").</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Taking place in the open ocean (e.g., "pelagic <i>fishing</i>," "pelagic <i>sealing</i>").</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pendant" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Pendant</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A length of wire or rope secured at one end to a mast or spar and having a block or other fitting at the lower end. Often used incorrectly when referring to a Pennant (flag).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A length of wire or rope hooked to a tacckle on leeboards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-108"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pennant" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Pennant_(commissioning)" title="Pennant (commissioning)">Pennant</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A long, thin triangular flag flown from the masthead of a military ship (as opposed to a <a href="/wiki/Burgee" title="Burgee">burgee</a>, the flags thus flown on yachts).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="picket_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Picket_(military)" title="Picket (military)">Picket boat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A boat on sentry duty, or one placed on a line forward of a position to warn against an enemy advance.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pier" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Pier" title="Pier">Pier</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A raised structure, typically supported by widely spread piles or pillars, used industrially for loading and unloading commercial ships, receationally for walking and housing attractions at a seaside resort, or as a structure for use by boatless fishermen. The lighter structure of a pier contrasts with the more solid foundations of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quay" title="Quay">quay</a> or the closely spaced piles of a <a href="/wiki/Wharf" title="Wharf">wharf</a>. In North America, the term "pier" used alone connotes either a pier used (or formerly used) by commercial shipping or one used for fishing, while in Europe the term used alone connotes a recreational pier at a seaside resort.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pier-head_jump" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Pier-head jump</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When a sailor is drafted to a warship at the last minute, just before she sails.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pilot" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Maritime_pilot" title="Maritime pilot">Pilot</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Navigator. A specially knowledgeable person qualified to navigate a vessel through difficult waters, e.g. harbour pilot etc.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pilot_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Pilot_boat" title="Pilot boat">Pilot boat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of boat used to transport maritime pilots between land and the inbound or outbound ships that they are piloting.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pim" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">PIM</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Points (or plan) of intended movement. The charted course for a naval unit's movements.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pinnace" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Pinnace</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  <a href="/wiki/Pinnace_(ship%27s_boat)" title="Pinnace (ship's boat)">Pinnace (ship's boat)</a>, a small, light boat propelled by oars or a sail, used as a tender to larger vessels during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  <a href="/wiki/Full-rigged_pinnace" title="Full-rigged pinnace">Full-rigged pinnace</a>, a small "race built" galleon, squared rigged with either two or three masts.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  In modern usage, any small boat other than a <a href="/wiki/Launch_(boat)" title="Launch (boat)">launch</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lifeboat_(shipboard)" title="Lifeboat (shipboard)">lifeboat</a> associated with a larger vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pintle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Pintle" title="Pintle">Pintle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The pin or bolt on which a ship's rudder pivots. The pintle rests in the gudgeon.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pipe" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Pipe (Bos'n's), or a bos'n's call <span id="Bos.27n.27s_pipe"></span><span id="Bos.27n.27s_call"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A whistle used by <a href="/wiki/Boatswain" title="Boatswain">Boatswains</a> (bosuns or bos'ns) to issue commands. Consisting of a metal tube which directs the breath over an aperture on the top of a hollow ball to produce high pitched notes. The pitch of the notes can be changed by partly covering the aperture with the finger of the hand in which the pipe is held. The shape of the instrument is similar to that of a smoking pipe.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pipe_down" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Pipe down</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A signal on the bosun's pipe to signal the end of the day, requiring lights (and smoking pipes) to be extinguished and silence from the crew.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="piping_the_side" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Piping the side</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Salute" title="Salute">salute</a> on the bos'n's pipe(s) performed in the company of the deck watch on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starboard" title="Starboard">starboard</a> side of the <a href="/wiki/Deck_(ship)" title="Deck (ship)">quarterdeck</a> or at the head of the gangway, to welcome or bid farewell to the ship's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_(nautical)" title="Captain (nautical)">Captain</a>, senior officers and honoured visitors.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="piracy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">Piracy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An act of robbery or criminal violence at sea by the occupants of one vessel against the occupants of another vessel (thus excluding such acts committed by the crew or passengers of a vessel against others aboard the same vessel). Piracy is distinguished from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Privateering" title="Privateering">privateering</a>, which is authorized by national authorities and therefore a legitimate form of war-like activity by non-state actors.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pirate" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Pirate</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">One who engages in an act of <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">piracy</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pitch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ship_motions" title="Ship motions">Pitch</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel's motion, rotating about the beam/transverse axis, causing the fore and aft ends to rise and fall repetitively.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pitchpole" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Pitchpole</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To capsize a boat stern over bow, rather than by rolling over.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="plane" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planing_(sailing)" title="Planing (sailing)">Plane</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To skim over the water at high speed rather than push through it.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="plimsoll_line" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plimsoll_line" title="Plimsoll line">Plimsoll line</a> (also national Load Line) <span id="National_Load_Line"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A special marking, positioned amidships, that indicates the draft of the vessel and the legal limit to which the vessel may be loaded for specific water types and temperatures.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="plotting_room" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Plotting room</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">see <i>transmitting station</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="point" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Point</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A unit of bearing equal to one thirty-second of a circle, i.e., 11.25°. A turn of 32 points is a complete turn through 360°.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="point_up" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Point up</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To change the direction of a sailboat so that it is more up wind. To bring the bow windward. Also called heading up. This is the opposite of falling off.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="points_of_sail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Points_of_sail" title="Points of sail">Points of sail</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The course of a sailing vessel in relation to the direction of the wind, divided into six points: <i>in irons</i> (pointed directly into the wind), <i>close hauled</i> (sailing as close into the direction of the wind as possible), <i>close reach</i> (between close hauled and beam reach), <i>beam reach</i> (perpendicular to the wind), <i>broad reach</i> (wind behind the vessel at an angle), and <i>running downwind</i> or <i>running before the wind</i> (the wind is behind the vessel).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pontoon" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Pontoon_(boat)" title="Pontoon (boat)">Pontoon</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A flat-bottomed vessel used as a <a href="/wiki/Ferry" title="Ferry">ferry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barge" title="Barge">barge</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Car_float" title="Car float">car float</a>, or a <a href="/wiki/Lighter_(barge)" title="Lighter (barge)">float</a> moored alongside a <a href="/wiki/Jetty" title="Jetty">jetty</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Ship" title="Ship">ship</a> to facilitate boarding.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="poop_deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Poop_deck" title="Poop deck">Poop deck</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A high deck on the aft superstructure of a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="pooped" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Pooped</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Swamped by a high, following sea.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Exhausted.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="port" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_(nautical)" title="Port (nautical)">Port</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The left side of the boat. Towards the left-hand side of the ship facing forward (formerly Larboard). Denoted with a red light at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-109"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="port_of_registry" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_of_registry" title="Port of registry">Port of registry</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The port listed in a vessel<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s registration documents and lettered on her stern. Often used incorrectly as a synonym for "<a href="/wiki/Home_port" title="Home port">home port</a>", meaning the port at which the vessel is based, but which may differ from her port of registry.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="port_tack" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Port tack</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When sailing with the wind coming from the port side of the vessel. Must give way to boats on <i>starboard tack</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="porthole" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Porthole or port</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An opening in a ship's side, esp. a round one for admitting light and air, fitted with thick glass and, often, a hinged metal cover, a window</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="post-captain" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Post-captain</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An obsolete alternative form of the rank of captain in the Royal Navy; once achieved promotion thereafter was entirely due to seniority.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="powder_hulk" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Powder hulk</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hulk_(ship)" title="Hulk (ship)">hulk</a> used to store <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="powder_magazine" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Powder magazine</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small room/closet area in the hull of the ship used for storing <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> in barrels, or, "kegs", usually located centrally so as to have easy access to the grated loading area. Sometimes may be an enclosed closet with a door, so it can be locked and only the captain would have the key, similar to how rum is stored.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="predreadnought" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Pre-dreadnought_battleship" title="Pre-dreadnought battleship">Predreadnought</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Term used retrospectively after 1906 for a wide variety of steam battleships built between the 1880s and c. 1905 designed with only a few large guns for long-range fire, relying on an intermediate secondary battery used at shorter ranges for most of their offensive power, and having <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triple-expansion_steam_engine" title="Triple-expansion steam engine">triple-expansion steam engines</a>. They were rendered obsolete by the revolutionary <a href="/wiki/Dreadnought" title="Dreadnought">dreadnought</a> battleships which began to appear in 1906 and which differed from predreadnoughts in having <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a> propulsion and an "all-big-gun" armament layout in which the ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s primary gun power resided in a primary battery of its largest guns intended for use at long range, with other gun armament limited to small weapons intended for defense against <a href="/wiki/Torpedo_boat" title="Torpedo boat">torpedo boats</a> and other small warships.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="press_gang" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Impressment" title="Impressment">Press gang</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Formed body of personnel from a ship of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> (either a ship seeking personnel for its own crew or from a 'press tender' seeking men for a number of ships) that would identify and force (press) men, usually merchant sailors into service on naval ships usually against their will.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="preventer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Preventer (gybe preventer, jibe preventer)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sail control line originating at some point on the boom leading to a fixed point on the boat's deck or rail (usually a cleat or pad eye) used to prevent or moderate the effects of an accidental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jibe_(sailing)" title="Jibe (sailing)">jibe</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="principal_warfare_officer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Principal_Warfare_Officer" title="Principal Warfare Officer">Principal Warfare Officer</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PWO" title="PWO">PWO</a>, one of a number of Warfare branch specialist officers.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="prison_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Prison_ship" title="Prison ship">Prison ship</a> or prison hulk <span id="Prison_hulk"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel used as a prison, often to hold convicts awaiting transportation to penal colonies; particularly common in the British Empire in the 18th and 19th centuries.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="private_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Private_ship" title="Private ship">Private ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In British usage, a commissioned warship in active service that is not being used as the <a href="/wiki/Flagship" title="Flagship">flagship</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Flag_officer" title="Flag officer">flag officer</a>. The term does not imply in any way that the ship is privately owned.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="privateer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Privateer" title="Privateer">Privateer</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A privately owned ship authorised by a national power (by means of a <a href="#Letter_of_marque">#Letter of marque</a>) to conduct hostilities against an enemy. Also called a <b>private man of war</b>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="prize" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Prize</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A property captured at sea in virtue of the rights of war, as a vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="prize_crew" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Prize crew</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Members of a warship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s crew assigned to man a vessel taken as a prize.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="propeller_.28fixed.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Propeller (fixed)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A propeller mounted on a rigid shaft protruding from the hull of a vessel, usually driven by an inboard motor;</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="propeller_.28folding.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Propeller (folding)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A propeller with folding blades, furling to reduce drag on a sailing vessel when not in use.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="propeller_walk" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Propeller_walk" title="Propeller walk">Propeller walk</a> or prop walk <span id="Prop_walk"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">tendency for a propeller to push the stern sideways. In theory a right hand propeller in reverse will walk the stern to port.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="prow" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Prow" title="Prow">Prow</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">a poetical alternative term for bows.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="puddening" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Puddening</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Fibres of old rope packed between spars, or used as a fender.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="purchase" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Purchase</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A mechanical method of increasing force, such as a tackle or lever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-110"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="purser" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Purser" title="Purser">Purser</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The person who buys, stores and sells all stores on board ships, including victuals, rum and tobacco. Originally a private merchant, latterly a warrant officer.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Q">Q</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Q">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="quarterdeck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Quarterdeck" title="Quarterdeck">Quarterdeck</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The aftermost deck of a warship. In the age of sail, the quarterdeck was the preserve of the ship's officers.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="queen.27s_regulations" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">Queen's</a> (<a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">King's</a>) Regulations</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The standing orders governing the British <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> issued in the name of the current <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">Monarch</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="quay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Quay</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A stone or concrete structure on navigable water used for loading and unloading vessels, generally synonymous with a <a href="/wiki/Wharf" title="Wharf">wharf</a> (<i>q.v.</i>), although the solid foundations of a quay contrast with the closely spaced piles of a wharf. When "quay" and "wharf" are used as synonyms, the term "quay" is more common in everyday speech in the United Kingdom, many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Commonwealth" title="British Commonwealth">Commonwealth</a> countries, and the Republic of Ireland, while "wharf" is more commonly used in the United States.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To land or tie up at a quay.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="quayside" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Quayside</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  An area alongside a quay.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Having the attribute of being alongside a quay, e.g., "The ship is moored quayside."</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="R">R</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: R">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="rabbet" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Rabbet" title="Rabbet">Rabbet</a> or rebate <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span><span id="Rebate"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A groove cut in wood to form part of a joint.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="radar" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">Radar</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Acronym for <b>RA</b>dio <b>D</b>etection <b>A</b>nd <b>R</b>anging. An electronic system designed to transmit radio signals and receive reflected images of those signals from a "target" in order to determine the bearing and distance to the "target".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="radar_reflector" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Radar reflector</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A special fixture fitted to a vessel or incorporated into the design of certain aids to navigation to enhance their ability to reflect radar energy. In general, these fixtures will materially improve the visibility for use by vessels with radar.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rake" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rake</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To incline from the perpendicular; something so inclined is <i>raked</i> or <i>raking</i>, e.g., a <i>raked</i> or <i>raking</i> stem, stern, mast, funnel, etc.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ram" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Naval_ram" title="Naval ram">Ram</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A weapon consisting of an underwater prolongation of the bow of a vessel to form an armored beak, intended to be driven into the hull of an enemy vessel in order to puncture the hull and disable or sink that vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  An armored warship of the second half of the 19th century designed to use such a weapon as her primary means of attack.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  To intentionally collide with another vessel with the intention of damaging or sinking her.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  To accidentally collide bow-first with another vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="range_clock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Range_clock" title="Range clock">Range clock</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A clockwork device used aboard a warship to continuously calculate the range to an enemy ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="range_lights" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Range lights</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Two lights associated to form a range (a line formed by the extension of a line connecting two charted points) which often, but not necessarily, indicates the channel centerline. The front range light is the lower of the two, and nearer to the mariner using the range. The rear light is higher and further from the mariner.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rating" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Naval_rating" title="Naval rating">Rating</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  In British usage, an enlisted member of a country's navy, i.e., all members of the navy who are not officers or warrant officers.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In contemporary US Navy and US Coast Guard usage, the occupational specialty of an enlisted member of the service.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ratlines" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ratlines" title="Ratlines">Ratlines</a></dfn></dt>
<dt>(also "rattlins" or "ratlins")</dt>
<dd class="glossary">The rungs fastened between the shrouds permanently rigged from <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Special:Search/bulwark" title="wikt:Special:Search/bulwark">bulwarks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Top_(sailing_ship)" title="Top (sailing ship)">tops</a> to the mast to form ladders enabling access to the <a href="#Topmast">topmasts</a> and yards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-111"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="razee" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Razee" title="Razee">Razee</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A sailing ship that has been cut down to reduce the number of decks.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To cut down a sailing ship to reduce the number of decks.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="reaching" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Points_of_sail" title="Points of sail">Reaching</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Sailing across the wind: from about 60° to about 160° off the wind. Reaching consists of "close reaching" (about 60° to 80°), "beam reaching" (about 90°) and "broad reaching" (about 120° to 160°). See also <i>beating</i> and <i>running</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="reaching_sail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Reaching Sail</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A specifically designed sail for tighter reaching legs. Reaching sails are often used in racing with a true wind angle of 35 to 95 degrees. They are generally used before the wind angle moves aft enough to permit spinnakers to be flown.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ready_about" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ready about</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A call to indicate imminent tacking (see <i>going about</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-112"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="receiver_of_wreck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Receiver_of_Wreck" title="Receiver of Wreck">Receiver of Wreck</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A government official whose duty is to give owners of shipwrecks the opportunity to retrieve their property and ensure that law-abiding finders of wreck receive an appropriate reward.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="receiving_hulk" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Receiving hulk or receiving ship <span id="Receiving_ship"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hulk_(ship)" title="Hulk (ship)">hulk</a> used in harbor to house newly recruited sailors before they are assigned to a crew.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="red_duster" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Red Duster</dfn></dt>
