<dd class="glossary">Traditional nickname for the <a href="/wiki/Red_Ensign" title="Red Ensign">Red Ensign</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Civil_ensign" title="Civil ensign">civil ensign</a> (flag) carried by <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> civilian vessels.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="red-to-red" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Red-to-red</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A passage of two vessels moving in the opposite direction on their port sides, so called because the red navigation light on one of the vessels faces the red light on the other vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="reduced_cat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Reduced cat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A light version on the cat o'nine tails for use on boys; also called "boys' pussy".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="reef" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Reef</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  <a href="/wiki/Reefing" title="Reefing">Reefing</a>: To temporarily reduce the area of a sail exposed to the wind, usually to guard against adverse effects of strong wind or to slow the vessel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-113"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  <a href="/wiki/Reef" title="Reef">Reef</a>: Rock or coral, possibly only revealed at low tide, shallow enough that the vessel will at least touch if not go aground.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="reef-points" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Reef-points</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Small flat lengths of braided cord attached by eyelets to a sail along the reef band, used to secure the excess fabric after reefing. Typically, a reef point consists of two lengths of cord which taper towards their ends – the narrow end of each is threaded through an eye in the wide end of the other and then the pair are rove through the eyelet in the reef band such that one length hangs before and the other abaft the sail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="reef-bands" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Reef-bands</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Long pieces of rough canvas sewed across the sails to give them additional strength.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="reef-tackles" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Reef-tackles</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Ropes employed in the operation of reefing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184824_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184824-29">[29]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="reefer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Reefer</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A shipboard refrigerator.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A <a href="/wiki/Reefer_ship" title="Reefer ship">refrigerated cargo ship</a>, used to carry perishable goods that require refrigeration (also <i>reefer ship</i>)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="reeve" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Reeve</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">(Past tense rove) To thread a line through blocks in order to gain a mechanical advantage, such as in a block and tackle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184824_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184824-29">[29]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="regatta" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Regatta" title="Regatta">Regatta</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A series of boat races, usually of sailboats or rowboats, but occasionally of powered boats.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="relative_bearing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Relative_bearing" title="Relative bearing">Relative bearing</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A bearing relative to the direction of the ship: the clockwise angle between the ship's direction and an object. See also <i>absolute bearing</i> and <i>bearing</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="research_vessel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Research_vessel" title="Research vessel">Research vessel</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship designed and equipped to carry out research at sea, especially <a href="/wiki/Hydrographic_survey" title="Hydrographic survey">hydrographic survey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanographic</a> research, <a href="/wiki/Fishery" title="Fishery">fisheries</a> research, <a href="/wiki/Navy" title="Navy">naval</a> research, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">polar</a> research, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_exploration" title="Oil exploration">oil exploration</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rib_tickler" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rib tickler</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A bargeman's name for the tiller.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-114"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="riding_light" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Riding light</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A light hung from the forestay when at anchor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-115"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rigging" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Rigging" title="Rigging">Rigging</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The system of masts and lines on ships and other sailing vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184824_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184824-29">[29]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rigging_chocks" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rigging chocks</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Thick blocks of wood fixed outside the rails to take the chain plates for the shrouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-116"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rigging_screw" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rigging screw</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A bottle screw used to keep wires taut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-117"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="righting_couple" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Righting couple</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The force which tends to restore a ship to equilibrium once a heel has altered the relationship between her centre of buoyancy and her centre of gravity.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rigol" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rigol</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The rim or 'eyebrow' above a port-hole or scuttle.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rip_rap" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rip rap</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A man-made pile of rocks and rubble often surrounding an off-shore lighthouse or as a base for an aid to navigation.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="roads" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Roads</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>roadstead</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="roadstead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Roadstead" title="Roadstead">Roadstead</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sheltered area outside a harbour where a ship can lie safe at anchor, also known as a <i>roads</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rode" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rode</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The anchor line, rope or cable connecting the anchor chain to the vessel. Also Anchor Rode.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rogue_wave" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Rogue_wave" title="Rogue wave">Rogue wave</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A surprisingly large wave for a given sea state; formally, a wave whose height is more than twice the <a href="/wiki/Significant_wave_height" title="Significant wave height">significant wave height</a> (i.e., the mean of the largest third of waves in a wave record).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="roll" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ship_motions" title="Ship motions">Roll</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel's motion rotating from side to side, about the fore-aft/longitudinal axis. Listing is a lasting, stable tilt, or heel, along the longitudinal axis. Roll is also an alternate name for the longitudinal axis (roll axis).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="roll-on.2Froll-off_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Roll-on/roll-off" title="Roll-on/roll-off">Roll-on/roll-off ship</a> (also <i>RORO</i> or <i>ro-ro</i>) <span id="RORO"></span><span id="Ro-ro"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel designed to carry wheeled cargo that can drive on and off the ship on its own wheels.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rolling-tackle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rolling-tackle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A number of pulleys, engaged to confine the yard to the weather side of the mast; this tackle is much used in a rough sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184825_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184825-30">[30]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rolling_vang" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rolling vang</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A second set of sprit-head vangs played out forward to rail near the bows, used to give additional control and support when needed in a seaway. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-118"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="romper" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Romper</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In a convoy, a ship that breaks ranks and "romps" ahead.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ropes.2C_the" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ropes, the</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  All cordage, the lines in the rigging.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ropes.2C_the" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ropes, the</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Cordage of over 1 inches in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184825_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184825-30">[30]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rope.27s_end" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rope's end</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A summary punishment device.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rope_yarn" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rope yarn</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A period, traditionally on Wednesday afternoons, when a tailor boarded a sailing warship while the vessel was in port; the crew was excused from most duties and had light duty mending uniforms and hammocks and darning socks. When the ship was at sea, the crew similarly was excused from most duties on Wednesday afternoons to engage in mending chores. Wednesday afternoons, like Sundays, thus were a more social time which allowed crewmen a rest from their normal duties, similar to a Sunday, and, because the crew used <i>rope yarn</i> for mending, Wednesday afternoon became known as <i>rope yarn Sunday</i>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  After uniforms began to require less care, and through the mid-20th century, a period on Wednesday afternoon when naval crew members were excused from their regular duties to run personal errands.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  Since the mid-20th century, any period of free time when a naval crew is given early liberty or otherwise excused from its normally scheduled duties.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  One of threads of which a rope is composed. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184825_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184825-30">[30]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="roro_or_ro-ro" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">RORO or ro-ro</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>roll-on/roll-off ship</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="round_to" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Round to</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To turn the bow of a vessel into the wind.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rove" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rove</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">see <i>reeve</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184824_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184824-29">[29]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rowlock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Rowlock" title="Rowlock">Rowlock</a> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A bracket providing the fulcrum for an <a href="/wiki/Oar" title="Oar">oar</a>. See also <i>thole</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="royal" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Royal</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  In large sailing ships, a mast right above the topgallant mast.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The sail of such a mast.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rubbing_strake" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rubbing strake</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An extra plank fitted to the outside of the hull, usually at deck level, to protect the topsides.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rudder" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">Rudder</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A steering device which can be placed aft, externally relative to the keel or compounded into the keel either independently or as part of the bulb/centerboard.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ruffle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ruffle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Part of the anchor winch. This is a serrated iron ring attached to the barrel, to which the pawl is applied to prevent backruns of the anchor chain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-119"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rum-runner" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rum-runner</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">see <i>go-fast boat</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rummage_.28obsolete_spelling_romage.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rummage (obsolete spelling "romage")</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A place or room for the stowage of cargo in a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The act of stowing cargo aboard a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  To arrange (cargo, goods, etc.) in the hold of a vessel; to move or rearrange such goods; the pulling and moving about of packages incident to close stowage aboard a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  To search a vessel for smuggled goods, e.g., "After the long voyage, the customs officers <i>rummaged</i> the ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="rummage_sale" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Rummage sale</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sale of damaged cargo (from French <i>arrimage</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="run" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Run</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The stern of the underwater body of a ship from where it begins to curve upward and inward.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A voyage.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="running_before_the_wind" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Points_of_sail" title="Points of sail">Running before the wind</a> or running <span id="Running"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Sailing more than about 160° away from the wind. If directly away from the wind, it's a <i>dead run</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="running_backstay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Running backstays</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The adjustable backstays of the main mast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-120"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="running_gear" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Running_gear" title="Running gear">Running gear</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The propellers, shafts, struts and related parts of a <a href="/wiki/Motorboat" title="Motorboat">motorboat</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="running_rigging" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Running_rigging" title="Running rigging">Running rigging</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Rigging" title="Rigging">Rigging</a> used to manipulate sails, spars, etc. in order to control the movement of the ship. Cf. standing rigging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184826_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184826-31">[31]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="S">S</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: S">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="safe_harbour" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Safe harbour</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A harbour which provides safety from bad weather</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="safe_haven" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Safe haven</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A safe harbour, including natural harbours, which provide safety from bad weather or attack.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="saddle_chock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Saddle chock</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A transverse beam placed over the transom with fairleads for mooring warps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-121"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sagging" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Hogging_and_sagging" title="Hogging and sagging">Sagging</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When the trough of a wave is amidships, causing the hull to deflect so the ends of the <a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">keel</a> are higher than the middle. The opposite of hogging.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sail" title="Sail">Sail</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A piece of fabric attached to a vessel and arranged such that it causes the wind to drive the vessel along. It may be attached to the vessel via a combination of <a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">mast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spar_(sailing)" title="Spar (sailing)">spars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rope" title="Rope">ropes</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The power harnessed by a sail or sails to propel a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  To use sail power to propel a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  A trip in a boat or ship, especially a sailboat or sailing ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">5.  In American usage, a <i><a href="/wiki/Sail_(submarine)" title="Sail (submarine)">sail</a></i> is a tower-like structure on the dorsal (topside) surface of submarines constructed since the mid-20th century which houses periscopes, access trunks for the bridge, etc.; called a <i>fin</i> in European and British Commonwealth countries. It differs from the <a href="/wiki/Conning_tower" title="Conning tower">conning tower</a> of earlier submarines, which was similar in appearance to a sail or fin, but housed instruments and controls from which the periscopes were used to direct the submarine and launch <a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">torpedo</a> attacks, functions not performed in a modern sail (or fin).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sail_loft" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sail_loft" title="Sail loft">Sail loft</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large open space used by <a href="/wiki/Sailmaker" title="Sailmaker">sailmakers</a> to spread out <a href="/wiki/Sail" title="Sail">sails</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sail-plan" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sail-plan" title="Sail-plan">Sail-plan</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A set of drawings showing various sail combinations recommended for use in various situations.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sailing_skiff" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sailing skiff</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">see 'skiff'.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sailmaker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sailmaker" title="Sailmaker">Sailmaker</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A craftsman who makes and repairs sails, working either on shore in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sail_loft" title="Sail loft">sail loft</a> or aboard a large, ocean-going sailing ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sailorman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sailorman</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">London term for sailing barge, or a bargeman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-122"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sally_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sally ship</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A method of freeing a vessel grounded on mud in which the crew forms a line and runs back and forth athwartships (q.v.) to cause her to rock back and forth, breaking the mud<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s suction and freeing her with little or no hull damage. When this is required, the crew is given the order <i>Sally ship!</i></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="saltie" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Saltie</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Great Lakes term for a vessel that sails the oceans.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="salty_dog" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Salty dog</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Slang for a sailor, especially for a seaman in the navy.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="salvor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Salvor</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A person engaged in salvage of a ship or items lost at sea.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sampan" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sampan" title="Sampan">Sampan</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A relatively flat bottomed Chinese wooden boat from 3.5 to 4.5 m long; some with a small shelter and may be used as permanent habitation on inland waters; generally used in coastal areas or rivers and as traditional fishing boats. It is unusual for a sampan to sail far from land as they do not have the means to survive rough weather.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sampson_post" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sampson post</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A strong vertical post used to support a <a href="/wiki/Ship" title="Ship">ship</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Windlass" title="Windlass">windlass</a> and the heel of a ship's <a href="/wiki/Bowsprit" title="Bowsprit">bowsprit</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="s.b." style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">S.B. (or SB) <span id="SB"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Prefix for "<a href="/wiki/Thames_sailing_barge" title="Thames sailing barge">Sailing Barge</a>", used before a ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s name.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="scandalize" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Scandalize</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To reduce the area and efficiency of a sail by expedient means (slacking the peak and tricing up the tack) without properly reefing, thus slowing boat speed. Also used in the past as a sign of mourning.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="scantlings" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Scantling" title="Scantling">Scantlings</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Dimensions of ships structural members, e.g., frame, beam, girder, etc.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="schooner" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Schooner" title="Schooner">Schooner</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of sailing vessel characterized by the use of fore-and-aft sails on two or more masts with the forward mast being no taller than the rear masts, first used by the Dutch in the 16th or 17th century.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="scow" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Scow" title="Scow">Scow</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A method of preparing an anchor for tripping by attaching an anchor cable to the crown and fixing to the ring by a light seizing (also known as becue). The seizing can be broken if the anchor becomes fouled.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A type of clinker dinghy, characteristically beamy and slow.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  An inland racing boat with no keel, a large sail plan, and a planing hull.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="screecher" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Screecher" title="Screecher">Screecher</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">This is a specialty sail whose name comes from combining the names <a href="/wiki/Spinnaker" title="Spinnaker">spinnaker</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reaching_(sailing)" title="Reaching (sailing)">Reaching</a> sails and can be used as an upwind <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">genoa sail</a>, reaching sail, or downwind sail.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="screw" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Screw</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  <a href="/wiki/Propeller" title="Propeller">Propeller</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Propeller-driven (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Screw_frigate" title="Screw frigate">screw frigate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Screw_sloop" title="Screw sloop">screw sloop</a>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="scud" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Scud</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A name given by sailors to the lowest clouds, which are mostly observed in squally weather.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="scudding" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Scudding</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A term applied to a vessel when carried furiously along by a tempest.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="scull" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scull" title="Scull">Scull</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  An oar used for <a href="/wiki/Sculling" title="Sculling">sculling</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A boat propelled by sculling, generally for recreation or racing.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sculling" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sculling" title="Sculling">Sculling</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A method of using oars to propel watercraft in which the oar or oars touch the water on both the port and starboard sides of the craft, or over the stern. On sailboats with transom-mounted rudders, forward propulsion can be made by a balanced side to side movement of the tiller, a form of sculling.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="scuppers" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Scuppers</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Originally a series of pipes fitted through the ship's side from inside the thicker deck waterway to the topside planking to drain water overboard, larger quantities drained through freeing ports, which were openings in the bulwarks.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="scuttle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Scuttle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small opening, or lid thereof, in a ship's deck or hull.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="scuttlebutt" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Scuttlebutt</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A barrel with a hole in used to hold water that sailors would drink from. By extension (in modern naval usage), a shipboard drinking fountain or water cooler.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Slang for <a href="/wiki/Gossip" title="Gossip">gossip</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="scuttling" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Scuttling" title="Scuttling">Scuttling</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Making a hole in the hull of a vessel or opening seacocks, especially in order to sink a vessel deliberately.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sea_anchor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sea_anchor" title="Sea anchor">Sea anchor</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A stabilizer deployed in the water for <a href="/wiki/Heaving_to" title="Heaving to">heaving to</a> in heavy weather. It acts as a brake and keeps the hull in line with the wind and perpendicular to waves. Often in the form of a large bag made of heavy canvas. See also <i>drogue</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sea_chest" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sea chest</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A watertight box built against the hull of the ship communicating with the sea through a grillage, to which valves and piping are attached to allow water in for ballast, engine cooling, and firefighting purposes. Also a wooden box used to store a sailor's effects.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sea_state" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sea_state" title="Sea state">Sea state</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The general condition of the free surface on a large body of water with respect to wind waves and swell at a certain location and moment, characterized by statistics, including the <a href="/wiki/Wave_height" title="Wave height">wave height</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Period_(physics)" title="Period (physics)">period</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_spectrum" title="Power spectrum">power spectrum</a>. The sea state varies with time, as the wind conditions or swell conditions change.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sea_trial" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sea_trial" title="Sea trial">Sea trial</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The testing phase of a boat, ship, or submarine, usually the final step in her construction, conducted to measure a vessel's performance and general seaworthiness before her owners take delivery of her.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="seaboots" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Seaboot" title="Seaboot">Seaboots</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">High waterproof boots for use at sea. In leisure sailing, known as <i>sailing wellies</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="seacock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Seacock" title="Seacock">Seacock</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Valve" title="Valve">valve</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Hull_(watercraft)" title="Hull (watercraft)">hull</a> of a boat.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sealing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sealing</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The hunting of <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The caulked floor of the hold. (also: ceiling). <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-123"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="seaman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Seaman" title="Seaman">Seaman</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Generic term for sailor, or (part of) a low naval rank</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="seaworthy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Seaworthy</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Certified for, and capable of, safely sailing at sea.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="second_mate" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Second_mate" title="Second mate">Second mate</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Also called <i>second officer</i>, a licensed member of the deck department of a merchant ship, third – or on some ocean liners fourth – in command; a watchkeeping officer, customarily the ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s navigator. Other duties vary, but the second mate is often the medical officer and in charge of maintaining distress signaling equipment. On oil tankers, the second mate usually assists the chief mate with tank-cleaning operations.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="second_officer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Second officer</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>second mate</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="seekers" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Seekers</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">London term for sailing barges that sought cargo, carrying cargo for other merchants at a fee, rather than for the owner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-124"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="self-sustaining" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Self-sustaining</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A merchant ship which is able to unload herself without any assistance from a harbor's facilities is <i>self-sustaining</i>, while a ship which requires the assistance of a harbor's facilities to unload herself is <i>non-self-sustaining</i>. Self-sustaining ships are more expensive to build, maintain, and operate than non-self-sustaining ships, but have the advantage of being able to operate in less-developed ports that lack the infrastructure necessary to unload ships.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="self-unloader" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Self-unloader</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Great Lakes slang term for a vessel with a conveyor or some other method of unloading the cargo without shoreside equipment.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sennet_whip" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Rope" title="Rope">Sennet whip</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A summary punitive implement</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="serve" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Serve</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Covering a rope with thin line to protect it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-125"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="set" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Set</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The direction toward which the current is flowing.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sextant" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sextant" title="Sextant">Sextant</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Navigational instrument used to measure a ship's latitude.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shackle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Shackle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">U-shaped iron, with a screw pin at the open end used for securing stays to sails, allowing easy removal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-126"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shaft_alley" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Shaft alley</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Section of a ship that houses the propulsion shaft, running from the engine room to the <a href="/wiki/Stuffing_box" title="Stuffing box">stuffing box</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shakedown_cruise" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Shakedown_cruise" title="Shakedown cruise">Shakedown cruise</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A cruise performed before a ship enters service or after major changes such as a crew change, repair, or overhaul during which the performance of the ship and her crew are tested under working conditions.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shakes" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Shakes</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Pieces of barrels or casks broken down to save space. They are worth very little, leading to the phrase "no great shakes".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shanghaied" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Shanghaied</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Condition of a crewman involuntarily impressed into service on a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sheave" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sheave</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The wheel in a block, which rotates as the rope runs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-127"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sheer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sheer</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The upward curve of a vessel's longitudinal lines as viewed from the side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-128"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sheer_plan" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sheer plan</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a>, a diagram showing an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elevation_(view)" title="Elevation (view)">elevation</a> of the ship's sheer viewed from the <a href="/wiki/Broadside" title="Broadside">broadside</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sheet" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sheet_(sailing)" title="Sheet (sailing)">Sheet</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A rope, attached to the clew, used to control the setting of a sail in relation to the direction of the wind. The sheet is often passed through a tackle before being attach to fixed points on the deck, or in the case of a barge, to a traveller on the main horse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-129"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shell" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Racing_shell" title="Racing shell">Shell</a> (in the United Kingdom also <i>fine boat</i>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An extremely narrow, and often disproportionately long, rowing boat outfitted with long oars, outriggers to hold the oarlocks away from the boat, and sliding seats, specifically designed for racing or exercise.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shelter_deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Shelter deck</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An upper deck having no overhead protection from the weather itself, but sheltering the deck below it.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shift_colors" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Shift colors</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Changing the flag and pennant display when a moored vessel becomes underway, and vice versa. A highly coordinated display that ships take pride in; the desired effect is that of one set of flags vanishing while another set flashes out at precisely the same time. Also, slang for changing out of one's Navy uniform into civilian clothes to go ashore. (The US Navy's newsletter for retired personnel is nicknamed <i>Shift Colors</i> from this reason.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shift_tides" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shift_tides" title="Shift tides">Shift tides</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Sighting the positions of the sun and moon using a <a href="/wiki/Sextant" title="Sextant">sextant</a> and using a <a href="/wiki/Nautical_almanac" title="Nautical almanac">nautical almanac</a> to determine the location and phase of the moon and calculating the relative effect of the tides on the navigation of the ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ship</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Noun – Strictly, a three-masted vessel square-rigged on all three masts, or on three masts of a vessel with more than three. Hence a ship-rigged barque would be a four master, square-rigged on fore, main and mizzen, with spanker and gaff topsail only on the <a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">Jigger-mast</a>. Generally now used refers to most medium or large vessels outfitted with smaller boats. As a consequence of this, submarines may be larger than small ships, but are called boats because they do not carry boats of their own.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Verb – To bring something aboard swiftly, as in "Ship oars."</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ship-of-the-line" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ship_of_the_line" title="Ship of the line">Ship-of-the-line</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of sailing warship constructed from the 1600s through the mid-1800s to serve as part of the <a href="/wiki/Line_of_battle" title="Line of battle">line of battle</a>; the largest and most powerful warships of the era.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ship_over" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ship over</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Verb – To reenlist. When a sailor extends his or her service another term.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ship_rig" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ship rig</dfn></dt>
<dt class="glossary" id="ship-rigged_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ship-rigged ship <span id="Ship_rigged_ship"></span><span id="Ship_rigged"></span><span id="Ship-rigged"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>full rigged ship</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ship_sloop" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ship_sloop" title="Ship sloop">Ship sloop</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of <a href="/wiki/Sloop-of-war" title="Sloop-of-war">sloop-of-war</a> introduced in the 1740s which had three square-rigged masts (in contrast to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brig_sloop" title="Brig sloop">brig sloop</a></i> introduced in the 1770s, which had two masts).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ship.27s_bell" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ship%27s_bell" title="Ship's bell">Ship's bell</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Striking the ship's bell is the traditional method of marking time and regulating the crew's watches. Each bell (from one to eight) represents a 30-minute period since the beginning of a four-hour <a href="/wiki/Watch_system" title="Watch system">watch</a>. For example, in the classical system, "Three bells in the morning watch" represents 90 minutes since the beginning of the morning watch, or 5:30am. "Eight bells" indicates the end of a watch.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ship.27s_biscuit" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ship's biscuit</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>hard tack</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ship.27s_company" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ship's company</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The <a href="/wiki/Crew" title="Crew">crew</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Ship" title="Ship">ship</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ship.27s_complement" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ship's complement</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The number of persons in a ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s crew, including officers.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ship.27s_husband" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ship's husband</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Once widely used term, now obsolete, for the man at a dockyard in charge of repairs to a ship. The term derived from the notion that the ship was a "lady" who needed to visit her "husband" when in need of repairs.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shipwright" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Shipwright</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A person who designs, builds, and repairs ships, especially wooden ones.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shipyard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Shipyard" title="Shipyard">Shipyard</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A facility where ships or boats are built and repaired. Routinely used as a synonym for <i>dockyard</i>, although <i>dockyard</i> sometimes is associated more closely with a facility used for maintenance and basing activities, while <i>shipyard</i> sometimes is associated more closely with a facility used in construction.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shoal" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Shoal" title="Shoal">Shoal</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Shallow water that is a hazard to navigation.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shoal_draught" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Shoal draught</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Shallow <a href="/wiki/Draft_(hull)" title="Draft (hull)">draught</a>, making the vessel capable of sailing in unusually shallow water.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shore_leave" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Shore leave</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Free time given to officers and crew of a naval vessel when they are off duty and allowed to disembark and spend time on land. See also <i>liberty</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="short_stay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Short stay</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The relative slackness of an anchor chain; this term means somewhat slack, but not vertical nor fully extended.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shorten" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Shorten</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  To take in the slack of (a rope).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To reduce (sail) by taking it in.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shot_across_the_bow" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Shot across the bow</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A shot fired close to and in front of a moving vessel to warn her to stop, often for boarding.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shroud" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Shroud_(sailing)" title="Shroud (sailing)">Shroud</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A rope or cable serving to hold a mast up from side to side.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="shrouds" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Shroud_(sailing)" title="Shroud (sailing)">Shrouds</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Standing rigging running from a mast to the sides of a ships to support the mast sideways. The <a href="/wiki/Shroud_(sailing)" title="Shroud (sailing)">shrouds</a> work with the <a href="/wiki/Stays_(nautical)" title="Stays (nautical)">stays</a>, which run forward and aft, to support the mast's weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-130"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sick_bay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sick_bay" title="Sick bay">Sick bay</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The compartment reserved for medical purposes.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sideboy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sideboy" title="Sideboy">Sideboy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">One of an even-numbered group of seamen posted in two rows on the quarterdeck when a visiting dignitary boards or leaves the ship, historically to help (or even hoist) him aboard.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sidewheel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sidewheel</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A side-mounted <a href="/wiki/Paddle_wheel" title="Paddle wheel">paddle wheel</a> used for propulsion by a <a href="/wiki/Paddle_steamer" title="Paddle steamer">paddle steamer</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Propelled by sidewheels (e.g., "sidewheel <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steamer</a>").</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sidewheeler" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sidewheeler</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Paddle_steamer" title="Paddle steamer">paddle steamer</a> propelled by a pair of <a href="/wiki/Paddle_wheel" title="Paddle wheel">paddle wheels</a>, one mounted on each side.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="siren" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siren_(noisemaker)" title="Siren (noisemaker)">Siren</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sound signal which uses electricity or compressed air to actuate either a disc or a cup shaped rotor.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sister_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sister_ship" title="Sister ship">Sister ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship of the same class as, and therefore virtually identical in design and appearance to, another ship. Sister ships share an identical or nearly identical hull and superstructure layout, similar displacement, and roughly comparable features and equipment. Often, sister ships become more differentiated during their service lives as their equipment (and, in the case of military ships, their armament) are separately altered.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="skeg" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Skeg" title="Skeg">Skeg</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A downward or sternward projection from the keel in front of the rudder. Protects the rudder from damage, and in <i>bilge keelers</i> may provide one "leg" of a tripod on which the boat stands when the tide is out.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="skiff" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Skiff" title="Skiff">Skiff</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small boat, traditionally a coastal or river craft, for leisure or fishing, with a single person or small crew. Sailing skiffs have developed into high performance competitive classes.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="skipper" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skipper_(boating)" title="Skipper (boating)">Skipper</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_(nautical)" title="Captain (nautical)">captain</a> of a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="skysail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Skysail</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sail set very high, above the royals. Only carried by a few ships.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="skyscraper" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Skyscraper</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small, triangular sail, above the skysail. Used in light winds on a few ships.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="slack_tide" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slack_tide" title="Slack tide">Slack tide</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">That period between rising tide and falling tide. or that period between falling tide and rising tide when there is no tidal induced current.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sling" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sling</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  To pass a rope around something preparatory to attaching a hoisting or lowering tackle to it.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A band of rope or iron for securing a yard to a mast; chiefly used in the plural, "<i>slings</i>".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="slip" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Slip</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A berth for a ship or boat; a place for a ship or boat to moor.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The difference between the theoretical distance traveled per revolution of a vessel's propeller and the actual advance of the vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  In marine engineering, the motion of the center of resistance of the float of a paddle wheel or the blade of an oar through the water horizontally.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  In marine engineering, the difference between a vessel's actual speed and the speed it would have if the propelling instrument acted upon a solid.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">5.  In marine engineering, the velocity relative to still water of the backward current of water produced by the propeller.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">6.  In marine insurance, a memorandum of the particulars of a risk for which a policy is to be executed, usually bearing the broker's name and initiated by the underwriters.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="slipway" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Slipway" title="Slipway">Slipway</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ramp on the shore by which ships or boats can be moved to and from the water. Slipways are used for building and repairing ships and boats. They are also used for launching and retrieving small boats on trailers towed by automobiles and flying boats on their undercarriage.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sloop" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sloop" title="Sloop">Sloop</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small to mid-sized sailboat larger than a dinghy, with one mast bearing a main sail and head sail and located farther forward than the mast of a <a href="/wiki/Cutter_(boat)" title="Cutter (boat)">cutter</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sloop-of-war" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sloop-of-war" title="Sloop-of-war">Sloop-of-war</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  In the 18th and 19th centuries, a small sailing warship carrying 18 or fewer guns with a single continuous gundeck.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In the 18th and 19th centuries, any sailing warship bearing fewer than 20 guns.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  In the 19th-century US Navy, the term used for the type of sailing warship known in other navies as a <a href="/wiki/Corvette" title="Corvette">corvette</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  In the early and mid-20th century, a small ocean-going warship not intended for fleet deployments, used instead for convoy escort, gunboat duties, etc.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="slop_chest" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Slop chest</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship's store of merchandise, such as clothing, tobacco, etc., maintained aboard merchant ships for sale to the crew.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="slush" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Slush</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Greasy substance obtained by boiling or scraping the fat from empty salted meat storage barrels, or the floating fat residue after boiling the crew's meal. In the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> the perquisite of the cook who could sell it or exchange it (usually for alcohol) with other members of the crew. Used for greasing parts of the running rigging of the ship and therefore valuable to the master and bosun.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="slush_fund" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Slush_fund" title="Slush fund">Slush fund</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The money obtained by the cook selling slush ashore. Used for the benefit of the crew (or the cook).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="smack" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Smack_(ship)" title="Smack (ship)">Smack</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A traditional fishing boat used off the coast of England and the Atlantic coast of America for most of the 19th century and in small numbers up to the mid-20th century. Originally a <a href="/wiki/Cutter_(boat)" title="Cutter (boat)">cutter</a>-rigged sailing boat, after about 1865 lengthened and given a <a href="/wiki/Ketch" title="Ketch">ketch</a> rig. Some had a <a href="/wiki/Topsail" title="Topsail">topsail</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mizzen_mast" title="Mizzen mast">mizzen mast</a>, others a <a href="/wiki/Bowsprit" title="Bowsprit">bowsprit</a> carrying a <a href="/wiki/Jib" title="Jib">jib</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="small_bower_.28anchor.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Small bower (anchor)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The smaller of two anchors carried in the bow.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="snotter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Snotter</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A short rope, spliced together at the ends and covered with hide, that is seized to the mast to hold the lower end of a sprit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184830_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184830-35">[35]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="snag" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Snag_(ecology)#Maritime_hazard" title="Snag (ecology)">Snag</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A tree or tree branch fixed in the bottom of a navigable body of water and partially submerged or rising nearly to the surface which can pierce and sink vessels. Also known as a <i>deadhead</i>. Snags were a particularly severe hazard in the 19th and early 20th centuries. <i>To be snagged</i> is to suffer damage from or to be sunk by such a hazard.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="snotty" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Snotty</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A midshipman (<a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> slang.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="snow" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Snow</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A form of <a href="/wiki/Brig" title="Brig">brig</a> where the gaff <a href="/wiki/Spanker_(sail)" title="Spanker (sail)">spanker</a> or driver is rigged on a "snow mast" a lighter spar supported in chocks close behind the <a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">main-mast</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="soft_eye" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Soft eye</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An eye splice without a thimble fitted.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sog" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">SOG</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Speed over ground, speed of the vessel relative to the Earth (and as shown by a GPS). Referenced on many fishing forums.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sonar" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sonar" title="Sonar">Sonar</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  An <a href="/wiki/Acronym" title="Acronym">acronym</a> for <b>SO</b>und <b>N</b>avigation <b>A</b>nd <b>R</b>anging, a method of using sound pulses to detect, range, and sometimes image underwater targets and obstacles or the bed of the sea. See also <i>echo sounding</i> and <i>ASDIC</i>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The equipment used to conduct such searches, ranging, and imaging.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sounding" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sounding</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heaving_the_lead.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="661" data-file-width="454" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Heaving_the_lead.jpg/170px-Heaving_the_lead.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Heaving_the_lead.jpg/255px-Heaving_the_lead.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Heaving_the_lead.jpg/340px-Heaving_the_lead.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heaving_the_lead.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sounding.</div>
</div>
</div>
Measuring the depth of the water. Traditionally done by <i>swinging the lead</i>, now commonly by <a href="/wiki/Echo_sounding" title="Echo sounding">echo sounding</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sou.27wester" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sou%27wester" title="Sou'wester">Sou'wester</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A storm from the south west.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A type of waterproof hat with a wide brim over the neck, worn in storms.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="spanker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Spanker_(sail)" title="Spanker (sail)">Spanker</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A fore-and-aft or gaff-rigged sail on the aft-most mast of a square-rigged vessel and the main fore-and-aft sail (spanker sail) on the aft-most mast of a (partially) fore-and-aft rigged vessel such as a <a href="/wiki/Schooner" title="Schooner">schooner</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Barquentine" title="Barquentine">barquentine</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Barque" title="Barque">barque</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="spanker-mast" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Spanker-mast</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The aft-most mast of a fore-and-aft or gaff-rigged vessel such as schooners, barquentines, and barques. A full-rigged ship has a spanker sail but not a spanker-mast (see <a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">Jigger-mast</a>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="spar" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Spar_(sailing)" title="Spar (sailing)">Spar</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A wooden, in later years also iron or steel pole used to support various pieces of rigging and sails. The big five-masted full-rigged <a href="/wiki/Tall_ship" title="Tall ship">tall ship</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Preu%C3%9Fen_(ship)" title="Preußen (ship)">Preussen</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German spelling</a>: <i>Preußen</i>) had crossed 30 steel yards, but only one wooden spar – the little gaff of its spanker sail.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="spar_torpedo" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Spar_torpedo" title="Spar torpedo">Spar torpedo</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A weapon consisting of a bomb placed at the end of a long spar and attached to a boat.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="speaking_tube" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Speaking tube</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">see <i>communication tube</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="spider_band_or_spider_hoop" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Spider band or Spider hoop</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An iron band around the base of a mast which holds a set of iron belaying pins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-131"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="spindrift" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Spindrift" title="Spindrift">Spindrift</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Finely divided water swept from crest of waves by strong winds.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="spinnaker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Spinnaker" title="Spinnaker">Spinnaker</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A large sail flown in front of the vessel while heading downwind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  a headsail set windward when running before the wind. The bargemans spinnaker is his topmast staysail, tacked to the to the mast, and sheeted round the weather crosstree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-132"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="spinnaker_pole" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Spinnaker_pole" title="Spinnaker pole">Spinnaker pole</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A spar used to help control a spinnaker or other <a href="/wiki/Headsail" title="Headsail">headsail</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="splice" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Rope_splicing" title="Rope splicing">Splice</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To join lines (ropes, cables, etc.) by unravelling their ends and intertwining them to form a continuous line. To form an eye or a knot by splicing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184830_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184830-35">[35]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="splice_the_mainbrace" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Splice_the_mainbrace" title="Splice the mainbrace">Splice the mainbrace</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Euphemism" title="Euphemism">euphemism</a>, it is an order given aboard naval vessels to issue the crew with a drink, traditionally <a href="/wiki/Grog" title="Grog">grog</a>. The phrase <i>splice the mainbrace</i> is used idiomatically meaning to go ashore on liberty, intending to go out for an evening of drinking.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sponson" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sponson" title="Sponson">Sponson</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A projection from the side of a vessel for protection, stability, or the mounting of equipment such as armaments or lifeboats. A sponson that extends a hull dimension at or below the waterline serves to increase flotation or add lift when underway. In salvage of a damaged or disabled vessel, a sponson may be a flotation tank attached to provide stability or buoyancy.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sponsor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sponsor</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The person, traditionally a woman, who christens a ship at its launching ceremony.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="spotting_top" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Spotting top</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A platform on a mast used to aid in <a href="/wiki/Gun_laying" title="Gun laying">gun laying</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="spreader" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Spreader_(sailboat)" title="Spreader (sailboat)">Spreader</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A spar on a sailboat used to deflect the shrouds to allow them to better support the mast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-133"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="spring" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Spring</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A line used parallel to that of the length of a craft, to prevent fore-aft motion of a boat, when moored or docked. Also used to assist the barge to swing its head round when leaving.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-134"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sprit" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sprit</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A spar, attached by a muzzle to a mast, or held in a snotter, that supports the peak of a spritsail. It is steadied by vangs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-135"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="spritsail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Spritsail" title="Spritsail">Spritsail</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A fore and aft sail, where the peak is supported by a sprit. It may be free-footed or use a boom.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A rig that uses a spritsail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-136"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sprittie" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sprittie</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A spritsail rigged barge<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-137"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="spurling_pipe" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Spurling pipe</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A pipe that connects to the chain locker, from which the anchor chain emerges onto the deck at the bow of a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="squadron" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Squadron_(naval)" title="Squadron (naval)">Squadron</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  In general, any significant group of warships which is considered too small to be designated a fleet, but otherwise not strictly defined by size. In some navies, the term <i><a href="/wiki/Flotilla" title="Flotilla">flotilla</a></i> may be used instead of or in addition to "squadron" to describe a significant group of warships smaller than a fleet.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Such a group of warships assigned to and named after a particular ocean, sea, or geographical region, commanded by an admiral who may be the naval commander-in-chief in that theatre, e.g., the <i><a href="/wiki/Asiatic_Squadron" title="Asiatic Squadron">Asiatic Squadron</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Squadron" title="North Atlantic Squadron">North Atlantic Squadron</a></i>, etc.; generally synonymous with similar naval formations known as <i>stations</i> (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  During the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a>, a temporary sub-division of a fleet.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  A temporary detachment of ships from a fleet.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">5.  Especially in the late 19th and early 20th centuries, a permanent battle formation of a fleet, equipped and trained to operate as a tactical unit under the overall command of the fleet or when detached from the fleet.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">6.  Especially in modern usage, an administrative naval command responsible for the manning, training, supply, and maintenance of a group of ships or submarines but not for directing their operations at sea.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="square" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Square</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To place at right angles with the mast or keel and parallel to the horizon e.g., "to <i>square</i> the yards."</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="square_meal" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Square meal</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sufficient quantity of food. Meals on board ship were served to the crew on a square wooden plate in harbor or at sea in good weather. Food in the Royal Navy was invariably better or at least in greater quantity than that available to the average landsman. However, while square wooden plates were indeed used on board ship, there is no established link between them and this particular term. The OED gives the earliest reference from the US in the mid-19th century.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="square_rig" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Square_rig" title="Square rig">Square rig</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A generic type of sail and rigging arrangement in which the primary driving sails are carried on yards which are perpendicular, or square, to the keel of the vessel and to the masts. A ship mainly so rigged is said to be <i>square-rigged</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="square_rigger" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Square rigger</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship which is square-rigged.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="squared_away" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Squared away</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Yards held rigidly perpendicular to their masts and parallel to the deck. This was rarely the best trim of the yards for efficiency but made a pretty sight for inspections and in harbor. The term is applied to situations and to people figuratively to mean that all difficulties have been resolved or that the person is performing well and is mentally and physically prepared.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="squat_effect" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Squat_effect" title="Squat effect">Squat effect</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The phenomenon by which a vessel moving quickly through shallow water creates an area of lowered pressure under its keel that reduces the ship's buoyancy, particularly at the bow. The reduced buoyancy causes the ship to "squat" lower in the water than would ordinarily be expected, and thus its effective draught is increased.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="s.s." style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">S.S. (or SS) <span id="SS"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Prefix for "<a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">Steam Ship</a>", used before a ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s name.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stack" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stack (also <i><a href="/wiki/Funnel_(ship)" title="Funnel (ship)">funnel</a></i>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Deck cargo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-138"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  See <i>funnel</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stackie" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stackie</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A barge designed to take a large deck cargo, usually of hay of straw needed to feed working horses. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-139"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stanchion" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Stanchion" title="Stanchion">Stanchion</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Vertical post near a deck's edge that supports life-lines. A timber fitted in between the frame heads on a wooden hull or a bracket on a steel vessel, approx one meter high, to support the bulwark plank or plating and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hand_rail" title="Hand rail">rail</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stand_.28past_tense_.27.27stood.27.27.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stand (past tense <i>stood</i>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Of a ship or its captain, to steer, sail, or steam, usually used in conjunction with a specified direction or destination, e.g., <i>The ship stood out of the harbor</i> or <i>The ship stood toward the east</i> or <i>The ship stood toward the missing vessel's last known position</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stand-on_.28vessel.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stand-on (vessel)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel <a href="/wiki/International_Regulations_for_Preventing_Collisions_at_Sea" title="International Regulations for Preventing Collisions at Sea">directed</a> to keep her course and speed where two vessels are approaching one another so as to involve a risk of collision.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="standing_part" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Standing part</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The section of a rope at a cleat or a block that is under tension, as opposed to the loose end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-140"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="standing_rigging" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Standing_rigging" title="Standing rigging">Standing rigging</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Rigging" title="Rigging">Rigging</a> which is used to support masts and spars, and is not normally manipulated during normal operations. Cf. running rigging.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stanliff" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stanliff</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A heavy wire cable attached to the mast at the hounds to support the weight of a spritsail at the heel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-141"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="starboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starboard" title="Starboard">Starboard</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The right side of the boat. Towards the right-hand side of a vessel facing forward. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-142"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup> Denoted with a green light at night. Derived from the old steering oar or <i>steerboard</i> which preceded the invention of the rudder.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="starboard_tack" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Starboard tack</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When sailing with the wind coming from the starboard side of the vessel. Has right of way over boats on <i>port tack</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="starter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Starter</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A rope used as a punitive device. See <i>teazer</i>, <i>togey</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stateroom" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stateroom</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A superior cabin for a vessel<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s officer.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In American usage, also a private passenger cabin in a vessel</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="station" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Station</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  In chiefly 19th- and early 20th-century usage, a naval formation under a commander-in-chief who controls all naval operations, and sometimes all naval shore facilities, within a specified geographic area (e.g., the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/China_Station" title="China Station">China Station</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/East_Indies_Station" title="East Indies Station">East Indies Station</a></i>, etc.), sometimes synonymous with <i><a href="/wiki/Squadron_(naval)" title="Squadron (naval)">squadron</a></i> (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  In <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a>, a harbour or cove with a foreshore suitable for a facility to support nearby fishing.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  <i>Naval station</i>, a naval base; a <i>naval air station</i> is a base for naval aircraft.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  <i>Coaling station</i>, a facility that supplies ships with coal.</dd>
