<dt class="glossary" id="stay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Stays_(nautical)" title="Stays (nautical)">Stay</a> <span id="Stays"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A strong rope supporting a mast, and leading from the head of one mast down to some other mast or other part of the vessel; rigging running fore (<i>forestay</i>) and aft (<i>backstay</i>) from a mast to the hull. The stays support a mast's weight forward and aft, while the <a href="/wiki/Shroud_(sailing)" title="Shroud (sailing)">shrouds</a> support its weight from side to side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184831_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184831-38">[38]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To incline forward, aft, or to one side by means of stays, e.g., to <i>stay</i> a mast.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  To tack; put on the other tack, e.g., to <i>stay</i> ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  To change; tack; go about; be in stays, as a ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">5.  A station or fixed anchorage for vessels.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">6.  <i>In stays</i> or <i>hove in stays</i>: in the act of going about while tacking.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">7.  <i>Miss stays</i> an unsuccessful attempt to tack.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stayfall" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stayfall</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A flexible wire cable rove through blocks, one on the stemhead, and one on the end of the forestay. This is the means by which the mainmast is lowered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-143"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="staysail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Staysail" title="Staysail">Staysail</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sail whose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luff_(sail)" title="Luff (sail)">luff</a> is attached to a forestay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-144"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="steamer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Steamer</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel equipped with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steam_propulsion" title="Steam propulsion">steam propulsion</a>. Also <i><a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steamboat</a></i> or <i>steamship</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="steerage" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steerage" title="Steerage">Steerage</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The effect of the helm on a vessel; the act of steering a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  19th- and early 20th-century term for the section of a passenger ship that provided inexpensive accommodation with no individual cabins.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="steerageway" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Steerageway</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The minimum speed at which a vessel will answer the helm, below which she cannot be steered. Speed sufficient for the rudder to "bite."</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="steering_flat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Steering flat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In a vessel, the compartment containing the steering gear.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="steering_oar" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Steering_oar" title="Steering oar">Steering oar</a> or steering board <span id="Steering_board"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A long, flat board or oar that went from the stern to well underwater, used to steer vessels before the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">rudder</a>. Traditionally on the starboard side of a ship (the "steering board" side).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="steeve" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Steeve</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A spar or derrick with a block at one end, used for stowing cargo.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To incline upwards at an angle (esp. of a bowsprit) rather than lie horizontally; to set at a particular upwards incline</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stem" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Stem_(ship)" title="Stem (ship)">Stem</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The extension of keel at the forward end of a ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  On a <a href="/wiki/Barge" title="Barge">barge</a>, the foremost timber set vertically to the keel, forming the head of the stem; it carries the forestay and other rigging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-145"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stern" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Stern" title="Stern">Stern</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The rear part of a ship, technically defined as the area built up over the sternpost, extending upwards from the counter rail to the <a href="/wiki/Taffrail" title="Taffrail">taffrail</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stern_chaser" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stern chaser</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>chase gun</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stern_tube" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stern tube</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The tube under the hull to bear the tailshaft for propulsion (usually at stern).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A <a href="/wiki/Torpedo_tube" title="Torpedo tube">torpedo tube</a> mounted in the stern of a submarine.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sterndrive" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sterndrive" title="Sterndrive">Sterndrive</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A propeller drive system similar to the lower part of an <a href="/wiki/Outboard_motor" title="Outboard motor">outboard motor</a> extending below the hull of a larger power boat or yacht, but driven by an engine mounted within the hull. Unlike a fixed propeller (but like an outboard), the boat may be steered by twisting the drive. See also <i>inboard motor</i> and <i>outboard motor</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sternwalk" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sternwalk</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An external walkway or gallery for the use of officers installed on the stern chiefly of British warships until the early 20th century.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sternway" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sternway</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The reverse movement of a boat or watercraft through the water.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sternwheel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sternwheel</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A stern-mounted <a href="/wiki/Paddle_wheel" title="Paddle wheel">paddle wheel</a> used for propulsion by a <a href="/wiki/Paddle_steamer" title="Paddle steamer">paddle steamer</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Propelled by a sternwheel (e.g., "sternwheel <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steamer</a>").</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sternwheeler" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sternwheeler</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Paddle_steamer" title="Paddle steamer">paddle steamer</a> propelled by a sternwheel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="steward" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Steward</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A member of a vessel<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s crew involved in commissary duties or in personal services to passengers or other crew members.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stood" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stood</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>Stand</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stopper_knot" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Stopper_knot" title="Stopper knot">Stopper knot</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A knot tied in the end of a rope, usually to stop it passing through a hole; most commonly a <a href="/wiki/Figure-eight_knot" title="Figure-eight knot">figure-eight knot</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stoppers" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stoppers</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A short rope to check a cable in a fixed position. <i>Anchor stoppers</i> hold the anchor when catted, <i>Bitt stoppers, Deck stoppers</i> used to retain the cable when at anchor, <i>shroud stoppers</i> contain a damaged shroud, <i>Foretack</i> and <i>Sheet Stoppers</i> secure the tacks until they are belayed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184832_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184832-39">[39]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="storeship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storeship" title="Storeship">Storeship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd><b>(also "store ship" or "stores ship")</b></dd>
<dd class="glossary">1.  During the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a> and immediately afterwards, a captured ship used to stow supplies and other goods for naval purposes.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Since the mid-20th century, a type of naval ship which provides supplies such as frozen, chilled' and dry provisions and propulsion and aviation fuel to warships which are at sea for an extended period of time. In some navies, synonymous with <i>replenishment oiler</i>, <i>fleet replenisher</i>, or <i>fleet tanker</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stove" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stove or Stove in <span id="Stove_in"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">(past tense of <i>stave</i>, often applied as present tense) to smash inward, to force a hole or break in, as in a cask, door or other (wooden) barrier.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stow" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stow</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">to store, or to put away e.g., personal effects, tackle, or cargo.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stowage" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Stowage" title="Stowage">Stowage</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The amount of room for storing materials on board a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stowaway" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Stowaway" title="Stowaway">Stowaway</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A trespasser on a ship; a person aboard a ship without permission and/or without payment, and usually boards undetected, remains hidden aboard, and jumps ship just before making port or reaching a port's dock; sometimes found aboard and imprisoned in the brig until the ship makes port and the prisoner can be transferred to the police or military.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="straggler" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Straggler</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In a convoy, a ship that is unable to maintain speed and falls behind.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="strake" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Strake" title="Strake">Strake</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">One of the overlapping boards in a <a href="/wiki/Clinker_(boat_building)" title="Clinker (boat building)">clinker</a> built hull.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stretcher" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stretcher</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An inclined foot rest, attached to the boat, to which a rower may place and in some instances (usually in competition) attach his feet.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="strike" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Strike</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  To haul down or lower (a flag, mast, etc.).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To surrender the vessel to the enemy, from <i>strike the colors</i>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  To remove a naval vessel<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s name from a country's naval register (after which the vessel is considered <i>stricken</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="strike_the_colors" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Strike the colors</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To surrender the vessel to an enemy, from the custom during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a> of lowering the vessel<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ensign_(flag)" title="Ensign (flag)">ensign</a> to indicate that she is surrendering.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="studding_sails" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Studding_sail" title="Studding sail">Studding sails</a> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ʌ/: 'u' in 'cut'">ʌ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/əl/: 'le' in 'bottle'">əl</span></span>/</a></span></span><span id="Studding_sail"></span><span id="Studding-sail"></span><span id="Studding-sails"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Long and narrow sails, used only in fine weather, on the outside of the large square sails.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stw" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">STW</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Speed through (the) water, speed of the vessel relative to the surrounding water (and as shown by a <a href="/wiki/Pitometer_log" title="Pitometer log">Log</a>). Used in navigation.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="stumpie" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Stumpie</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A barge without a topmast, or a tops'l barge underway with out her topsails set. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-146"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sunfish" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Sunfish_(sailboat)" title="Sunfish (sailboat)">Sunfish</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A personal-sized, beach-launched sailing <a href="/wiki/Dinghy" title="Dinghy">dinghy</a> with a pontoon-type hull, daggerboard, and lateen sail mounted to an un-stayed mast.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="superstructure" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Superstructure" title="Superstructure">Superstructure</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The parts of the ship or a boat, including sailboats, fishing boats, passenger ships, and submarines, that project above her main deck. This does not usually include its masts or any armament turrets.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="surge" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Surge</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A vessel's transient motion in a fore and aft direction.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To gently slacken a rope, so it slides up the capstan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184832_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184832-39">[39]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="survey_vessel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Survey vessel</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Any type of ship or boat that is used for mapping a body of water's bottom, <a href="/wiki/Benthic_zone" title="Benthic zone">benthic zone</a>, full water column, and surface for purposes of <a href="/wiki/Hydrography" title="Hydrography">hydrography</a>, general <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marine_salvage" title="Marine salvage">marine salvage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dredging" title="Dredging">dredging</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_archaeology" title="Marine archaeology">marine archaeology</a>, or the study of marine habitats.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sway" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sway</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A vessel's lateral motion from side to side.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  (v) To hoist: "Sway up my dunnage". <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184833_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184833-40">[40]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sweep" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sweep</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A long <a href="/wiki/Oar" title="Oar">oar</a> used to steer an unpowered <a href="/wiki/Lighter_(barge)" title="Lighter (barge)">lighter</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="swigging" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Swigging</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To take up the last bit of slack on a line such as a halyard, anchor line or dockline by taking a single turn round a cleat and alternately heaving on the rope above and below the cleat while keeping the tension on the tail.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="swimmie" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Swimmie</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A barge with a square overhanging bow, like a swimhead lighter. aka Muffies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-147"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="sweat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Sweat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A technique to finally tension a halyard, by pulling alternatively on the tail from the cleat and at right angles on the taut standing line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-148"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="swinging_the_compass" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Swinging the compass</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Measuring the accuracy in a ship's magnetic <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compass</a> so its readings can be adjusted – often by turning the ship and taking bearings on reference points.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="swinging_the_lamp" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Swinging the lamp</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Telling <a href="/wiki/Nautical_fiction" title="Nautical fiction">sea stories</a>. Referring to lamps slung from the <a href="/wiki/Deckhead" title="Deckhead">deckhead</a> which swing while at sea. Often used to indicate that the story teller is exaggerating.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="swinging_the_lead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Swinging the lead</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Measuring the depth of water beneath a ship using a <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>-weighted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sounding_line" title="Sounding line">sounding line</a>. Regarded as a relatively easy job, thus:</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Feigning illness etc to avoid a hard job.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="T">T</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: T">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="tabernacle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tabernacle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large bracket attached firmly to the deck, to which the foot of the mast is fixed. It has two sides or cheeks and a bolt forming the pivot around which the mast is raised and lowered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-149"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tack" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Tack_(sailing)" title="Tack (sailing)">Tack</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A leg of the route of a sailing vessel, particularly in relation to <i>tacking</i> (<i>q.v.</i>) and to <i>starboard tack</i> and <i>port tack</i> (also <i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Hard tack: <i>q.v.</i>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  The front bottom corner of a sail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-150"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tacking" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Tacking_(sailing)" title="Tacking (sailing)">Tacking</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Zig-zagging so as to sail directly towards the wind (and for some rigs also away from it).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  <i>Going about</i> (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tacking_duels" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tacking duels</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In sailboat racing on an upwind leg of the race course the complex manoeuvres of lead and overtaking boats to vie for the aerodynamic advantage of clear air. This results from the ongoing strategy of the lead boat's effort to keep the following boat(s) in the blanket of disturbed bad air he is creating.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tackle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tackle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A pair of blocks through which is rove a rope to provide an advantageous purchase. Used for lifting heavy loads and to raise and trim sails. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-151"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tactical_diameter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Tactical_diameter" title="Tactical diameter">Tactical diameter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The perpendicular distance between a ship's course when the helm is put hard over and her course when she has turned through 180 degrees; the ratio of the tactical diameter divided by the ship's <a href="/wiki/Length_between_perpendiculars" title="Length between perpendiculars">length between perpendiculars</a> gives a dimensionless parameter which can be used to compare the manoeuverability of ships.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="taffrail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Taffrail" title="Taffrail">Taffrail</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A rail at the stern of the boat that covers the head of the counter timbers.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tail</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The loose end of a rope that has been secured to a winch or a cleat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-152"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tailshaft" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Drive_shaft" title="Drive shaft">Tailshaft</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A kind of metallic shafting (a rod of metal) to hold the propeller and connected to the power engine. When the tailshaft is moved, the propeller may also be moved for propulsion.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="taken_aback" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Taken aback</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An inattentive helmsmen might allow the dangerous situation to arise where the wind is blowing into the sails 'backwards', causing a sudden (and possibly dangerous) shift in the position of the sails.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="taking_the_wind_out_of_his_sails" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Taking the wind out of his sails</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To sail in a way that steals the wind from another ship. cf. overbear.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tall_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Tall_ship" title="Tall ship">Tall ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A tall ship is a large, traditionally-rigged sailing vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tally" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tally</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The operation of hauling aft the sheets, or drawing them in the direction of the ship's stern.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tanker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Tanker_(ship)" title="Tanker (ship)">Tanker</a> (also <i>tank ship</i> or <i>tankship</i>) <span id="Tank_ship"></span><span id="Tankship"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship designed to transport liquids in bulk.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="target_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Target_ship" title="Target ship">Target ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel, typically an obsolete or captured warship, used for naval gunnery practice or for weapons testing. The term includes both ships intended to be sunk and ships intended to survive and see repeated use as a target.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="task_force" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Task_Force" title="Task Force">Task Force</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Temporary naval organisations composed of particular ships, aircraft, submarines, military land forces, or shore service units, assigned to fulfill certain missions. Seemingly drawn originally from Royal Navy heritage, the emphasis is placed on the individual commander of the unit, and references to 'CTF' are common for "Commander Task Force".</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tattle_tale" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tattle Tale</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Light cord attached to a mooring line at two points a few inches apart with a slack section in between (resembling an inch-worm) to indicate when the line is stretching from the ship's rising with the tide. Obviously only used when moored to a fixed dock or pier and only on watches with a flood tide.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tell-tale" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Tell-tale#Sailing" title="Tell-tale">Tell-tale</a> (sometimes tell-tail) <span id="Tell-tail"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A light piece of string, yarn, rope or plastic (often magnetic audio tape) attached to a <a href="/wiki/Stays_(nautical)" title="Stays (nautical)">stay</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Shroud_(sailing)" title="Shroud (sailing)">shroud</a> to indicate the local wind direction. They may also be attached to the surface and/or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leech_(sail)" title="Leech (sail)">leech</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Sail" title="Sail">sail</a> to indicate the state of the air flow over the surface of the sail. They are referenced when optimizing the trim of the sails to achieve the best boat speed in the prevailing wind conditions. (See <i>dogvane</i>)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tender" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ship%27s_tender" title="Ship's tender">Tender</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A type of naval <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_ship" title="Auxiliary ship">auxiliary ship</a> (q.v.) designed to provide advanced basing services in undeveloped harbors to seaplanes, flying boats, torpedo boats, destroyers, or submarines.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A vessel used to provide transportation services for people and supplies to and from shore for a larger vessel, sometimes called a <i>ship's tender</i>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  A vessel used to maintain navigational aids, such as buoys and lighthouses.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="t.e.v." style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">T.E.V. (or TEV) <span id="TEV"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Prefix for "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turbo-electric" title="Turbo-electric">Turbo-Electric</a> Vessel," used before a ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s name.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="texas" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Texas_(steamboat)" title="Texas (steamboat)">Texas</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A structure or section of a steamboat that includes the pilothouse and the crew's quarters, located on the hurricane deck, in this case also called the <i>texas deck</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="texas_deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Texas deck</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The deck of a steamboat on which its <i>texas</i> (<i>q.v.</i>) is located. Also known as a <i>hurricane deck</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="third_mate" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Third_mate" title="Third mate">Third mate</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Also called the <i>third officer</i>, a licensed member of the deck department of a merchant ship, fourth – or on some ocean liners fifth – in command; a watchkeeping officer, customarily also the ship's safety officer, responsible for the ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s firefighting equipment, lifeboats, and other emergency systems. Other duties of the third mate vary depending on the type of ship, its crewing, and other factors.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="third_officer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Third officer</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>third mate</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="thole" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Thole</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Vertical wooden peg or pin inserted through the gunwale to form a fulcrum for oars when rowing. Used in place of a <i>rowlock</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="throat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Throat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The forward top corner of a square fore and aft sail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-153"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The end of the gaff, next to the mast. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184834_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184834-41">[41]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="three_sheets_to_the_wind" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Three sheets to the wind</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">On a three-masted ship, having the sheets of the three lower courses loose will result in the ship meandering aimlessly downwind. Also, a sailor who has drunk strong spirits beyond his capacity.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="thwart" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Thwart" title="Thwart">Thwart</a> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/θ/: 'th' in 'thigh'">θ</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/ɔːr/: 'ar' in 'war'">ɔːr</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A bench seat across the width of an open boat.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="timoneer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Timoneer</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">From the French <i>timonnier</i>, is a name given, on particular occasions, to the steersman of a ship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tin_can" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tin can</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">US Navy slang for a <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">destroyer</a>; often shortened to <i>can</i> (<i>q.v.</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tinclad" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tinclad</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A lightly armored steam-powered river gunboat used by the US Navy during the American Civil War (1861–1865).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tingle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tingle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A thin temporary patch.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tiller" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Tiller" title="Tiller">Tiller</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A lever used for steering, attached to the top of the rudder post. Used mainly on smaller vessels, such as dinghies and rowing boats.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="toe-rail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Toe-rail</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A low strip running around the edge of the deck like a low bulwark. It may be shortened or have gaps in it to allow water to flow off the deck.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="toe_the_line" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Toe the line or Toe the mark <span id="Toe_the_mark"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">At parade, sailors and soldiers were required to stand in line, their toes in line with a seam of the deck.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tompion" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Tampion" title="Tampion">Tompion</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A block of wood inserted into the barrel of a gun on a 19th-century warship to keep out the sea spray; also used for covers for the ends of the barrels of more modern ships' guns, the larger of which are often adorned with the ship's crest or other decoration.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tonnage" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Tonnage" title="Tonnage">Tonnage</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><span class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">Various measures of the size or cargo carrying capacity of a ship, including:</span></p>
1. <a href="/wiki/Deadweight_tonnage" title="Deadweight tonnage">Deadweight tonnage</a>, the total weight of a vessel, mostly without payload.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  <a href="/wiki/Displacement_(ship)" title="Displacement (ship)">Displacement</a> tonnage, the total weight of a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  <a href="/wiki/Gross_register_tonnage" title="Gross register tonnage">Gross register tonnage</a>, the total internal volume of a vessel, with one gross register ton equal to 100 <a href="/wiki/Cubic_foot" title="Cubic foot">cubic feet</a> (2.8316846592 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cubic_meter" title="Cubic meter">cubic meters</a>).</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  <a href="/wiki/Gross_tonnage" title="Gross tonnage">Gross tonnage</a>, a function of the volume of all of a ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s internal spaces.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">5.  Lightship or lightweight tonnage, the weight of a ship without any fuel, cargo, supplies, water, passengers, etc. on board.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">6.  <a href="/wiki/Net_register_tonnage" title="Net register tonnage">Net register tonnage</a>, the volume of cargo a vessel can carry.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">7.  <a href="/wiki/Net_tonnage" title="Net tonnage">Net tonnage</a>, the volume of all cargo spaces on a ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">8.  <a href="/wiki/Thames_Measurement" title="Thames Measurement">Thames Measurement</a> tonnage, the volume of a small vessel calculated based on her length and beam.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="top" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Top_(sailing_ship)" title="Top (sailing ship)">Top</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The platform at the upper end of each (lower) mast of a square-rigged ship, typically one-fourth to one-third of the way up the mast. The main purpose of a top is to anchor the shrouds of the topmast that extends above it. See also <i>fighting top</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184835_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184835-42">[42]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="topgallant" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Topgallant</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The mast or sails above the tops. (See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topgallant_mast" title="Topgallant mast">topgallant mast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Topgallant_sail" title="Topgallant sail">topgallant sail</a>.)</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tophamper" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tophamper</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A collective term for the masts, yards, sails and rigging of a sailing ship, or for similarly insubstantial structures above the upper deck of any ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="topman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Topman</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A crewmember stationed in a top.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="topmast" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Topmast" title="Topmast">Topmast</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The second section of the mast above the deck; formerly the upper mast, later surmounted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topgallant_mast" title="Topgallant mast">topgallant mast</a>; carrying the topsails.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="topmast_pole" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Topmast pole</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Part of the spar between the hounds and the truck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-154"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="topping_lift" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Topping_lift" title="Topping lift">Topping lift</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A line which is part of the rigging on a sailing boat; it applies upward force on a spar or boom. The most common topping lift on a modern sailing boat is attached to the boom<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184835_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184835-42">[42]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="topsail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Topsail" title="Topsail">Topsail</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The second sail (counting from the bottom) up a mast. These may be either square sails or fore-and-aft ones, in which case they often "fill in" between the mast and the gaff of the sail below.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="topsides" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Topsides" title="Topsides">Topsides</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The part of the hull between the waterline and the deck. Also, <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms#Above-water_hull" title="Glossary of nautical terms">Above-water hull</a></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="torpedo" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">Torpedo</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Prior to about 1900, the term for a variety of explosive devices designed for use in water, including <i><a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">mines</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Spar_torpedo" title="Spar torpedo">spar torpedoes</a></i> and, after the mid-19th century, "automotive," "automobile," "locomotive," or "fish" torpedoes (self-propelled weapons which fit the modern definition of "torpedo").</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Since about 1900, a term used exclusively for a self-propelled weapon with an explosive warhead, launched above or below the water surface, propelled underwater towards a target, and designed to detonate either on contact with its target or in proximity to it.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="touch_and_go" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Touch and go</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The bottom of the ship touching the bottom, but not grounding.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Stopping at a dock or pier for a very short time without tying up, to let off or take on crew or goods.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  Practice of aircraft on aircraft carriers touching the carrier deck and taking off again without dropping hooks.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="towing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Towing</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The operation of drawing a vessel forward by means of long lines.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="traffic_separation_scheme" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Traffic_Separation_Scheme" title="Traffic Separation Scheme">Traffic Separation Scheme</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Shipping corridors marked by buoys which separate incoming from outgoing vessels. Improperly called <i>Sea Lanes</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="trailboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Trailboard" title="Trailboard">Trailboard</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A decorative board at the bow of a vessel, sometimes bearing the vessel's name.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="training_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Training ship</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship used to train students as sailors, especially a ship employed by a navy or coast guard to train future officers. The term refers both to ships used for training at sea and to old, immobile hulks used to house classrooms.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tramp_freighter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tramp freighter</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Cargo_ship" title="Cargo ship">cargo ship</a> engaged in the <a href="/wiki/Tramp_trade" title="Tramp trade">tramp trade</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tramp_steamer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tramp steamer</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Steamship" title="Steamship">steamship</a> engaged in the tramp trade.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tramp_trade" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Tramp_trade" title="Tramp trade">Tramp trade</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Shipping trade on the spot market in which the vessels involved do not have a fixed schedule or itinerary or published ports of call. This contrasts with freight liner service, in which vessels make regular, scheduled runs between published ports.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tramper" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tramper</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel engaged in the tramp trade.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="transmitting_station" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Transmitting station</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">British term for a room located in the interior of a ship containing computers and other specialised equipment needed to calculate the range and bearing of a target from information gathered by the ship's spotters and range finders. These were designated "plotting rooms" by the US Navy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="transom" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Transom_(nautical)" title="Transom (nautical)">Transom</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Lateral members fasten inside the sternpost, to which the hull and deckplanks are fitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-155"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  The aft "wall" of the stern; often the part to which an outboard unit or the drive portion of a sterndrive is attached.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  A more or less flat surface across the <a href="/wiki/Stern" title="Stern">stern</a> of a vessel. Dinghies tend to have almost vertical transoms, whereas yachts' transoms may be raked forward or aft.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="transport" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Transport</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>troopship</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="traveller.28s.29" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Mechanical_traveller" title="Mechanical traveller">Travellers</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184835_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184835-42">[42]</a></sup></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Small fittings that slide on a rod or line. The most common use is for the inboard end of the mainsheet.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A more esoteric form of traveller consists of "slight iron rings, encircling the backstays, which are used for hoisting the top-gallant yards, and confining them to the backstays".</dd>
<dd class="glossary">An iron ring that moves on the main horse on a sailing barge. It is fitted with an eye onto which is hooked the main sheet, of the loose-footed main-sail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-156"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="trawler" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Trawler</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commercial_trawler" title="Commercial trawler">Commercial trawler</a></i>, a fishing boat that uses a <a href="/wiki/Trawling" title="Trawling">trawl net</a> or <a href="/wiki/Seine_fishing" title="Seine fishing">dragnet</a> to catch fish.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A fisherman who uses a trawl net.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  <i><a href="/wiki/Naval_trawler" title="Naval trawler">Naval trawler</a></i>, a converted trawler, or boat built in that style, used for naval purposes.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  <i><a href="/wiki/Recreational_trawler" title="Recreational trawler">Recreational trawler</a></i>, a pleasure boat built in the style of a trawler.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="treenail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Treenail" title="Treenail">Treenail</a> (also trenail, trennel, or trunnel)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A wooden peg, pin, or dowel used to fasten pieces of wood together, particularly the hull, gunnels, thwarts, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184835_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184835-42">[42]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="trice" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Trice</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To haul and tie up by means of a rope, to make it less inconvenient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184836_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184836-45">[45]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="trick" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Trick</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A period of time spent at the wheel (<i>"my trick's over"</i>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="trim" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Trim</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Relationship of ship's hull to waterline.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Adjustments made to sails to maximize their efficiency.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="trimaran" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Trimaran" title="Trimaran">Trimaran</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel with three hulls.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="trimmer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Trimmer, sometimes <a href="/wiki/Coal_trimmer" title="Coal trimmer">Coal trimmer</a> <span id="Coal_trimmer"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">person responsible for ensuring that a vessel remains in 'trim' (that the cargo and fuel are evenly balanced). An important task on a coal-fired vessel, as it could get 'out-of-trim' coal is consumed.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="trooping" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Trooping</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Operating as a <a href="/wiki/Troopship" title="Troopship">troopship</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="troopship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Troopship" title="Troopship">Troopship</a> (also troop ship, troop transport, or trooper) <span id="Troop_ship"></span><span id="Troop_transport"></span><span id="Trooper"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship used to carry soldiers. Troopships are not specially designed for military operations and unlike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landing_ship" title="Landing ship">landing ships</a> cannot land troops directly onto a shore; instead they unload troops at a harbor or onto smaller vessels for transportation to shore.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="true_bearing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">True bearing</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An absolute bearing (<i>q.v.</i>) using true north.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="true_north" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/True_north" title="True north">True north</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The direction of the geographical <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="truss" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Truss</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The rope or iron used to keep the center of a yard to the mast.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tug_or_tugboat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tug or <a href="/wiki/Tugboat" title="Tugboat">tugboat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A boat that maneuvers other vessels by pushing or towing them. Tugs are powerful for their size and strongly built, and some are ocean-going.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tumblehome" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Tumblehome" title="Tumblehome">Tumblehome</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Hull shape, when viewed in a transverse section, where the widest part of the hull is someway below deck level.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="turn" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Turn_(knot)" title="Turn (knot)">Turn</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A knot passing behind or around an object.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="turn_to" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Turn To (Turn Two)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A term meaning "Get to work," often hand-signed by two fingers and hand motion in turning fashion.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="turnbuckle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Turnbuckle" title="Turnbuckle">Turnbuckle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">see <i>bottlescrew</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="turret" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Gun_turret" title="Gun turret">Turret</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  Originally (in the mid-to-late 19th century), an enclosed armored rotating cylindrical box mounting guns which fired through gunports, the turret rotating over a bearing mounted on a ship's deck or within her hull. Turret-equipped ships contrasted sharply with those equipped with <a href="/wiki/Barbette" title="Barbette">barbettes</a>, which in the second half of the 19th century were open-topped armored rings over which rotating gun(s) mounted on a turntable could fire.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Since the late 19th century, an enclosed armored rotating gunhouse mounted above a barbette, with the gun(s) and their rotating turntable mounted in the barbette protected by the gunhouse; in 20th- and 21st-century usage, this generally is any armored, rotating gun installation on a warship.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="turtleback_deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Turtleback deck</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Deck_(ship)" title="Deck (ship)">deck</a> that has slight positive curvature when viewed in cross-section. The purpose of this curvature is usually to shed water, but in warships it also functions to make the deck more resistant to shells.</dd>
<dd><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="turtling" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Turtling_(sailing)" title="Turtling (sailing)">Turtling</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In <a href="/wiki/Dinghy_sailing" title="Dinghy sailing">dinghy sailing</a> especially (but can include other boats), a boat is said to be <b>turtling</b> or to <b>turn turtle</b> when the boat is fully inverted with the mast pointing down to the lake bottom or seabed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[A]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OxfordDictionaries_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordDictionaries-49">[48]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tweendeck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tweendeck" title="Tweendeck">Tweendeck</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A deck on a general <a href="/wiki/Cargo_ship" title="Cargo ship">cargo ship</a> located between the <i>main deck</i> (or <i>weather deck</i>) and the <i>hold</i> space. A general cargo ship may have one or two tweendecks (or none at all).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tweendeck_space" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tweendeck space</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The space on a tweendeck available for carrying cargo or other uses.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tweendecker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Tweendecker" title="Tweendecker">Tweendecker</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A general <a href="/wiki/Cargo_ship" title="Cargo ship">cargo ship</a> equipped with one or more tweendecks.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="two_six_heave" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Two_six_heave" title="Two six heave">Two six heave</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Royal Navy slang term meaning to pull. Originally a sailing navy term referring to the two members of a gun crew (numbers two and six) who ran out the gun by pulling on the ropes that secured it in place.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="two_blocks" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Two blocks</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When the two blocks in a tackle have become so close that no further movement is possible as in chock-a-block. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-157"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="tye" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Tye</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A chain or rope used for hoisting or lowering a yard. A tye runs from the horizontal center of a given yard to a corresponding mast and from there down to a tackle. Sometimes specifically called a <b>chain tye</b> or a <b>rope tye</b>.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="U">U</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: U">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="unassisted_sailing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Unassisted_sailing" title="Unassisted sailing">Unassisted sailing</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A voyage, usually singlehanded, with no intermediate port stops or physical assistance from external sources.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="under_the_weather" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Under the weather</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Serving a watch on the weather side of the ship, exposed to wind and spray.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="under_way_or_underway" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Under way or underway</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel that is moving under control: that is, neither at anchor, made fast to the shore, aground nor adrift. Way refers to speed sufficient to steer with the rudder. "Under weigh" is an erroneous synonym.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-158"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="underwater_hull" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Underwater hull or underwater ship <span id="Underwater_ship"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The underwater section of a vessel beneath the waterline, normally not visible except when in drydock or (historically), <i>careened</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="underway_replenishment" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Underway_replenishment" title="Underway replenishment">Underway replenishment</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A method employed by navies to transfer fuel, munitions, and stores from one ship to another while underway. Sometimes abbreviation as <i>UNREP</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="unreeve" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Unreeve</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To pull a rope from a sheave or block.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-159"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="unship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Unship</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  To remove from a vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To remove an oar or mast from its normal position</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="up-and-down" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Up-and-down</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The relative slackness of an anchor chain where the anchor chain is slack and hangs vertically down from the hawsepipe.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="up-behind" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Up-behind</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Slack off quickly and run slack to a belaying point. This order is given when a line or wire has been stopped off or falls have been four-in-hand and the hauling part is to be belayed.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="upbound" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Downbound_/_Upbound" title="Downbound / Upbound">Upbound</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A vessel traveling upstream.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  Westward-traveling vessels in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> region (terminology as used by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Lawrence_Seaway_Development_Corporation" title="St. Lawrence Seaway Development Corporation">St. Lawrence Seaway Development Corporation</a>).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="uppers" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Uppers</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The brails above the mains synonym of 'Peaks'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-160"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="upper-yardmen" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Upper-yardmen</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Specially selected personnel .</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="V">V</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: V">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="v-hull" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/V-hull_(boat)" title="V-hull (boat)">V-hull</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The shape of a boat or ship in which the contours of the hull come in a straight line to the keel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="vang" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Vang</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A rope (line) leading from gaff to either side of the deck, used to prevent the gaff from sagging.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  One of a pair of ropes leading from the deck to the head of a spritsail. It steadies the sprit and can control the sails performmance during a tack. The vang fall blocks are mounted slightly afore the main horse while rolling vangs are extra preventers lead forward to keep the sail to leeward in heavy weather<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-161"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  See <i>boom vang</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="vanishing_angle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Vanishing angle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The maximum degree of heel after which a vessel becomes unable to return to an upright position.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="veer_away" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Veer away</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To let go a rope gently <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184836_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184836-45">[45]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="vessel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Vessel</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Any craft designed for transportation on water, such as a <a href="/wiki/Ship" title="Ship">ship</a> or <a href="/wiki/Boat" title="Boat">boat</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="viol" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Viol</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large rope used to unmoor or heave up the anchor. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184836_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184836-45">[45]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="voice_pipe" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Voice pipe or voice tube</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">see <i>communication tube</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="voyage" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Voyage</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A long journey by ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  To go on such a journey.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="voyl" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Voyl</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">see <i>viol</i>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184836_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184836-45">[45]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="W">W</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: W">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="waft" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Waft</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A signal flag on a vessel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="waist" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Waist</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The central deck of a ship between the forecastle and the quarterdeck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="wake" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Wake" title="Wake">Wake</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Turbulence behind a vessel. Not to be confused with <i>wash</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="wale" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Wale" title="Wale">Wale</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Any of the strong and thick planks running lengthwise along a vessel, forming the lower part of the vessel's sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-162"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="wardroom" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Wardroom" title="Wardroom">Wardroom</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  The living quarters of a naval ship designated for the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commissioned_officer" title="Commissioned officer">commissioned officers</a> other than the captain.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A collective term for the commissioned officers of a naval ship excluding her captain; e.g., <i>The captain rarely referred to his wardroom for advice, and this led to their discontent</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="warp" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Warp</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  To move a vessel by hauling on a line or cable that is fastened to an anchor or pier; especially to move a sailing ship through a restricted place such as a harbour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184837_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184837-51">[50]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A line or cable used in warping a ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-163"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  The length of the shrouds from the bolster to the deadeye<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184837_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184837-51">[50]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="wash" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Wash</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The waves created by a vessel. Not to be confused with <i>wake</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="watch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Watch_system" title="Watch system">Watch</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A period of time during which a part of the crew is on duty. Changes of watch are marked by strokes on the <a href="/wiki/Ship%27s_bell" title="Ship's bell">ship's bell</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="watchstanding_or_watchkeeping" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Watchstanding" title="Watchstanding">Watchstanding</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The allocation of crew or staff to a watch.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="water_kite" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Water kite</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i>paravane</i> (definition 2).</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="watercraft" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Watercraft" title="Watercraft">Watercraft</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Water transport vessels. <a href="/wiki/Ship" title="Ship">Ships</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boat" title="Boat">boats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Personal_water_craft" title="Personal water craft">personal water craft</a>, etc.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="waterline" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Waterline" title="Waterline">Waterline</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The line where the hull of a ship meets the water<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s surface.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="watersail" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Watersail" title="Watersail">Watersail</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sail hung below the boom on gaff rig boats for extra downwind performance when racing.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="waterway" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Waterway</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  <a href="/wiki/Waterway" title="Waterway">Waterway</a>, a navigable body of water.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A strake of timber laid against the frames or bulwark stanchions at the margin of a laid wooden deck, usually about twice the thickness of the deck planking.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="way" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Way</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Speed, progress, or momentum, or more technically, the point at which there is sufficient water flow past a vessel's rudder for it to be able to steer the vessel (i.e., the rudder begins to "bite," sometimes also called "steerage way.") To <i>make way</i> is to move; to "have way on" or "to have steerage way" is to have enough speed to control the vessel with its rudder; to <i>lose way</i> is to slow down or to not have enough speed to control with the rudder. "Way enough" is a coxswain's command that the oarsmen stop rowing, and allow the boat to proceed with its existing way.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="way-landing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Way-landing</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An intermediate stop along the route of a steamboat.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="way-lay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Way-lay</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The verb's origin, from wegelage, means "lying in wait, with evil or hostile intent." so to be waylaid referred to a ship taken off its course, route, or way, by surprise, typically by unfortunate or nefarious means. In H. Melville's novel, 'Moby Dick', the great white whale <i>waylaid</i> the ship and sank it with only a few souls surviving in lifeboats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="waypoint" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Waypoint" title="Waypoint">Waypoint</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A location defined by navigational coordinates, especially as part of a planned route.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="ways" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Ways</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The timbers of shipyard stocks that slope into the water and along which a ship or large boat is launched. A ship undergoing construction in a shipyard is said to be <i>on the ways</i>, while a ship scrapped there is said to be <i>broken up in the ways</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="wearing_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jibe_(sailing)" title="Jibe (sailing)">Wearing ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Tacking away from the wind in a square-rigged vessel. See also <i>gybe</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="weather_deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Weather deck</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Whichever deck is that exposed to the weather – usually either the main deck or, in larger vessels, the upper deck.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="weather_gage" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Weather_gage" title="Weather gage">Weather gage</a> or weather gauge or weather-beam <span id="Weather_gauge"></span><span id="Weather-beam"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Favorable position over another sailing vessel with respect to the wind.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="weather_helm" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Weather_helm" title="Weather helm">Weather helm</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The tendency of a sailboat to turn to windward in a strong wind when there is no change in the rudder's position. This is the opposite of lee helm and is the result of a dynamically unbalanced condition. See also <i>Center of lateral resistance</i>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="weather_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Weather_ship" title="Weather ship">Weather ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship stationed in the ocean as a platform for surface and upper air meteorological observations for use in weather forecasting.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="weather_side" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Weather side</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The side of a ship exposed to the wind.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="weatherly" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Weatherly</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship that is easily sailed and maneuvered; makes little leeway when sailing to windward.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="weigh_anchor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Weigh anchor</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To heave up (an anchor) preparatory to sailing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-164"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="well" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Well</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Place in the ship's hold for pumps.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="well-found" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Well-found</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Properly set up or provisioned.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="wetted_area" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Wetted_area" title="Wetted area">Wetted area</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In sailboating, portion of the hull immersed in water.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="whaleback" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Whaleback" title="Whaleback">Whaleback</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A type of cargo steamship of unusual design formerly used on the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> of <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, notably for carrying grain or ore. The hull continuously curved above the <a href="/wiki/Waterline" title="Waterline">waterline</a> from vertical to horizontal, and when the ship was fully loaded, only the rounded portion of her hull (the "whaleback" proper) was visible above the waterline. With sides curved in towards the ends, whalebacks had a spoon bow and a very convex upper deck.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A type of high-speed <a href="/wiki/Launch_(boat)" title="Launch (boat)">launch</a> first designed for the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, or certain smaller rescue and research vessels most common in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> that, like the Great Lakes vessels, have hulls that curve over to meet the deck, although the "whaleback" designation comes not from the curve along the <a href="/wiki/Gunwale" title="Gunwale">gunwale</a> as in the Great Lakes vessels, but from the fore-and-aft arch in the deck.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  A sheltered portion of the forward deck on certain British fishing boats designed, in part, so that water taken over the bow is more easily shed over the sides. The feature has been incorporated into some pleasure craft – aboard which it is known as a <i>whaleback deck</i> – based on the hull design of older whaling boats.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="whaleboat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Whaleboat" title="Whaleboat">Whaleboat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A type of open boat that is relatively narrow and pointed at both ends, enabling it to move either forwards or backwards equally well.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  On modern warships, a relatively light and seaworthy boat for transport of ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s crew.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.  A type of vessel designed as a <a href="/wiki/Lifeboat_(rescue)" title="Lifeboat (rescue)">lifeboat</a> or "monomoy" used for recreational and competitive rowing in the San Francisco Bay area and coastal Massachusetts.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.  Informally, any <a href="/wiki/Whaler" title="Whaler">whaler</a> of any size.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">5.  Informally, any vessel engaged in <a href="/wiki/Whale_watching" title="Whale watching">whale watching</a>.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="whaler" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Whaler" title="Whaler">Whaler</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.  A specialized vessel designed for catching or processing whales.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.  A person engaged in the catching or processing of whales.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="wharf" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Wharf" title="Wharf">Wharf</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A structure on the shore of a harbor or on the bank of a river or canal where ships may dock to load and unload cargo or passengers. Such a structure includes one or more berths (i.e., mooring locations), and may also include <a href="/wiki/Pier" title="Pier">piers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warehouse" title="Warehouse">warehouses</a>, or other facilities necessary for handling the ships. The term "wharf' is generally synonymous with "quay" (<i>q.v.</i>), although the solid foundations of a quay contrast with the closely spaced piles of a wharf. When "quay" and "wharf" are used as synonyms, the term "quay" is more common in everyday speech in the United Kingdom, many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Commonwealth" title="British Commonwealth">Commonwealth</a> countries, and the Republic of Ireland, while "wharf" is more commonly used in the United States.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="wheel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steering_wheel_(ship)" title="Steering wheel (ship)">Wheel</a> or ship's wheel <span id="Ship.27s_wheel"></span></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The usual steering device on larger vessels: a wheel with a horizontal axis, connected by cables to the rudder.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="wheelhouse" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Wheelhouse</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Location on a ship where the wheel is located; also called pilothouse or bridge.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="whelkie" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Whelkie</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small sailing pram.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="wherry" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Wherry" title="Wherry">Wherry</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of boat traditionally used for carrying cargo or passengers on rivers and canals in England, particularly on the River Thames and the Norfolk and Suffolk Broads.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="whiff" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Whiff</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A chiefly British term for a narrow clinker-built skiff having outriggers, for one oarsman.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="whip" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Whip</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small single block tackle, used to raise light loads from a hold <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184837_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184837-51">[50]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="whip_upon_whip" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Whip upon whip</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Connecting two whips together. This runs more smoothly than using a double block with single block tackle which would have the equivalent purchase. Can be used for topsail and top-gallant halliards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184837_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArt_of_Rigging184837-51">[50]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="whipping" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Whipping</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The binding with twine of the loose end of a rope to prevent it unravelling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017_2-165"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThames_Sailing_Barge_glossary2017-2">[2]</a></sup></dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="whipstaff" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Whipstaff" title="Whipstaff">Whipstaff</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vertical lever connected to a tiller, used for steering on larger ships before the development of the ship's wheel.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="whiskers" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Whiskers</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Spreaders from the bows to spread the bowsprit shrouds.</dd>
<dt class="glossary" id="whiskerstay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Whiskerstay</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">One of the pair of stays that stabilize the bowsprit horizontally affixed to forward end of the bowsprit and just aft the stem.</dd>
